The Five Senses as Sources of Necessary Knowledge
Baqillani enumerates the five senses as sources of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five senses as sources of necessary knowledge, including the distinction between sense perception and the physical organs.

Core Idea
The senses give necessary knowledge, and 'sense' means perception, not the physical organ.

Baqillani begins a new chapter on the sources of knowledge. He answers a key question: from how many ways does necessary knowledge come? He identifies six ways, among them the five senses. But he carefully defines what he means by 'sense' to avoid confusion. This passage lays the foundation for understanding how we acquire certain knowledge through sensory perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the question-answer format and the list of five senses.
The passage is from Baqillani's Tamhid al-Awa'il.
2
Identify the five senses and the key distinction
List the five senses mentioned. Then note Baqillani's clarification: by 'sense' he means perception, not the physical organs like eyes or nose. Why is this distinction important?
This avoids a common misunderstanding.
3
Understand the necessary nature of sensory knowledge
Baqillani states that knowledge from the senses is necessary and compels the soul without any possibility of doubt. Reflect on why sensory knowledge is considered certain in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في مدارك العلوم فإن قال قائل: فَمِنْ كَمْ وجه يقع العلم بالمعلوم إذا كان ضرورة؟ من ستة طرق فمنها الحواس الخمس: وهي حاسة البصر وحاسة السمع وحاسة الذوق وحاسة الشم وحاسة اللمس وقصدنا بذكر الحاسة ها هنا الإدراك الموجود بالحواس لا الأجسام المؤتلفة على الصورة التي ما حصل عليها من الأجسام سميناه عيناً وأنفاً وأُذُن وفماً ويداً. فكل علم حصل عند إدراك حاسة من هذه الحواس فهو علم ضرورةٍ يلزم النفس لزوماً لا يمكن معه الشكُ في المدرَك ولا الارتياب به وكل حاسة من هذه الحواس تختص في وقتنا هذا على عادة جارية بإدراك جنس أو أجناس
Chapter on the sources of knowledge. If a questioner says: From how many ways does knowledge of the known occur when it is necessary? From six ways, among them the five senses: the sense of sight, the sense of hearing, the sense of taste, the sense of smell, and the sense of touch. And our intention in mentioning 'sense' here is the perception that exists through the senses, not the bodies that are composed in the form that we have called eye, nose, ear, mouth, and hand. So every knowledge that occurs upon the perception of a sense from these senses is necessary knowledge that compels the soul in a way that doubt about the perceived object is not possible, nor skepticism. And each of these senses, in our time, according to the usual custom, is specific to perceiving a genus or genera.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on the sources of knowledge (madaarik al-ilm). It begins with a question: from how many ways does knowledge occur when it is necessary? The answer: from six ways. Among them are the five senses: sight, hearing, taste, smell, touch. The author specifies that by 'sense' he means the perception (idrak) found through the senses, not the physical organs (like eye, nose, etc.). So any knowledge obtained through one of these senses is necessary knowledge that compels the soul in a way that no doubt is possible regarding the perceived object. Each sense, at the present time according to the usual course of nature, is specific to perceiving a certain type or types of objects.

Key Terms
حواس
علم ضروري
إدراك
بصر
سمع
ذوق
شم
لمس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'sense' (al-hassa) refers to the physical organs like eyes and ears.
Correction: Baqillani explicitly states that by 'sense' he means the perception (idrak) that occurs through the senses, not the physical bodies we call eye, nose, etc.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani mean by 'al-hassa' (sense) in this passage?
A) The physical organs like eyes and ears
B) The perception itself that occurs through the senses
C) The faculty of reason
D) The objects perceived

Key Takeaway
The five senses provide necessary knowledge, and 'sense' means the perception itself, not the physical organ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you experienced something with your senses that later caused doubt in your heart. Why did doubt creep in? According to Baqillani, sensory knowledge is necessary and cannot be doubted. Where did the doubt actually come from? Was it from the perception itself or from your interpretation? Resolve now to trust your perceptions in matters of faith, but filter them through the Qur'an and Sunnah.
?
You have been given eyes, ears, and other senses. How have you used them today in ways that increase your certainty in Allah? How have you used them in ways that distract you? This lesson reminds us that perception itself gives necessary knowledge. When you hear the Qur'an recited, do you perceive it with the same certainty as your other senses? Make a commitment to listen to the Qur'an with the awareness that this hearing should compel your soul undeniably.
?
Baqillani distinguishes between the physical organ and the perception. Which do you rely on more: your physical abilities or the perception of your heart? In your daily life, are you more focused on the 'eye' or on the act of seeing truth? This lesson calls you to shift your attention from the tools to the perception itself. How can you train yourself to see and hear with the inner senses that lead to certain faith?

The Sixth Type of Necessary Knowledge: Direct Inner Awareness
Baqillani enumerates six examples of the sixth type of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's concept of the sixth type of necessary knowledge that arises directly in the soul without sensory input, including its various examples.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge is not only from the senses; it also springs directly from the soul.

Baqillani completes his classification of necessary knowledge by describing a type that does not depend on the five senses. This knowledge arises directly in the soul through innate awareness, self-reflection, logical necessity, and tawātur reports. In this lesson, we explore the six examples he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage identifying the six examples of the sixth type of necessary knowledge.
The passage has footnote markers; focus on the main enumeration.
2
Identify the first three examples
Note self-knowledge (inner states), speaker's intention, and logical dichotomies.
These are immediate and not based on sensory processing.
3
Identify the next two examples
Recognize knowledge of psychological states from signs and knowledge from tawātur.
Tawātur yields certainty about historical facts.
4
Connect to the broader classification
Understand how this type differs from sensory knowledge and why it is considered necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب السادس منها ضرورة تخترع في النفس ابتداءً من غير أن تكون موجودة ببعض هذه الحواس (۲) كعلم الإنسان بنفسه (٣) وما يجده فيها (٤) من الصحة والسقم واللذة والألم والغم والفرح والقدرة والعجز والإرادة والكراهة والإدراك والعمى (٥) وغير ذلك مما يحدث في النفس (٦) مما يدركه الحي إذا وجد به، ومنه (۷) أيضاً العلمُ الواقع بقصد المتكلم إلى ما يقصد يقصده بخطابه دون غيره وأنه قاصد إلى اكتساب ما يوجد به من ضروب مقدوراته من الكلام وغيره، ومنه أيضاً (1) العلم بأن الأجسام متى موجودة فلا بد من أن تكون مجتمعة متماسّة الأبعاض أو مفترقة متباينة وأن الخبر عن وجود الشيء وأنه على بعض الأوصاف لا بد أن يكون صدقاً أو كذباً وأن الخبرين المتضادّ مُخبَرُهُما لا يجوز أن يكونا جميعاً صدقاً أو كذباً وما كانت ذلك جری مجری من الأمور المنقسمة في العقل إلى أمرين لا واسطة بينهما . ومنه أيضاً العلمُ بخجل الخَجِل وَوَجَل الوَجل والعلم بالشجاعة والجبن والبر والعُقوق والتحية والاستهزاء والواقع عند مشاهدة الامارات، ومنه أيضاً العلم المُخْتَرَع في النفس بما تواتر الخبر عن كونه واستفاض عن وجوده نحو العلم الواقع عند (۱۰) إخبار المخبرين عن الصين وخراسان وفارس (۱۱) وكرمان وعن ظهور موسى وعيسى ومحمد صلى الله عليه (۱۳) وعلى جميع الأنبياء.

(۲) اعلم أن للحواس إدراكات لمعان معقولات غائبات تدرك بالوسائط ومعنى إدراكها للمعقولات بالوسائط أنها تتأمل في أحوال المحسوسات وتقيس بعضها إلى بعض فتتنبه لمناسبات بينهما ومباينات فتدرك فيها معاني كلية وتجزم بنسب بعضها إلى بعض ثم تتوصل بها إلى مكان آخر ثم هكذا إلى أن تستكمل جوهرها حسب جهدها وجهدها وجدها وجدها فهو سبب أيضاً للعلم بذلك لما فيه من خلاف الملاحدة والسمينة في جميع النظريات سواء كان في الإلهيات أو الحسابيات أو الهندسيات نقل عنهم أنهم قالوا لا طريق إلى العلم سوى الحس ولهذا أنكروا إفادة الخبر المتواتر أيضاً. وبعض الفلاسفة في الإلهيات كأرسطو، فإنه قال : لا يمكن تحصيل اليقين في المباحث الإلهية .
And the sixth type of them is necessary [knowledge] that is invented in the soul initially, without being existent by some of these senses (2), like a person's knowledge of himself (3) and what he finds in himself (4) of health and sickness, pleasure and pain, grief and joy, ability and inability, will and aversion, perception and blindness (5), and other things that occur in the soul (6) that the living being perceives when it exists with it. And from it (7) also is knowledge that actually occurs concerning the speaker's intention of what he intends by his speech to the exclusion of other things, and that he intends to acquire the kinds of what he can do from speech and other things. And from it also (1) is knowledge that bodies, when they exist, must be either joined, with parts touching, or separated, distinct; and that a report about the existence of a thing and that it has some attributes must be either true or false; and that two contradictory reports about the same subject cannot both be true or both false; and what is like that from matters that are divided in the mind into two [alternatives] with no middle term. And from it also is knowledge of the shame of the bashful and the fear of the fearful, and knowledge of courage and cowardice, piety and impiety, greeting and mockery, occurring upon observing indications. And from it also is knowledge invented in the soul by the tawātur (massively transmitted) report about something and its widespread existence, like the knowledge that occurs upon (10) the reports of reporters about China, Khurāsān, Fārs (11), and Kirmān, and about the appearance of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad (peace be upon him) (13) and upon all the prophets.

(2) Know that the senses have perceptions of intelligible, absent meanings that are perceived through intermediaries. The meaning of their perception of intelligibles through intermediaries is that they reflect on the states of sensibles and compare some to others, then become aware of correspondences and differences between them, thus perceiving universal meanings in them and judging the relations of some to others, then proceed from them to another place, then continue thus until they complete their essence according to their effort and effort, their striving and striving. So it is also a cause for knowledge of that, because of the opposition of the materialists (mulāḥida) and the Samaniyya in all theoretical matters, whether in theology or mathematics or geometry. It is transmitted from them that they said: 'There is no path to knowledge except the senses,' and therefore they also denied the benefit of tawātur reports. And some philosophers in theology, like Aristotle, said: 'It is not possible to obtain certainty in divine inquiries.'
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the sixth type of necessary knowledge (al-ḍarb al-sādis min al-‘ulūm al-ḍarūriyya). Baqillani classifies this as knowledge that arises in the soul directly, not through the five senses. He gives several examples: (1) self-knowledge and awareness of one's own states (health, sickness, emotions, abilities, etc.); (2) knowledge of the speaker's intention from his speech—i.e., we know when someone speaks that they intend to communicate something specific; (3) knowledge of necessary logical and physical dichotomies, such as that bodies must be either together or separate, reports must be true or false, contradictory reports cannot both be true or false—these are self-evident rational judgments; (4) knowledge of psychological states from outward signs, like shame in a bashful person or fear in a fearful one; (5) knowledge from tawātur (massively transmitted reports), which yields certainty about historical facts like the existence of China or the prophets. The long footnote (2) elaborates on the role of the senses: the senses can perceive intelligible meanings through intermediaries by reflection and comparison, and this process is a cause for knowledge. It then refutes the materialists and the Samaniyya who claimed only sense-perception yields knowledge, denying even tawātur and rational certainty. It also mentions that some philosophers like Aristotle denied certainty in theology. The footnote thus defends the possibility of knowledge beyond the senses, including rational and transmitted knowledge, which is central to the Ash‘ari epistemological project.

Key Terms
ضرب سادس
علم ضروري
تواتر
قولان لا واسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All necessary knowledge comes from the five senses.
Correction: Baqillani shows that there are necessary knowledges that arise directly in the soul without sensory input, such as self-awareness, logical truths, and tawātur.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of Baqillani's examples of the sixth type of necessary knowledge?
A) Knowledge of one's own health
B) Knowledge that contradictory reports cannot both be true
C) Knowledge of the taste of honey
D) Knowledge from tawātur reports

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that necessary knowledge can be immediate and non-sensory, encompassing self-awareness, logical truths, and tawātur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that your knowledge of your own inner states—joy, grief, health, sickness—is immediate and necessary. When you experience grief, do you treat that knowledge as certain? If you do, why do you sometimes doubt Allah's knowledge of that same grief when you pray? Today, bring one real inner state to Allah in full certainty that He knows it directly.
?
Baqillani says that tawātur (massively transmitted reports) gives necessary knowledge of the existence of past prophets. You have received the report of Prophet Muhammad ﷺ through tawātur. Do you truly hold that knowledge as certain and necessary? If your answer is yes, then how must that certainty affect the way you respond to his commands today? Pick one command you have been neglecting and obey it today with full conviction.

Defending Non-Sensory Knowledge: Refuting Materialists and the Samaniyya
Baqillani refutes materialist and skeptical objections to non-sensory knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that only sense-perception yields knowledge and defends the validity of rational and transmitted knowledge.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge comes from the senses; reason and tawātur are valid sources of certainty.

In this footnote, Baqillani addresses objections from materialists (mulāḥida) and the Samaniyya who denied any knowledge beyond sense-perception. He explains how the senses themselves can lead to intelligible knowledge through reflection, and he criticizes those who reject tawātur and rational certainty, including Aristotle's skepticism in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote passage
Read the Arabic passage of the footnote, which explains the role of the senses and refutes opponents.
This passage is a commentary on the main enumeration.
2
Understand the role of the senses
Note Baqillani's explanation that the senses perceive intelligibles through intermediaries by reflection and comparison.
3
Identify the opponents
Recognize the materialists (mulāḥida), the Samaniyya, and Aristotle as those who limit knowledge to the senses or deny certainty in theology.
4
Analyze Baqillani's refutation
Understand how Baqillani argues that the senses themselves can be a cause for non-sensory knowledge and that tawātur provides certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب السادس منها ضرورة تخترع في النفس ابتداءً من غير أن تكون موجودة ببعض هذه الحواس (۲) كعلم الإنسان بنفسه (٣) وما يجده فيها (٤) من الصحة والسقم واللذة والألم والغم والفرح والقدرة والعجز والإرادة والكراهة والإدراك والعمى (٥) وغير ذلك مما يحدث في النفس (٦) مما يدركه الحي إذا وجد به، ومنه (۷) أيضاً العلمُ الواقع بقصد المتكلم إلى ما يقصد يقصده بخطابه دون غيره وأنه قاصد إلى اكتساب ما يوجد به من ضروب مقدوراته من الكلام وغيره، ومنه أيضاً (1) العلم بأن الأجسام متى موجودة فلا بد من أن تكون مجتمعة متماسّة الأبعاض أو مفترقة متباينة وأن الخبر عن وجود الشيء وأنه على بعض الأوصاف لا بد أن يكون صدقاً أو كذباً وأن الخبرين المتضادّ مُخبَرُهُما لا يجوز أن يكونا جميعاً صدقاً أو كذباً وما كانت ذلك جری مجری من الأمور المنقسمة في العقل إلى أمرين لا واسطة بينهما . ومنه أيضاً العلمُ بخجل الخَجِل وَوَجَل الوَجل والعلم بالشجاعة والجبن والبر والعُقوق والتحية والاستهزاء والواقع عند مشاهدة الامارات، ومنه أيضاً العلم المُخْتَرَع في النفس بما تواتر الخبر عن كونه واستفاض عن وجوده نحو العلم الواقع عند (۱۰) إخبار المخبرين عن الصين وخراسان وفارس (۱۱) وكرمان وعن ظهور موسى وعيسى ومحمد صلى الله عليه (۱۳) وعلى جميع الأنبياء.

(۲) اعلم أن للحواس إدراكات لمعان معقولات غائبات تدرك بالوسائط ومعنى إدراكها للمعقولات بالوسائط أنها تتأمل في أحوال المحسوسات وتقيس بعضها إلى بعض فتتنبه لمناسبات بينهما ومباينات فتدرك فيها معاني كلية وتجزم بنسب بعضها إلى بعض ثم تتوصل بها إلى مكان آخر ثم هكذا إلى أن تستكمل جوهرها حسب جهدها وجهدها وجدها وجدها فهو سبب أيضاً للعلم بذلك لما فيه من خلاف الملاحدة والسمينة في جميع النظريات سواء كان في الإلهيات أو الحسابيات أو الهندسيات نقل عنهم أنهم قالوا لا طريق إلى العلم سوى الحس ولهذا أنكروا إفادة الخبر المتواتر أيضاً. وبعض الفلاسفة في الإلهيات كأرسطو، فإنه قال : لا يمكن تحصيل اليقين في المباحث الإلهية .
And the sixth type of them is necessary [knowledge] that is invented in the soul initially, without being existent by some of these senses (2), like a person's knowledge of himself (3) and what he finds in himself (4) of health and sickness, pleasure and pain, grief and joy, ability and inability, will and aversion, perception and blindness (5), and other things that occur in the soul (6) that the living being perceives when it exists with it. And from it (7) also is knowledge that actually occurs concerning the speaker's intention of what he intends by his speech to the exclusion of other things, and that he intends to acquire the kinds of what he can do from speech and other things. And from it also (1) is knowledge that bodies, when they exist, must be either joined, with parts touching, or separated, distinct; and that a report about the existence of a thing and that it has some attributes must be either true or false; and that two contradictory reports about the same subject cannot both be true or both false; and what is like that from matters that are divided in the mind into two [alternatives] with no middle term. And from it also is knowledge of the shame of the bashful and the fear of the fearful, and knowledge of courage and cowardice, piety and impiety, greeting and mockery, occurring upon observing indications. And from it also is knowledge invented in the soul by the tawātur (massively transmitted) report about something and its widespread existence, like the knowledge that occurs upon (10) the reports of reporters about China, Khurāsān, Fārs (11), and Kirmān, and about the appearance of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad (peace be upon him) (13) and upon all the prophets.

(2) Know that the senses have perceptions of intelligible, absent meanings that are perceived through intermediaries. The meaning of their perception of intelligibles through intermediaries is that they reflect on the states of sensibles and compare some to others, then become aware of correspondences and differences between them, thus perceiving universal meanings in them and judging the relations of some to others, then proceed from them to another place, then continue thus until they complete their essence according to their effort and effort, their striving and striving. Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the sixth type of necessary knowledge (al-ḍarb al-sādis min al-‘ulūm al-ḍarūriyya). Baqillani classifies this as knowledge that arises in the soul directly, not through the five senses. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the senses cannot perceive something, it cannot be known.
Correction: Baqillani explains that the senses can lead to intelligible knowledge through reflection, and tawātur provides certainty independent of personal sensory experience.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, which group claimed that 'there is no path to knowledge except the senses'?
A) The Ash‘arites
B) The materialists and Samaniyya
C) The philosophers like Aristotle
D) The Sufis

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the sensory exclusivism of materialists and Samaniyya by showing that the senses, when reflected upon, yield intelligible knowledge, and tawātur provides certainty beyond the senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are confronted with a truth of the unseen (like the realities of the grave or the Day of Judgment), do you find yourself instinctively doubting because you cannot see or touch it? What does that reveal about your heart's reliance on the senses? How can you actively cultivate certainty in what the tawātur of revelation has brought?
?
Aristotle—a brilliant mind—said certainty in divine matters is impossible. Is there a part of you that secretly agrees, treating theological questions as mere speculation? Where in your life have you allowed intellectual pride to justify doubt about Allah's attributes? What concrete step can you take this week to replace that skepticism with the conviction that comes from reflecting on creation and trusting the report of the truthful?
?
The materialists among us are not just long-dead philosophers—they live in our desires that prioritize the visible and tangible over the eternal. Think of a recent decision where you acted as if only what you can see matters. Did you sacrifice a spiritual duty for a worldly gain? How can you use the very process Baqillani describes—reflecting on what you see to arrive at a higher truth—to reorient your heart toward the unseen reality that truly deserves your attention?

Divisions of Known Things: Existent and Non-Existent
Baqillani classifies all known things into existent and non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's classification of all known things into existent and non-existent, including the four types of non-existent and the linguistic basis for equating 'thing' with existence.

Core Idea
All known things are either existent or non-existent — and non-existent has many shades.

Baqillani begins his discussion of epistemology by laying out the fundamental division of everything that can be known. He argues that 'thing' (shay') is synonymous with existence in Arabic usage, and then distinguishes between what exists and what does not, offering a nuanced categorization of non-existents. This passage establishes the conceptual framework for all subsequent theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repetitive structure: Baqillani lists each type using 'منه'.
2
Understand the linguistic argument
Baqillani uses everyday Arabic speech to prove that 'thing' implies existence. Pay attention to the examples of 'taking something', 'hearing something', and 'seeing something'.
3
Identify the two main categories
The passage divides all known things into existent (mawjud) and non-existent (ma'dum). See how Baqillani defines each, especially the non-existent as 'not a thing'.
4
Examine the four types of non-existent
Baqillani enumerates four subtypes of non-existent: the impossible, the possible but never to occur, the future, and the past. Find each one and its example.
The fifth type is incomplete due to a page break.
5
Reflect on theological implications
Ask yourself why this classification matters. How does it help in discussing what God can and cannot do, or what will and will not happen?
This connects to the learning objective.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في أقسام المعلومات
جميع المعلومات على ضربين معدوم وموجود فالموجود هو الشيء الثابت الكائن لأن معنى الشيء عندنا أنه موجود يدل على ذلك قول أهل اللغة: «شيء» إثبات وقولهم: «ليس بشيء» نفي يبين ذلك أن القائل يقول: ما أخذت من زيد شيئاً ولا سمعت منه شيئاً ولا رأيت شيئاً نفي للمذكور، وقولهم: أخذت شيئاً وسمعت شيئاً ورأيت شيئاً إثبات للمذكور ورجوع إلى كائن موجود فوجب أن يكون كل موجود شيئاً وكل شيء موجوداً.
والمعدوم منتفي ليس بشيء فمنه معلوم معدوم لم يوجد قط ولا أن يوجد وهو المحال الممتنع الذي ليس بشيء وهو القول المتناقض نحو اجتماع الضدين وكون الجسم في مكانين وما جرى مجرى ذلك مما لم يوجد قط ولا يوجد أبداً؛ ومنه معدوم لم يوجد قط ولا يوجد أبداً وهو يصح ويمكن أن يوجد نحو ما علم الله أنه لا يكون من مقدوراته وأخبر أنه لا يكون من نحو رد أهل المعاد إلى الدنيا وخلق مثل العالم وأمثال ذلك مما علم وأخبر أنه لا يفعله وإن كان مما يصح فعله له؛ ومعلوم معدوم في وقتنا هذا وسيوجد فيما بعد نحو الحشر والنشر والجزاء والثواب والعقاب وقيام الساعة وأمثال ذلك مما أخبر تعالى أنه سيفعله وعلم أنه سيوجد؛ ومعلوم آخر هو معدوم في وقتنا هذا، وقد كان موجوداً قبل ذلك نحو ما كان وتقضى من أحوالنا وتصرفنا من كلامنا وقيامنا وقعودنا الذي كان في أمس يومنا وتقضى ومضى؛
Chapter on discourse concerning the divisions of known things. All known things are of two types: non-existent (ma'dum) and existent (mawjud). The existent is the thing that is established and subsisting, because the meaning of 'thing' (shay') for us is that it is existent. That is indicated by the saying of the people of language: 'thing' is affirmation, and their saying 'not a thing' is negation. That is clarified by the speaker saying: 'I did not take anything from Zayd, nor did I hear anything from him, nor did I see anything' – negation of what is mentioned; and their saying: 'I took something, heard something, saw something' – affirmation of what is mentioned, returning to something that subsists, existent. So it is necessary that every existent is a thing and every thing is existent. And the non-existent is negated, not a thing. Among it are: a known non-existent that never existed nor will exist – that is the impossible (al-muhal al-mumtani'), which is not a thing, and it is contradictory speech, like the conjunction of two opposites, a body being in two places, and what runs in that course, which never existed nor will ever exist; and among it is a non-existent that never existed nor will ever exist, but it is sound and possible to exist, like what God knows He will not do from among His capabilities and informed that He will not do, such as returning the people of the Hereafter to the world, creating a like of the world, and the like, which He knows and informed that He will not do, even though it is possible for Him to do; and a known non-existent in our time that will exist later, like the gathering, the resurrection, the recompense, reward and punishment, the establishment of the Hour, and the like, which God the Exalted informed He will do and knows will exist; and another known non-existent in our time, which had existed before that, like what has passed and elapsed of our states and our actions: our speech, our standing, our sitting, which were yesterday and have passed and gone;
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a classification of all known things (al-ma'lumat). Baqillani divides them into existent (mawjud) and non-existent (ma'dum). He first defines 'existent' by equating it with 'thing' (shay') based on linguistic usage: when Arabs say 'thing' they affirm existence; when they say 'not a thing' they deny it. Thus every existent is a thing and vice versa. Then he defines non-existent as negated, not a thing. He then provides a detailed breakdown of non-existents into four categories. The first is the impossible (muhal) that neither was nor can be, like contradictions. The second is what is possible but will never occur because God has decreed otherwise, e.g., recreating the world. The third is what is not present now but will occur in the future, like the Resurrection. The fourth is what is not present now but was present in the past, like past actions. A fifth category is hinted at but the text is incomplete.

ممتنع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think 'non-existent' is simply nothing or unreal, with no further distinctions.
Correction: Baqillani shows that non-existence has different types: some are impossible, some are possible but won't happen, some will happen in the future, and some happened in the past.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's classification of non-existents shows that theological discussion about what God can or will do requires precise categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'I can't' or 'That's impossible' about your habits, relationships, or circumstances. Based on Baqillani’s categories, are you confusing logical impossibility with what you simply have not seen God do yet? What would change if you saw your 'impossible' as merely 'possible but not yet willed'?
?
Baqillani says that past actions are 'non-existent now'—your words, your standing, your sitting from yesterday are gone. Are you clinging to regrets or pride over what is now non-existent? How would you live today if you truly believed that only what God preserves of your deeds will remain?
?
When you anticipate resurrection and judgment—are they as real to you as the objects you touch? The lesson says they are 'non-existent now but will exist'. Does that knowledge drive you to action, or do you treat the future as irrelevant fantasy? What will you do today because of what you know will certainly come?

The Impossible Non-Existent
Definition of non-existence and first category: the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of non-existence and the first category: the impossible non-existent that never was and never will be.

Core Idea
The impossible non-existent is a contradiction that can never exist.

Before diving into the five categories, the author first defines non-existence as a negation (not a thing). The first category is the clearest: the impossible non-existent, which includes self-contradictory statements like two opposites coexisting. This passage introduces the fundamental distinction between what can and cannot be.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the full passage below, noting that the first part defines non-existence and then singles out the impossible.
The impossible is completely excluded from existence.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'معدوم', 'محال', 'ممتنع', and 'متناقض'. Notice how they are used to describe the impossible.
These terms are foundational for kalam discussions.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives examples: conjunction of opposites, body in two places. Think about why these are impossible.
The impossibility is logical, not just practical.

Source Text

والمعدوم مُنتَفٍ ليس بشيء فمنه معلوم معدوم لم يوجد قط ولا أن يوجد وهو المحال الممتنع الذي ليس بشيء وهو القول المتناقض نحو اجتماع الضدّين وكون الجسم في مكانين وما جرى مجرى ذلك مما لم يوجد قط ولا يوجد أبداً؛ ومنه معدوم لم يوجد قط ولا يوجد أبداً وهو يصح ويمكن أن يوجد نحو ما علم الله أنه لا يكون من مقدوراته وأخبر أنه لا يكون من نحو رَدّه أهل المعاد إلى الدنيا وخلق مثل العالم وأمثال ذلك مما علم وأخبر أنه لا يفعله وإن كان مما يصح فعله له؛ و معلوم معدوم في وقتنا هذا وسيوجد فيما بعد نحو الحشر والنشر والجزاء والثواب والعقاب وقيام الساعة وأمثال ذلك مما أخبر تعالى أنه سيفعله وعلم أنه سيوجد؛ ومعلوم آخر هو معدوم في وقتنا هذا، وقد كان موجوداً قبل ذلك نحو ما كان وتقضى من أحوالنا وتصرفنا من كلامنا وقيامنا وقعودنا الذي كان في أمس يومنا وتقضى ومضى؛ ومعلوم آخر معدوم هو مقدور ويمكن أن يكون ويمكن أن لا يكون ولا يُدرى هل يكون أم لا يكون نحو ما يقدر الله تعالى عليه مما لا نعلم أيفعله أم لا يفعله، نحو تحريك الساكن من الأجسام وتسكين المتحرك منها، وأمثال ذلك.
And the non-existent is negated; it is not a thing. Among it is a known non-existent which never existed nor will exist, and it is the impossible, which is not a thing, and it is the contradictory statement – such as the conjunction of two opposites, a body being in two places, and what runs in that course, which never existed nor will ever exist. And among it is a non-existent which never existed nor will ever exist, but it is sound and can exist – such as what God knows He will not do from among His capabilities, and He has informed that He will not do – like returning the people of the resurrection to the world, creating a world like this one, and similar examples of what He knows and has informed that He will not do, even though it is sound for Him to do. And a known non-existent in our present time which will exist later – such as the resurrection, gathering, recompense, reward, punishment, the establishment of the Hour, and similar examples of what God has informed He will do and knows that it will come to exist. And another known non-existent in our present time which existed before – such as what has passed and elapsed of our states and actions, our speech, standing, and sitting which were yesterday and have passed and gone. And another known non-existent that is possible (maqdur) and can be and can not be, and it is not known whether it will be or not – such as what God is capable of that we do not know whether He will do it or not, like moving a stationary body or making a moving body stationary, and similar examples.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage classifies non-existent (ma'dum) into five categories. First: the impossible non-existent that never was and never will be, like contradictions. Second: the possible non-existent that never was and never will be but could have been, like God's not doing certain possible acts. Third: the future non-existent that will come to be, like the resurrection. Fourth: the past non-existent that once existed but is now gone, like our past actions. Fifth: the possible non-existent that may or may not come to be, and its occurrence is unknown to us, like certain potential acts of God. This classification shows that non-existence is not a simple concept but has different modalities.

Possible Non-Existent: Past, Future, and Unknown
Four categories of possible non-existent things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four remaining categories of non-existent: the possible but never existing, the future, the past, and the possible unknown.

Core Idea
Possible non-existents differ by whether they can occur, did occur, will occur, or are unknown.

Having defined the impossible, the author now turns to possible non-existents. These are things that could exist in principle but differ in their temporal relation to actuality. Each category illustrates how non-existence is not uniform; it has modalities tied to divine knowledge and action.

Guided Path
1
Read the remainder of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the four categories. Notice the distinct phrases: 'لم يوجد قط ولا يوجد أبداً وهو يصح ويمكن أن يوجد', 'معدوم في وقتنا هذا وسيوجد', 'معدوم في وقتنا هذا وقد كان موجوداً', 'مقدور ويمكن أن يكون ويمكن أن لا يكون'.
Each category has a specific temporal or modal marker.
2
Categorize each type
For each category, identify whether it is possible, when it existed/not, and whether its occurrence is known. Make a mental table.
The second category is possible but never occurs; the third will occur; the fourth occurred; the fifth may or may not.
3
Link to divine attributes
Notice how each category relates to God's knowledge (علم) and power (مقدور). The second and fifth involve what God can do but does not do or unknown.
This shows the connection between metaphysics and theology.

Definition of the Possible (al-Mumkin)
Baqillani defines the possible as what may or may not exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the possible (al-mumkin) as that which may either exist or not exist, and why it is not merely what is unknown.

Core Idea
The possible is ontologically neutral to existence and non-existence.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani introduces one of the three rational judgments: the possible (al-mumkin). He gives a concise definition and illustrates it with examples from divine power. Understanding this concept is foundational for distinguishing what can be attributed to Allah from what cannot.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the compact style and the logical structure of the definition.
The Arabic starts mid-sentence; the antecedent of 'هو' refers to the category of the possible from the previous lesson.
2
Identify the definition of the possible
Look for the three characteristics: it can be, it can not be, and its occurrence is unknown. This is the core definition.
3
Examine the examples
Baqillani gives examples of divine actions that are possible. Consider why moving a stationary body or stilling a moving one are perfect examples of possibility.
These actions are neither necessary nor impossible; they are equally within divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو مقدور ويمكن أن يكون ويمكن أن لا يكون ولا يُدرى هل يكون أم لا يكون نحو ما يقدر الله تعالى عليه مما لا نعلم أيفعله أم لا يفعله، نحو تحريك الساكن من الأجسام وتسكين المتحرك منها، وأمثال ذلك.
It is possible and it can be and it can not be and it is not known whether it will be or not, such as what Allah the Exalted is able to do that we do not know whether He will do it or not do it, like moving the stationary among bodies and making stationary the moving among them, and similar things.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines the possible (al-mumkin) as that which can either exist or not exist, and its actual occurrence is unknown. The author uses the example of divine actions: Allah has power over many things, but we do not know which He will actualize. The examples given are moving what is stationary and stilling what is moving among bodies — actions that are equally within divine power.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The possible is simply what is unknown to us.
Correction: The passage defines the possible as ontologically neutral to existence and non-existence, not merely unknown. The unknownness is a consequence, not the definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage says we do not know whether the possible will occur. Is your heart at peace with that uncertainty, or do you find yourself clinging to control? What would it look like for you to truly accept that Allah alone knows what will be—and to release your grip on that area of your life right now?

Proof of Temporal Nature from Dependence on Accidents
Baqillani proves bodies are temporal because they cannot precede accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's rational proof that bodies are temporal (muhdath) because they cannot precede accidents.

Core Idea
Bodies cannot precede accidents; therefore they are temporal.

This lesson presents a foundational argument from Imam al-Baqillani demonstrating the temporal origination of bodies. The argument hinges on the necessary relation between bodies and accidents: a body always exists in one of two states—its parts either contiguous or separated—both of which are accidents. Since bodies cannot precede the accidents that inhere in them, and accidents are temporally originated, bodies must be temporal as well.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument flow.
Notice that the argument has a clear logical structure: axiom → implication → conclusion.
2
Identify the two states
The passage states that a body must be either with parts contiguous (muttasil) or separated (mutafarriq). There is no third possibility. Recognize that both are accidents.
This is an exhaustive dichotomy based on necessary knowledge.
3
Understand the logical implication
Because the body cannot exist without one of these two states (both accidents), it cannot precede accidents. If accidents are temporal, then the body must also be temporal.
The key is: whatever cannot exist without something temporal is itself temporal.
4
Apply the argument
Consider how this argument refutes the eternity of the physical world. If the world were eternal, it would have to exist without accidents at some point, which is impossible.
This proof is a cornerstone of kalam cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلا الأمرين يُوجب حدوثه . والدليل على أن الجسم لا يجوز أن يسبق الحوادث أنا نعلم باضطرار أنه متى كان موجوداً فلا يخلو أن يكون متماس الأبعاض مجتمعاً أو متبايناً مفترقاً لأنه ليس بين أن تكون أجزاؤه مُتَماسَّة أو متباينة منزلةٌ ثالثة فوجب ألا يصح أن يسبق الحوادث وما لم يسبق الحوادث فواجب كونُه مُحْدَثاً إذ كان لا بد أن يكون إنما وُجِد مع وجودها أو بعدها
And both matters necessitate its temporal origination. The proof that the body may not precede accidents is that we know necessarily that whenever it exists, it cannot be other than either having its parts in contact and united, or separated and divided, because there is no third station between its parts being contiguous or separate. Therefore it is necessary that it cannot precede accidents. And whatever does not precede accidents, it is necessary that it is originated (muhdath), since it must be that it existed either with them or after them.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage provides a proof for the temporal origination (huduth) of bodies. The argument is based on the relationship between bodies and accidents (a'rad). Baqillani states that both matters (the body being either joined or separated) necessitate its temporal nature. The proof begins with an axiom: we know necessarily that whenever a body exists, it either has its parts in contact and united (muttasil) or separated and divided (mutafarriq). There is no third possibility. Since both states are accidents, and the body cannot exist without one of them, the body cannot precede accidents. But accidents are temporal (they come into being and pass away). Therefore, the body must also be temporal, because if it were eternal, it would have to exist before accidents, but that is impossible. Hence the body exists only with the accidents or after them, which implies it is originated. This is a foundational argument in kalam to prove that the material world is not eternal.

Key Terms
جسم (body)
حادث (temporal/originated)
حوادث (accidents)
متماس (contiguous)
متباين (separated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the body's dependence on accidents merely shows its contingency (imkan), not its actual temporal origination (huduth).
Correction: Baqillani argues that the body cannot precede accidents at all. Since accidents are temporally originated, anything that cannot exist without them must itself be originated—not merely contingent but actually brought into being.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why must a body be temporal (muhdath)?
A) Because it is composed of parts
B) Because it cannot exist without accidents, and accidents are temporal
C) Because it is perceptible
D) Because it changes

Key Takeaway
The body's necessary dependence on accidents, which are temporal, proves that the body itself is originated (muhdath) and cannot be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body changes every moment—cells die, thoughts shift, you move from rest to activity. Knowing that your body cannot exist without these changing accidents, are you living as if you control your own existence? What would it mean to truly rely on the One who creates you anew every instant?
?
If every part of the material world, including your own body, is temporally originated, then your attachment to health, wealth, and comfort is attachment to things that did not exist and will not last. Which of these attachments have you allowed to distract you from your purpose? What is one thing you will release today as a result of this truth?
?
Baqillani argues that there is no 'neutral' state for a body—it always has some accident. Your life is never neutral either; every moment you are either moving toward or away from your Creator. What accident of your soul—a thought, intention, or action—are you currently in? Is it bringing you closer to the Originator or further?

Proof from Impossibility of Traversing Infinite Past
Baqillani argues that reaching the present from an infinite past is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that an infinite series of past events is impossible and why the present moment must have a finite past.

Core Idea
If the past were infinite, we could never reach the present moment.

Baqillani continues his argument for the temporal origination of the world with a second proof. He asks how we could possibly traverse an infinite number of past steps to arrive at the present. By examining the logical implications, he concludes that an infinite past is impossible, because we would never come to an end of the sequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the rhetorical question opening the passage.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Identify the premise (present event exists), the reasoning (traversal of infinite series is impossible), and the conclusion (past must have a beginning).
Baqillani uses a conditional: if infinite, then no traversal.
3
Reflect on the key concept: termination of the infinite
Consider why 'termination of what has no end' is impossible. Baqillani argues that from the present, going backwards never reaches a limit, so the series cannot be infinite.
This is the core logical step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأقول أيضاً كيف يمكنني قطع ما لا بداية في جانب الماضي من الخطوات مثلاً للوصول إلى المكان المرام فمعنى ذلك أنه لم يحصل مني وصول لأن ما لا أول له من الحوادث لا تنتهي النوبة إلى وجود الحادث الحاضر في الحال. وانقضاء ما لا نهاية له محال لأنك إذا لاحظت الحادث الحاضر ثم انتقلت إلى ما قبله وهلم جرا على الترتيب لم تفض إلى نهاية . ووجود ما لا نهاية له من الحوادث محال وإن لم يمكن عدم إفضائك إلى نهاية لكان لتلك الحوادث أول .
I also say: How can I traverse what has no beginning in the past, for example steps, to reach the intended place? That would mean that I did not arrive, because for events that have no first, the turn does not end at the existence of the present event at the moment. And the termination of what has no end is impossible, because if you consider the present event and then move to what is before it, and so on in order, you will not reach an end. And the existence of events that have no end is impossible; and if it were not impossible that you do not reach an end, then those events would have a beginning.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof for the temporal origination of the world: the impossibility of traversing an infinite past. Baqillani asks rhetorically: how could one traverse an infinite sequence of past steps to reach the present? If the past were infinite, one could never complete the traversal to arrive at the present moment. The argument is that if there were an infinite number of past events, we could never reach the present event. The termination of an infinite series (inqidā' mā lā nihāyata lahu) is impossible because if you start from the present and go backwards, you never come to an end. Therefore, the existence of an infinite number of past events is impossible. If it were not impossible, then those events would have a beginning. So the past must be finite.

Key Terms
لا بداية (no beginning)
ما لا نهاية له (what has no end)
الحادث الحاضر (the present event)
انقضاء (termination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of traversing an infinite series only applies to the future, not the past.
Correction: Baqillani applies the same reasoning to the past: if the past were infinite, we could never reach the present moment. Thus, the past must also be finite.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani say that if the past were infinite, we could never reach the present?
A) Because the present is the end of the past, and an infinite series has no end
B) Because the past is unchangeable
C) Because time is circular
D) Because God created the world at a specific time

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that the impossibility of traversing an infinite series applies to the past, proving that the world must have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world had a beginning, what does that mean for your own beginning? You were not always here. How does the certainty of your finite existence affect the way you spend your time today?
?
Baqillani argues that you could never reach the present from an infinite past. But you are here now. This moment is proof that the past is finite. Are you living as if this moment—and every moment—matters because it is part of a limited number? Or do you act as if you have infinite time?
?
The proof shows that an infinite series cannot be traversed. Your life, too, is a finite series of moments. When you consider your own end, does the impossibility of an infinite past make you more conscious of the reality of an end—and what lies beyond it?

The Parity Proof for the World's Origination
Baqillani enumerates the parity argument to prove the world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the parity of numbers to prove that the series of past events must be finite and therefore the world is originated.

Core Idea
An infinite past would force a number to be both even and odd—a logical impossibility.

Baqillani presents a crisp rational proof that the world must have a beginning. He argues that if there were an infinite number of past events, their count would have to be either even or odd, leading to contradictions. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the parity argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Baqillani sets up the conditional: if the past had no end, then its number must be even or odd (or both/neither).
The phrase 'لا نهاية له' means 'without end' or 'infinite'.
2
Identify the impossibility
Focus on why the number cannot be only even or only odd: each can become the other by adding or subtracting one, which is impossible for an infinite set to lack.
Try to think: if you have an infinite line of numbers, can it be missing a '1' to become odd or even?
3
Connect to the world's origination
Understand that since the infinite series is impossible, the past must have a beginning. Therefore, the world, which is not free of events (hawadith), must itself be originated.
The argument does not directly prove a Creator, only that the world began.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا دليل عقلي بعبارة أخرى فنقول : لو كان أفراد العالم التي دخلت في الوجود لا نهاية لها لكان لا يخلو عددها عن أن يكون زوجاً أو فرداً معاً أو لا زوجاً ولا فرداً ومحال أن يكون زوجاً وفرداً جميعاً ولا زوجاً ولا فرداً فإن في ذلك جمعاً بين النفي والإثبات وهما ضدان إذ في إثبات أحدهما نفي الآخر وفي نفي أحدهما إثبات الآخر ومحال أن يكون زوجاً فقط لأن الزوج يكون فرداً بزيادة واحد فيكون يعوز ما لا نهاية له واحد ومحال أن يكون فرداً فقط لأن الفرد يكون زوجاً بزيادة واحد عليه فكيف يعوز واحد ما لا نهاية له فحصل من هذا أن العالم لا يخلو من الحوادث فهو إذاً حادث وإلا لزم استحالة وجود الحادث الحاضر لأنه لزم وجود حوادث لا أول لها فلانتفاء وجود حوادث لا أول لها انتفى ملزومه وهو كون ما لا يخلو من الحوادث قديماً، فثبت نقيضه وهو ما لا يخلو من الحوادث حادث فتبين وجود انتهاء الحوادث التي دخلت في الوجود إلى أول. وبهذا دليل الدليل قول بعض الملحدين بتسلسل الوالدية والولدية في جانب الماضي إلى غير نهاية ويقال في البذر والزرع ونحو ذلك مثل ذلك ويقال في إبطال قولهم ما من نطفة إلا من إنسان ولا من إنسان إلا من نطفة وهكذا إلى غير بداية وقولهم ما من زرع إلا من بذر ولا من بذر إلا من زرع وهكذا إلى غير بداية في جانب الماضي يلزم . منه ذلك المحال وما أدى إلى المحال محال . وبهذا الدليل العقلي ينقض قولهم أي الفلاسفة بوجود جواهر عقلية سموها عقولاً ونفوساً ملكية زعموا أنها أزلية .
And we have a rational proof in another expression: We say: If the individuals of the world that came into existence had no end, then their number would not be free from being either even or odd together, or neither even nor odd. It is impossible for it to be both even and odd together, nor neither even nor odd, for that would be combining negation and affirmation, which are opposites, since in affirming one is the negation of the other, and in negating one is the affirmation of the other. And it is impossible for it to be even only, because the even becomes odd by adding one, so what has no end lacks one. And it is impossible for it to be odd only, because the odd becomes even by adding one to it, so how could what has no end lack one? From this it follows that the world is not free of events (hawadith), so it is therefore originated (hadith). Otherwise, the impossibility of the existence of the present event would follow, because there would be events with no beginning. And since the existence of events with no beginning is impossible, its necessary condition (malzum) is negated, namely that that which is not free of events is eternal (qadim). So its opposite is established, namely that that which is not free of events is originated. Thus it becomes clear that the events that came into existence have a beginning. And by this proof the statement of some atheists who affirm an infinite regress of fatherhood and sonship in the past, and similarly the statement about seed and planting, is refuted. And in refuting their statement that 'no sperm except from a man, and no man except from a sperm', and so on without beginning, and their statement that 'no plant except from a seed, and no seed except from a plant', and so on without beginning in the past, the impossible follows. And what leads to the impossible is impossible. And by this rational proof their statement, i.e. the philosophers, about the existence of intellectual substances which they call intellects and angelic souls, which they claim are eternal, is refuted.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage contains a polished rational proof (dali aqli) for the temporal origination of the world using the parity of numbers. Baqillani argues that if there were an infinite number of past events, their count would have to be either even or odd. It cannot be both or neither. But if it were only even, adding one would make it odd, so the infinite would lack one. Similarly, if only odd, adding one would make it even, again the infinite lacks one. Since this is impossible, the series of past events must be finite. Therefore, the world, which cannot be free of events, must be originated. If it were eternal, it would have preceded an infinite series of events, which is impossible. The proof also refutes atheists who propose infinite genealogical regress (e.g., every human from a sperm, every sperm from a human, ad infinitum) and the philosophers who claim that intellects and souls are eternal. All such views lead to the impossible, so they are invalid.

Key Terms
زوج (even)
فرد (odd)
لا نهاية له (infinite)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The parity argument applies to a completed infinite set of past events as a whole.
Correction: Baqillani's argument shows that an actual infinite cannot exist because it leads to a parity contradiction; it does not assume a completed infinite but proves its impossibility.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani eliminate the possibility that the number of past events is only even?
A) Because an even number cannot be odd.
B) Because adding one to an even number makes it odd, so the infinite would lack one.
C) Because the number of past events must be odd.
D) Because even and odd are opposites.

Key Takeaway
The parity argument demonstrates that an infinite series of past events is logically impossible, proving that the world must have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every event in your life is created and had a beginning, why do you sometimes act as if your failures or regrets are timeless? How does knowing that the past is truly finite free you to repent and start anew today?
?
The proof shows the world must have a beginning—you too had a beginning. Are you using your limited days for what matters most in the sight of Allah? What would you start doing today if you truly believed you have no guarantee of tomorrow?
?
Baqillani argues that an infinite series is impossible. Yet we often treat our own desires and distractions as if they will go on forever. What is one thing you are procrastinating on that you would do immediately if you grasped the urgency of your finitude?

Refuting Infinite Regress and Eternal Substances
Baqillani applies the parity proof to refute two specific doctrines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani applies the parity proof to refute the atheist doctrine of infinite genealogical regress and the philosophers' claim of eternal intellectual substances.

Core Idea
What leads to impossibility is itself impossible.

Having established the finitude of the past through the parity argument, Baqillani now turns to refute two opposing views: the atheist belief in an infinite chain of fathers and sons (or seeds and plants) and the philosophers' assertion that intellects and souls are eternal. This lesson shows how his proof demolishes these claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation statements
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the two groups being refuted: atheists (ملحدين) and philosophers (فلاسفة).
2
Understand the atheist claim
The atheists claim an infinite regress of fathers and sons, or seeds and plants. Baqillani asserts that this leads to the same parity contradiction.
This is an example of تسلسل (infinite regress) in the past.
3
Understand the philosophers' claim
Philosophers posit eternal intellectual substances (جواهر عقلية) like intellects and angelic souls. Baqillani argues these would also involve an infinite series, thus impossible.
These substances are claimed to be eternal (أزلية).
4
Connect to the parity principle
Both refutations rely on the principle that any position leading to an impossibility (like an infinite past) is false. This is a standard kalam argument.
Remember: ما أدى إلى المحال محال.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا دليل عقلي بعبارة أخرى فنقول : لو كان أفراد العالم التي دخلت في الوجود لا نهاية لها لكان لا يخلو عددها عن أن يكون زوجاً أو فرداً معاً أو لا زوجاً ولا فرداً ومحال أن يكون زوجاً وفرداً جميعاً ولا زوجاً ولا فرداً فإن في ذلك جمعاً بين النفي والإثبات وهما ضدان إذ في إثبات أحدهما نفي الآخر وفي نفي أحدهما إثبات الآخر ومحال أن يكون زوجاً فقط لأن الزوج يكون فرداً بزيادة واحد فيكون يعوز ما لا نهاية له واحد ومحال أن يكون فرداً فقط لأن الفرد يكون زوجاً بزيادة واحد عليه فكيف يعوز واحد ما لا نهاية له فحصل من هذا أن العالم لا يخلو من الحوادث فهو إذاً حادث وإلا لزم استحالة وجود الحادث الحاضر لأنه لزم وجود حوادث لا أول لها فلانتفاء وجود حوادث لا أول لها انتفى ملزومه وهو كون ما لا يخلو من الحوادث قديماً، فثبت نقيضه وهو ما لا يخلو من الحوادث حادث فتبين وجود انتهاء الحوادث التي دخلت في الوجود إلى أول. وبهذا دليل الدليل قول بعض الملحدين بتسلسل الوالدية والولدية في جانب الماضي إلى غير نهاية ويقال في البذر والزرع ونحو ذلك مثل ذلك ويقال في إبطال قولهم ما من نطفة إلا من إنسان ولا من إنسان إلا من نطفة وهكذا إلى غير بداية وقولهم ما من زرع إلا من بذر ولا من بذر إلا من زرع وهكذا إلى غير بداية في جانب الماضي يلزم . منه ذلك المحال وما أدى إلى المحال محال . وبهذا الدليل العقلي ينقض قولهم أي الفلاسفة بوجود جواهر عقلية سموها عقولاً ونفوساً ملكية زعموا أنها أزلية .
And we have a rational proof in another expression: We say: If the individuals of the world that came into existence had no end, then their number would not be free from being either even or odd together, or neither even nor odd. It is impossible for it to be both even and odd together, nor neither even nor odd, for that would be combining negation and affirmation, which are opposites, since in affirming one is the negation of the other, and in negating one is the affirmation of the other. And it is impossible for it to be even only, because the even becomes odd by adding one, so what has no end lacks one. And it is impossible for it to be odd only, because the odd becomes even by adding one to it, so how could what has no end lack one? From this it follows that the world is not free of events (hawadith), so it is therefore originated (hadith). Otherwise, the impossibility of the existence of the present event would follow, because there would be events with no beginning. And since the existence of events with no beginning is impossible, its necessary condition (malzum) is negated, namely that that which is not free of events is eternal (qadim). So its opposite is established, namely that that which is not free of events is originated. Thus it becomes clear that the events that came into existence have a beginning. And by this proof the statement of some atheists who affirm an infinite regress of fatherhood and sonship in the past, and similarly the statement about seed and planting, is refuted. And in refuting their statement that 'no sperm except from a man, and no man except from a sperm', and so on without beginning, and their statement that 'no plant except from a seed, and no seed except from a plant', and so on without beginning in the past, the impossible follows. And what leads to the impossible is impossible. And by this rational proof their statement, i.e. the philosophers, about the existence of intellectual substances which they call intellects and angelic souls, which they claim are eternal, is refuted.

This passage contains a polished rational proof (dali aqli) for the temporal origination of the world using the parity of numbers. Baqillani argues that if there were an infinite number of past events, their count would have to be either even or odd. It cannot be both or neither. Misconception: Infinite regress in the past is possible if each step has a preceding cause.
Correction: Baqillani argues that any actual infinite series in the past leads to the parity contradiction, so no such series can exist.

Checkpoint
What principle does Baqillani use to refute both the atheist and philosopher claims?
A) The world must have a Creator.
B) Whatever leads to impossibility is itself impossible.
C) The number of past events must be even.
D) Intellectual substances cannot be eternal because they are immaterial.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's parity proof serves as a powerful tool to reject any claim that implies an infinite past, including atheistic regress and eternal substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you rely on an unending cycle—be it a habit, a relationship, or a career path—rather than facing the reality that all things must have a beginning and an end? What would it look like to confront the 'impossible' pattern and seek a foundation in the Eternal?
?
The philosophers invented eternal intellectual substances to explain the universe without God. Is there any belief, philosophy, or worldview you hold that subtly replaces the sole eternity of Allah? What would change if you fully accepted that nothing besides Him is eternal?
?
Baqillani's principle 'what leads to the impossible is impossible' is a logical tool. But spiritually, are there thoughts or desires in your heart that logically lead to separation from Allah, yet you continue to entertain them? Will you treat those patterns as impossible and cut them off today?

Proof of the Contingency of Celestial Bodies
Bāqillānī proves celestial bodies are contingent through their change and movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that celestial bodies are contingent because they undergo observable change, particularly the sun's movement through zodiac signs.

Core Idea
Celestial bodies are not exempt from change; their movement proves they are contingent.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani addresses the claim that celestial bodies might be eternal. He argues that because these bodies undergo change—such as movement, composition, and transition—they must be contingent like all other physical objects. He then provides a concrete example: the sun's passage through the zodiac signs. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from general principle to specific proof.
The passage begins with a rhetorical question about planets and then states the denial based on observed change.
2
Identify the general principle
Find the statement that anything which undergoes limit, end, composition, motion, rest, and transition cannot be eternal. This is the foundation of the argument.
Baqillani uses 'جاز عليها من الحد والنهاية...' to list changes.
3
Analyze the specific proof with the sun
Focus on the sun's movement through constellations. Understand the reasoning: if the sun were eternal by its essence, it could not move; its movement shows it is contingent.
Key phrase: 'لعينها ونفسها' meaning 'by its essence and itself'.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and attempt the activity to solidify the proof.
Make sure you can explain why the sun's movement disproves eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمريخ والمشتري والزهرة وعُطارد؟ قيل له : أنكرنا ذلك لعلمنا بحدوث هذه النجوم وأنها جاريةٌ مجرى سائر أجسام العالم وذلك أنه قد جاز عليها من الحد والنهاية والتأليف والحركة والسكون والانتقال من حال إلى حال ما يجوز على سائر أجسام العالم فلو جاز أن تكون قديمة، مع ما وصفنا لجاز قدمُ سائر الأجسام . والدليل على حدوث هذه الأفلاك علمنا بأن الشمس تكون في برج الحَمَل ثم تنتقل إلى برج الثور ثم إلى غيرها من البروج وقد علمنا أنها لا تجوز أن تكون كائنة في برج الحمل ومتحركة إليه لعينها ونفسها لأن ذلك لو كان كذلك لم تُعلم نفسها إلا وهي كائنة في برج الحمل ولـوجب أن تكون لم تَزَلْ كائنة فيه لِعَيْنها ولا تزال كذلك وأن يستحيل خروجها عنـه وانتقالها منه، إذ كانت كائنة فيه ،لعينها، كما أنها إذا كانت قديمة لعينها وجوهراً لعينها استحال خروجها من القدم والجوهرية وفي علمنا لخروجها
And Mars, Jupiter, Venus, and Mercury? It was said to him: We deny that because we know the contingency of these stars and that they run the course of the rest of the bodies of the world, because there has passed upon them of limit, end, composition, motion, rest, and transition from state to state what passes upon the rest of the bodies of the world. For if it were permissible for them to be eternal, with what we have described, then the eternity of all bodies would be permissible. And the proof for the contingency of these orbs is our knowledge that the sun is in the constellation of Aries, then it moves to Taurus, then to other constellations. And we know that it is not permissible for it to be in Aries and moving to it by its essence and itself, because if that were so, it would not be known of itself except that it is in Aries, and it would be necessary that it had never ceased being in it by its essence, and would never cease to be so, and that its exit from it and departure from it would be impossible, since it is in it by its essence, just as if it were eternal by its essence and a substance by its essence, its exit from eternity and substantiality would be impossible, and in our knowledge of its exit [from them]...
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the contingency of celestial bodies. Baqillani begins by mentioning specific planets (Mars, Jupiter, Venus, Mercury) and then states that we know these stars are contingent because they undergo change: they have limits, composition, motion, rest, and transitions. If they were eternal despite these changes, then all bodies would have to be eternal. He then provides a specific argument using the sun: the sun moves through the zodiac signs. If the sun were eternal by its essence, it would have to remain in one location forever; its movement shows it cannot be eternal. The argument is that identity is tied to essence: if something is eternal by its essence, its essential properties cannot change. Since the sun changes location, it cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
حدوث
نجوم
أفلاك
برج
قدم
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think celestial bodies are exempt from change and therefore eternal.
Correction: Baqillani shows they are subject to observable change like movement, thus contingent.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani prove the contingency of the sun?
A) By its light
B) By its movement through the zodiac signs
C) By its size
D) By its heat

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's argument demonstrates that observable change in celestial bodies, such as the sun's movement, proves they are contingent and not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The sun moves from constellation to constellation, proving it is not eternal by essence. You too experience constant change—your mood, your health, your certainty. If these changes prove you are not self-sufficient, why do you still live as if you are? What would it mean for you today to truly accept your contingency and rely on the Necessary Being?
?
Bāqillānī argues that if something were eternal by essence, it could never change. Look at your own soul: have you changed? Have you sinned, repented, grown, or regressed? Do you see the imprint of contingency on your life? How will you respond to the fact that you are not eternal, that your time is limited, and that only the Creator is unchanging?
?
The proof shows that celestial bodies are not exempt from change—they too move and change. You may feel that your struggles or doubts make you weak, but change itself is a sign of contingency. Does this thought humble you or frighten you? Will you let it drive you to seek the One who never changes, or will you continue to cling to the illusion of your own permanence?

Refuting Claims to Know Eternity
Baqillani refutes three claims to knowledge of eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani systematically refutes three proposed sources of knowledge (scripture, sense, and inference) that could be used to claim the eternity of celestial bodies.

Core Idea
No source of knowledge proves the heavens are eternal.

Baqillani anticipates objections from those who deny the contingency of celestial bodies. He examines the possible ways one might claim to know their eternity—through scripture, direct observation, or logical inference—and shows each fails. This lesson walks through his rebuttals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the flow of objections and responses.
2
Identify the three claims
Notice how Baqillani presents three hypothetical claims: scripture (sam‘), sense observation (ʿiyān), and inference (istidlāl).
Each is introduced with 'in da‘aw...' (if they claim...).
3
Understand each rebuttal
For each claim, see how Baqillani counters it: scripture by counter-scripture, sense by their own lack of witness, inference by lack of valid evidence.
He also offers an alternative inference from earthly changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأفلاك من اجتماع وتفرق ثم قال : يقال لهم بم عرفتم أنه كذلك؟ فإن ادعوا السمع فيه عورضوا بالسمع الذي ورد ممن فيهم حجج الصدق، فهم أحق أن يصدقوا وهم الرسل. وإن ادعوا العيان والحس أكذبهم علمهم بأنفسهم أنهم لا يذكرون قدمهم ولا شهدوا تدبير النجوم والطائع. وإن رجعوا إلى الاستدلال بما عاينوا فليس في شيء مما عاينوا دليل تدبير النجوم ولا قدم الطبائع وتولد العالم من امتزاجها ، بل لو قلب عليهم القول كان أقرب إلى الوجود وأحق في الاستدلال . ثم يقال: إن اضطراب الفلك وتحرك النجوم وتقلبها على أحوال الاجتماع والتفرق يكون بتقلب أحوال الأرضين وما فيها من أنواع الأشجار والبحار والمياه، وجوهر التفرق التي يعلو نجارها، أو هي بجوهرها كالنيران والجواهر الحقيقية وبها ينقلب أمر النجوم وما ذكر فهذا أحق ، إذ هو أقرب إلى العين وأولى أن يكون دليلاً لما غاب عنا، ولا قوة إلا بالله . من كل برج إلى غيره وأن كونها فيه مضادّ لكونها في غيره دليل أنه لا يجوز أن يكون كونها في هذه البروج قديماً لأن ذلك لو كان كذلك لوجب أن تكون في برج الحمل في حال كونها في برج الثور ولم تكن بأن تكون أحد المكانين بأولى من أن تكون في الآخر إذا كان كونها فيهما قديماً لم يَزَلْ موجوداً ولا يزال كذلك موجوداً وإذا لم يَجُز ذلك ثبت حدوث حركات هذه الأفلاك وأكوانها وثبت بذلك حدوثها لأنها عندنا وعندهم لم توجد قط من هذه الأكوان ؛ وما لم يسبق الحوادث فواجب كونه مُحْدَثاً. وقد من قبل قول من زعم أن الحوادث لا أول لها بما يُغني عن رده ؛ فوجب القضاء على حدوث هذه الأجسام
And the orbs from assembly and separation. Then he said: It is said to them: How do you know that it is so? For if they claim scripture regarding it, they are confronted with the scripture that came from those in whom are proofs of truthfulness—they are more deserving of being believed, and they are the messengers. And if they claim direct sight and sense, their knowledge of themselves belies them, for they do not mention their eternal preexistence nor did they witness the management of the stars and natures. And if they return to inference based on what they have witnessed, there is nothing in what they have witnessed that indicates the management of the stars or the eternity of natures and the generation of the world from their mixture. Rather, if the argument were turned against them, it would be closer to existence and more deserving in inference. Then it is said: The agitation of the orb, the movement of the stars, and their variation over states of assembly and separation occur due to the variation of the conditions of the earths and what is in them of types of trees, seas, and waters; and the substance of separation whose carpenter rises, or they are by their substance like fires and real substances, and by them the affair of the stars turns, and what was mentioned—this is more deserving, since it is closer to the eye and more fitting to be a proof for what is hidden from us. There is no power except through God. From every constellation to another, and the fact that its being in one is opposed to its being in another is proof that it is not permissible for its being in these constellations to be eternal, because if that were so, it would be necessary for it to be in Aries while being in Taurus, and it would not be that being in one of the two places is more fitting than being in the other, if its being in both is eternal, never ceasing to exist, and never ceasing to exist thus. And when that is not permissible, the contingency of the movements of these orbs and their states is established, and by that their contingency is established, because for us and for them, they never existed apart from these states. And whatever does not precede temporally originated things is necessarily originated. And we have previously refuted the claim that temporally originated things have no beginning in a way that suffices from repeating it. So it is necessary to judge that these bodies are contingent.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The opponent might ask how we know the celestial bodies are contingent. Baqillani considers three possible sources: scripture (sam'), sense observation (ʿiyān), and inference (istidlāl). If they appeal to scripture, he counters that the scriptures of the truthful messengers (the prophets) are more authoritative. If they appeal to sense observation, he notes that they themselves have not witnessed the eternal past or the management of the stars. If they appeal to inference from observed things, he argues that what they observe does not prove the eternity of natures or the generation of the world from mixture. He then presents an alternative inference: the changes in the earth and its elements (trees, seas, waters) are more immediate and better evidence for what is unseen. Then he gives a logical argument: the sun's being in one constellation contradicts its being in another; if its being in both were eternal, it would have to be in two places at once, which is impossible. Therefore, its movements are contingent, and since it never exists without movement, it is contingent. He concludes that these bodies must be contingent.

Key Terms
سمع
عيان
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is wrong with claiming that sense observation (ʿiyān) proves the eternity of celestial bodies?
A) Sense observation is not reliable
B) Those making the claim have not themselves witnessed the eternal past
C) Sense observation only works for earthly things
D) Sense observation contradicts scripture

Key Takeaway
Scripture, sense, and inference all fail to prove the eternity of celestial bodies, and a better inference points to their contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your beliefs about the unseen—like the existence of God or the afterlife—which of these three sources (scripture, sense, inference) do you actually rely on? Are you honest enough to admit if one of them is weak in your own reasoning? How will you strengthen it today?
?
Think of a time you confidently claimed something about the world without verifying your source. Was it based on someone else's words, your own observation, or a shaky inference? In light of this lesson, how might that claim have been undone? What will you do differently next time?
?
Baqillani shows that turning the argument around can be more compelling. Is there a belief you hold that you have never questioned because it feels obvious? Could the opposite be closer to the truth? Commit to examining one such belief this week using the criteria of this lesson.
