The Logical Proof from the Sun's Movement
Baqillani presents a logical argument from the sun's movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's logical argument that the movement of the sun from constellation to constellation demonstrates the contingency of celestial bodies.

Core Idea
If the sun's positions were eternal, it would have to be in two places at once.

Having refuted external sources of knowledge, Baqillani now presents a direct logical argument. He observes that the sun's being in one constellation contradicts its being in another. If its positions were eternal, it would have to be simultaneously in two incompatible states—an impossibility. This proves the sun's movements are contingent, and since the celestial bodies never exist without movement, they themselves must be contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the key premise
The key premise is that the sun's being in one constellation (e.g., Aries) is incompatible with being in another (e.g., Taurus) at the same time.
This is an obvious impossibility.
3
Follow the reductio
If the sun's positions were eternal (unchanging), it would have to be in all constellations at once, which is impossible. Therefore, its positions cannot be eternal; they must be temporally originated.
4
Apply to the bodies themselves
Since the celestial bodies never exist without such movements, they themselves are contingent.
Baqillani also references a prior refutation of the possibility of an infinite past of events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?
If the sun itself—a massive, life-giving celestial body—is completely contingent and dependent on its Creator for its every movement, how much more dependent are you? Are you living today as if you are self-sufficient, or do you acknowledge your absolute need for Allah every second? What one habit can you change today to remind yourself of this dependence?
?
Baqillani's logical proof shows that it is impossible for the sun to be eternal. Consider something you treat as 'permanent' in your life—your health, your job, a relationship. How would your actions and priorities shift if you truly believed that this thing could be taken from you at any moment? Write down one concrete step you will take today to prioritize your relationship with Allah over that temporary thing.
?
The argument forces you to accept that even the most stable and constant objects in the universe are temporally originated. When you look at the sky tonight, will you remember that every star is a sign of its Creator's power and existence—or will you take it for granted? Commit to a specific action (like a du'a or reflection) the next time you see the sun or stars.

Objecting to Inductive Reasoning: The Dualist's Fallacy
Author challenges dualist's inductive argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the classical mutakallimun rejected the dualist's inductive argument that all bodies must be light or darkness based on observation alone.

Core Idea
Induction from observed cases cannot prove a universal claim about unseen matters.

The dualists argued that all bodies are composed of either light or darkness because they never observed a body free from both. The mutakallim responds by challenging the validity of moving from the observed to the unseen. Read this passage to see how he exposes their reliance on mere assertion rather than proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhetorical questions come through.
Notice that the passage consists of two questions and a conclusion.
2
Identify the two challenges
The author asks two pointed questions. Identify what each challenges about the dualist's reasoning.
3
Understand the logical flaw
The dualist's argument moves from limited observation to a universal claim. The author says this leap is unjustified.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes the dualist has only 'mere assertion' (دعوى). Think about what this means for the strength of their argument.
5
Connect to kalam methodology
Consider why mutakallimun require demonstrative proof, not just induction, for claims about unseen matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك؟ فيقال لهم: لم قلتم إن سائر الأجسام لا تنفك من لأنكم لم تجدوا خلافه؟ ولم زعمتم أن القضاء على غائب الأمور وما نأى العالم عنا بمجرد الشاهد والوجود ثابت صحيح؛ وأن الشيء على مثله وكل ما انفصل عنه؟ فلا يجدون في ذلك سوى الدعوى.
That? Then it is said to them: Why did you say that all bodies are not free from [light and darkness] because you have not found otherwise? And why did you claim that judgment about absent matters and what is remote from the world based solely on the witnessed and existent is established and correct, and that the thing is similar to it and everything that is separate from it? So they find nothing in that except mere assertion.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an objection against the dualist argument that all bodies must be composed of either light or darkness because no exception has been observed. The author (presumably the mutakallim) questions the dualist: 'Why do you say that all bodies are not free from light and darkness merely because you have not found any contrary? And why do you claim that judging unseen matters and distant realms solely on the basis of observed and present evidence is valid, and that the unseen thing must be like the observed?' The author then concludes that the dualist has no real evidence—only mere claim (سوی الدعوى). This challenges the inductive leap from the seen to the unseen.

Key Terms
غائب الأمور
شاهد
وجود
دعوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is always observed in a certain way, it must be that way everywhere.
Correction: Observation cannot guarantee universality, especially for unseen matters. The mutakallimun require a different method of proof.

Checkpoint
What does the author accuse the dualist of relying on in this passage?
A) A valid syllogism
B) Scriptural evidence
C) Mere assertion
D) Empirical analogy

Key Takeaway
The author argues that the dualist's inductive argument is not proof but mere assertion because it unjustifiably extends observed patterns to unseen matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one belief you hold about Allah or the unseen that you have accepted simply because you have never been presented with an alternative? Will you examine that belief with the same critical eye the author applies to the dualist?
?
The dualist claimed certainty based on limited observation. Have you ever felt certain about a religious matter based only on your personal experience or lack of contrary evidence? What would it mean for your faith if that certainty turned out to be mere assertion?
?
Consider a time when you dismissed a claim about the unseen because it didn't match your own observations. Does this lesson challenge you to reconsider the limits of human induction? How will you seek genuine proof (from revelation and sound reasoning) instead of relying on your own assumptions?

The Dilemma of Motion and Rest
Baqillani presents a dilemma challenging dualist inference from motion to nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the dualist's own principle of inference from motion to nature to create a dilemma that undermines their argument for two distinct principles.

Core Idea
Motion and rest both indicate nature; if motion proves difference, rest proves similarity.

Imam Baqillani now presents a clever dilemma that turns the dualist's own logic against them. He asks: why deny that rest can be just as revealing as motion? If you claim that the different motions of light and darkness particles prove they are different in nature, then you must accept that when their wholes come to rest at their centers, that proves they are similar. This puts the dualist in an impossible position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two rhetorical questions that structure the argument.
The passage begins with 'ثم يقال لهم' indicating a new objection.
2
Identify the two parts
First, Baqillani challenges the assumption that all bodies move naturally by pointing to stationary bodies like air. Second, he presents the core dilemma: if motion differences prove different natures, then rest should prove similarity.
3
Understand the dilemma
The dualist faces a choice: accept that rest indicates similarity (thereby abandoning their claim of two distinct natures) or reject it (which contradicts their own inference from motion). Either way, their argument fails.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on consistency
Consider why Baqillani's argument is powerful: it shows that the dualist's principle—'motion differences indicate nature differences'—cannot be applied selectively. If it holds for particles, it must hold for wholes as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: ما أنكرتم أن يكون في أجسام العالم ما طبعه الوقوف كالهواء وما جرى مجراه فيكون لا منحدراً ولا متصاعداً؟ وما أنكرتم إن دل اختلاف حركة جزئيات النور والظلام في هذا العالم لطلب المركز والشوق إلى كليهما على اختلاف جنسيهما، أن يدل وقوف كلية الظلام والنور في عالمها وموضع مركزهما على تماثلهما وتجانسهما واتفاق طباعهما؟ فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا قولهم؛ وإن أبوه نقضوا استدلالهم على اختلاف جنس النور والظلام وطباعهما باختلاف حركات جزئياتهما
Then it is said to them: Why do you deny that among the bodies of the world there is that whose nature is standing still like air and what runs its course, so that it is neither descending nor ascending? And why do you deny that if the difference in motion of the particles of light and darkness in this world, seeking the center and yearning for their wholes, indicates a difference in their kinds, then the standing still of the whole of darkness and light in their world and the location of their center would indicate their similarity, homogeneity, and agreement in nature? For if they pass over that, they abandon their statement; and if they refuse, they invalidate their inference about the difference in kind and nature of light and darkness based on the difference in motions of their particles.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a second objection that presents a dilemma. The author questions the dualist: 'Why do you deny that there could be bodies whose nature is to stay motionless, like air, neither descending nor ascending? And why do you deny that if the differing motions of particles of light and darkness (seeking their center) prove they are different in kind, then the fact that their wholes come to rest at their respective centers would prove they are similar and homogeneous?' The choice is stark: if the dualist accepts the second point, they abandon their claim; if they reject it, they contradict their own reasoning that motion differences prove difference in kind.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
مركز
كلية
جزئيات
تماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists may think that only motion indicates nature, while rest is neutral or uninformative.
Correction: Baqillani shows that rest is equally indicative of nature, because both motion and rest are effects of a body's inherent nature. If motion differences prove difference, then rest (when wholes cease moving at their centers) should prove similarity.

Checkpoint
What does the author say would follow if motion differences indicate different natures?
A) That rest would indicate similarity
B) That light and darkness are the same
C) That air is also made of light and darkness
D) That the dualists are correct

Key Takeaway
The dualist's reasoning from motion alone is self-defeating because rest, if consistently considered, would prove the opposite—that light and darkness share a single nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you cherry-picked evidence to support a belief while ignoring evidence that would contradict it? How might Baqillani's dilemma expose a similar inconsistency in your own reasoning about God or creation?
?
The dualists assumed motion proved difference but refused to apply the same reasoning to rest. What assumptions in your life are you reluctant to test because the implications are uncomfortable? Are you willing to follow the logic wherever it leads, even if it threatens a cherished idea?
?
If you claim to follow truth, are you consistent in applying the same standard of proof to arguments you oppose as to those you accept? Ask yourself: would you concede your own position if it faced a dilemma as stark as this? If not, why not?

Refuting Naturalists Through Induction
Author applies same induction argument against naturalists, forcing dualism and dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a parallel induction argument to refute the naturalist claim of four principles, forcing them into a dilemma that invalidates their method.

Core Idea
Induction from the sensible world cannot limit principles to four.

Having previously refuted the dualists, the author now turns to the naturalists. He employs the same logical weapon—induction from observed phenomena—to corner them. If they follow their own method consistently, they are forced to accept an infinite variety of principles, which contradicts their position. This lesson reveals the power of the author's dialectical strategy: turning an opponent's own argument against them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the targets
The passage names two groups: the naturalists (أصحاب الطبائع) and the dualists (الثنوية). Note how the author links them.
The naturalists argue using the witnessed (الشاهد) and the existent (الوجود).
2
Follow the logical move
The author says the naturalists are 'forced into the statement of the dualists'. Why? Because their own inductive method, when applied to the variety in the world, leads to at least two principles, not four.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the dilemma
Then both groups together are presented with a choice: accept that bodies have many kinds (infinite) or reject the inductive inference. Either way, their original position collapses.
The list of attributes (motions, rest, etc.) illustrates the endless variety.
4
Understand the conclusion
If they accept the many kinds, they abandon their limited set of principles. If they refuse, they invalidate their own argument from induction. No escape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يعارض أصحاب الطبائع، إذا استدلوا بالشاهد والوجود؛ فيلزمون قول الثنوية، ثم يؤخذ الفريقان بأن تكون الأجسام من أجناس كثيرة وما لا غاية له من نحو الحركات والسكون والروائح والطعوم والألوان واللين والخشونة والحياة والموت وغير ذلك مما لا تنفك منه أجسام العالم؛ فإن ركبوه تركوا قولهم؛ وإن أبوه نقضوا استدلالهم ولم يجدوا من المعارضة فصلا
And likewise the naturalists are countered: when they argue by the witnessed and existent, they are forced into the statement of the dualists. Then both groups are taken [to the conclusion] that bodies are of many kinds and what has no limit—such as motions, rest, odors, tastes, colors, softness, roughness, life, death, and other things from which the bodies of the world are not free. For if they accept that, they abandon their statement; and if they refuse, they invalidate their inference and find no way out of the counterargument.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage applies a parallel refutation to the naturalists. Just as the dualist argued from observed bodies to two principles, the naturalist argues from observed effects to four natures. The author counters by using the same inductive method to argue for an infinite variety of principles (motions, rest, odors, etc.). If the naturalist accepts this, they abandon their limited four principles; if they reject it, they undermine their own inductive inference. The argument shows that induction alone cannot restrict the number of principles without arbitrariness.

Key Terms
أصحاب الطبائع
شاهد
وجود
أجناس كثيرة
الحركات والسكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Induction from the sensible world can definitively determine a small set of fundamental principles (like four natures).
Correction: The author shows that induction leads either to an infinite number of principles or to arbitrary limitation, thus it cannot fix a definite number.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim the naturalists are forced into if they follow their own method?
A) Dualism
B) Monism
C) Atomism
D) Agreeing with the dualists

Key Takeaway
Induction from the world's variety cannot limit principles to a finite number; it forces either dualism or an infinite regress, destroying the naturalist's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The naturalists faced an uncomfortable dilemma: accept the logical consequences of their method or abandon it. Are there beliefs you hold that you have not fully reasoned through because the conclusion might be uncomfortable? What would it cost you to follow the argument wherever it leads?
?
If induction from the world cannot give you certainty about the ultimate nature of reality, what does that imply about your need for divine guidance? Are you willing to seek knowledge beyond your senses, even if it requires humility and submission?

The Substitution Argument and Its Refutation
Baqillani forces a choice: either substance substitutes for hypostases or not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the concept of substitution to force a logical dichotomy, closing off the Christian attempt to evade both identity and distinction.

Core Idea
Evasion is impossible: substitution reveals necessary identity or distinction.

Baqillani now faces a new Christian tactic: refusing to say the substance is either identical to or distinct from the hypostases. In response, he introduces the concept of substitution — does the substance take the place of the hypostases? This logical move closes the door on evasion and shows that the relationship must be one of the two.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice how concise Baqillani's response is.
2
Identify the Christian objection
The Christians say they do not affirm identity or distinction. Locate that statement in the passage.
3
Analyze Baqillani's dichotomy
Baqillani presents two possibilities: either the substance substitutes for the hypostases (identity) or it does not (distinction). Understand why these exhaust the options.
Substitution here means fulfilling the same logical role.
4
Recognize the refutation of evasion
Baqillani concludes that there is no point in evasion (مراوغة) because the very idea of substitution already captures agreement or difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا : لا نقول إنه موافق لها ولا مخالف لها، قيل لهم : فلا بد أن يسد مسدها فيوافقها أو لا يسد مسدها فيخالفها وهذا المعنى بنفسه هو الذي نعنيه بالاتفاق والاختلاف فلا معنى للمراوغة
And if they say: 'We do not say it is identical to them nor distinct from them,' it is said to them: 'Then it must either substitute for them, in which case it is identical to them, or not substitute for them, in which case it is distinct from them. This meaning in itself is what we mean by agreement and difference. So there is no point in evasion.'
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection from the Christians and Baqillani's rebuttal. The Christian objector attempts to avoid the earlier trap by denying both identity and distinction outright. Baqillani counters with a logical dichotomy: the substance either serves as a substitute for the hypostases (i.e., it can take their place in predication and function) or it does not. If it does substitute, it is identical; if it does not, it is distinct. There is no middle ground. Thus, the attempt to evade by refusing both terms is meaningless because the practical relationship of substitution reveals the underlying identity or difference.

Key Terms
موافقة
مخالفة
يسد مسد
مراوغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The relationship between the substance and the hypostases can be neither identical nor distinct, but something else entirely.
Correction: Baqillani shows that substitution forces either identity or distinction; there is no third option. The claim of neither is an evasion that collapses under logical scrutiny.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the inevitable consequence if the substance does not substitute for the hypostases?
A) It is identical to them.
B) It is distinct from them.
C) It is neither identical nor distinct.
D) The analogy is correct.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani forces the Christian to admit that the substance either substitutes for the hypostases (identity) or not (distinction), leaving no room for evasion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a vague position in your own beliefs—'I'm not sure if I agree or disagree'—just to avoid the discomfort of clarity? Where in your life does intellectual evasion cost you truth?
?
When you face a clear logical choice about Allah's attributes or your own obligations, do you honestly accept the conclusion, or do you try to escape behind 'it's a mystery'? What would it cost you to submit to the clear evidence?
?
Baqillani says evasion is pointless. Are there areas of your faith where you are still evading, hoping no one will force you to choose? What would it look like to abandon that evasion today?

Refuting Analogies of Hand, One, and Verse
Baqillani refutes analogies from ordinary language showing substance is not a collective term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects Christian analogies from ordinary language to the Trinity by distinguishing collective terms from specific terms.

Core Idea
Part-whole language does not apply to the Trinity because 'substance' is not a collective term.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a third Christian objection that uses everyday examples like the hand being part of a human, or one being part of ten, to argue that the hypostases could be part of the substance. Baqillani carefully dissects the logic, showing that these examples work because the terms refer to wholes, but 'substance' in Christian doctrine does not refer to a collective whole. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Christian objection
Read the objection presented at the beginning. Notice the analogies: hand/human, one/ten, verse/poem, verse/sura. Ask yourself: what are they trying to prove?
These analogies are meant to show that parts can be predicated of wholes without identity or difference.
2
Identify the key distinction
Baqillani states that 'human' applies to the whole including the hand. In such part-whole relationships, we do not say the part is identical to or different from the whole. Understand why this matters.
The terms used are collective names for wholes.
3
See the difference for 'substance'
Now Baqillani argues that 'substance' is not a collective term for the hypostases. In Christian doctrine, substance is a single entity and the hypostases are properties or individuals, not parts. This is the crux of the refutation.
If 'substance' were a collective term, the analogy might hold, but it is not.
4
Conclude with the failure of the analogy
Since the analogy fails on the linguistic level, the Christian objection collapses. Reflect on how Baqillani uses precise language analysis to defend Islamic doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا : أليس لا يقال : إن يد الإنسان من الإنسان مخالفة له ولا موافقة له، وكذلك الواحد من العشرة والبيت من القصيدة، والآية من السورة، فما أنكرتم من مثل ذلك في الجوهر والأقانيم؟ قيل لهم : إنما لم يجز إطلاق الخلاف والوفاق فيما ذكرتم؛ لأن قولنا (إنسان) واقع على الجملة التي منها اليد وكذلك العشرة والواحد منها والبيت من القصيدة والآية من السورة ومن المحال أن يكون الشيء مثل نفسه أو غيره أو خلاف نفسه وقولنا جوهر ليس بواقع عندكم على الجوهر والأقانيم التي هي خواصه ولا من أسماء الجمل؛ فسقط ما سألتم عنه.
And if they say: 'Is it not said that the hand of a human is neither distinct from the human nor identical to him, and likewise the one from ten, the verse from the poem, and the verse from the sura? So why do you deny the like of that concerning the substance and the hypostases?' It is said to them: 'It is not permissible to apply the terms difference and agreement to what you mentioned; because our saying "human" applies to the whole which includes the hand, and likewise the ten and the one from it, the verse from the poem, and the verse from the sura. And it is impossible for a thing to be like itself or other than itself or different from itself. Our saying "substance" does not, in your view, apply to the substance and the hypostases which are its properties, nor is it among the names of wholes. Therefore, what you asked about falls away.'
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a third Christian objection and Baqillani's answer. The Christians argue that in ordinary language, parts can be predicated of wholes without identity or difference: the hand is not identical to the human, but not distinct either; similarly one is not identical to ten, nor distinct. So why not apply the same to substance and hypostases? Baqillani responds by distinguishing between collective terms and specific terms. 'Human' is a collective term that applies to the whole including the hand; it does not assert identity or difference between the hand and the whole—they are simply part and whole. However, in Christian doctrine, 'substance' is not a collective term that includes the hypostases; rather, it is a separate entity that has properties (hypostases). The hypostases are not parts of the substance but distinct individuals. Thus, the analogy fails because the linguistic relationship in the examples is one of part-whole, not of substance-property. The Christian doctrine posits three persons in one substance, which is not analogous to a whole and its parts.

Key Terms
يد
إنسان
واحد
عشرة
جملة
أسماء الجمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Baqillani is denying the part-whole relationship.
Correction: He is actually showing that the part-whole relationship does not apply to the Trinity because 'substance' is not a collective term.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani reject the analogies of hand, one, and verse?
A) Because such analogies are blasphemous.
B) Because those terms apply to wholes, but 'substance' does not apply collectively to hypostases.
C) Because the hand is not a part of the human.
D) Because the Christians misquoted the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes Christian analogies by showing that the terms used are collective names for wholes, whereas 'substance' in Christian theology is not a collective term for the hypostases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a weak analogy to defend a belief because it sounded persuasive, without checking if the comparison truly holds? How does Baqillani's careful dismantling of the Christian analogy challenge you to examine your own reasoning habits? What will you do today to refine your understanding of tawheed so that you never rely on flawed arguments?
?
This lesson shows that language about Allah must be precise and not borrowed from created things. Are there any descriptions of Allah you use routinely that might be vague or borrowed from human experience without rigorous thought? What is the most uncomfortable question you would have to answer if you took every word about Allah as seriously as Baqillani did?
?
Baqillani defends the oneness of Allah using logic and language analysis. Have you ever felt that defending Islamic beliefs is merely an academic exercise? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that every stray thought about Allah's nature matters eternally? How can you cultivate the same urgency in preserving correct belief as Baqillani shows here?

First Argument: Rational vs Non-rational Revelation
The Barāhima propose a dichotomy for prophetic revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Barāhima's dichotomy argument against prophetic revelation and recognize why it rests on an unsupported assumption.

Core Idea
If reason already knows it, no prophet needed; if reason can't accept it, no prophet wanted.

The Barāhima, a group that denied prophethood, put forward several arguments. Their first is a simple but powerful dichotomy: whatever a prophet brings must be either rational or non-rational. On the surface, the logic seems tight, but Muslim theologians identified a crucial gap. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Notice how the Barāhima set up their either/or choice.
The argument is presented as a single continuous statement.
2
Identify the two cases
Find where the passage says 'if rational' and 'if not rational'. Write down the conclusion for each case.
The structure is: dichotomy → first horn → second horn.
3
Spot the hidden assumption
Ask: Does the argument assume that whatever is rational can be known by human reason alone? Is that always true? Think of a complex mathematical proof or a historical fact.
This assumption is the weak point that later scholars will challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن قال: إن الذي يأتي به الرسول لم يخل من أحد أمرين: إما أن يكون معقولاً وإما أن لا يكون معقولاً. فإن كان معقولاً فقد كفانا العقل التام بإدراكه والوصول إليه، فأي حاجة لنا إلى الرسول؟ وإن لم يكن معقولاً فلا يكون مقبولاً؛ إذ قبول ما ليس بمعقول خروج عن حد الإنسانية، ودخول في حريم البهيمية.
And among them is that he said: That which the messenger brings is not free from one of two matters: either it is rational or it is not rational. If it is rational, then perfect reason has sufficed us in comprehending it and attaining it, so what need do we have for the messenger? And if it is not rational, then it is not acceptable; for accepting what is not rational is a departure from the bounds of humanity and an entry into the realm of bestiality.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of the Barāhima against the necessity of prophets. It is a dichotomy argument. The Barāhima argue that whatever a prophet brings must be either rational (ma'qūl) or non-rational (ghayr ma'qūl). If it is rational, then human reason is sufficient to understand it, making a prophet unnecessary. If it is non-rational, then it cannot be accepted because accepting irrational things is beneath human dignity and akin to animal behavior. Thus, in either case, there is no need for a messenger or acceptance of his message.

Key Terms
معقول (rational)
غير معقول (non-rational)
عقل (reason)
بهيمية (bestiality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that rational things are always known by human reason alone, so prophets are unnecessary.
Correction: The argument fails to consider rational matters that are not known by unaided reason, such as details of worship or historical reports, which require a messenger.

Checkpoint
According to the Barāhima's argument, if a prophet's message is rational, what follows?
A) It must be accepted
B) Reason alone suffices, so no prophet is needed
C) It is beyond human comprehension
D) The prophet must be an angel

Key Takeaway
The Barāhima's dichotomy is a false one: it ignores that some rational truths require prophetic guidance to be known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely on your reason to figure out matters of faith, or do you accept that some truths require revelation? Think of a specific belief you held by reason alone—how does the Barāhima's argument challenge your reliance on that reasoning?
?
This argument claims that accepting non-rational things is bestial. Are there any religious rulings you have dismissed as 'not reasonable'? What would it mean for you to humble your reason before the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
The Barāhima saw no need for prophets if reason suffices. In what areas of your life do you act as if your own reasoning is sufficient, ignoring the guidance of prophetic teachings? What is one area where you will consciously submit to revelation even if it seems irrational to you?

Barāhima's Argument: Reason Sufficient for Gratitude
Barāhima argue that reason alone suffices for knowing God and gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Barāhima's argument that reason alone is sufficient for knowing God and fulfilling the duty of gratitude, and why they conclude that prophets are unnecessary.

Core Idea
Reason alone can know God and thank Him—no prophet needed.

The Barāhima present a second argument against the necessity of prophethood. They claim that reason proves God is wise, and a wise being would only obligate what reason can discern. Since reason already shows us the Creator and our duty of gratitude, following a human prophet becomes unnecessary, and any prophet contradicting reason is a liar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical progression from premises to conclusion.
The argument is compact—each sentence builds on the previous.
2
Identify the premises
Find the key premises: reason proves God is wise, the wise only obligates by reason, reason shows the Creator and His blessings, gratitude is required.
List these as stepping stones.
3
Follow the conclusion
See how the premises lead to reward/punishment based on reason, and then to the rejection of prophets.
The conclusion is: so why follow a human? If a prophet contradicts reason, that proves him a liar.
4
Reflect on the assumption
Ask yourself: does reason really tell us everything about how to thank God? This argument assumes reason is fully self-sufficient.
This connects to the misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن قال: قد دل العقل على أن الله تعالى حكيم، والحكيم لا يتعبد الخلق إلا بما تدل عليه عقولهم. وقد دلت الدلائل العقلية على أن للعالم صانعاً قادراً حكيماً وأنه أنعم على عباده نعماً توجب الشكر، فننظر في آيات خلقه بعقولنا ونشكره بآلائه علينا. وإذا عرفناه وشكرنا له استوجبنا ثوابه. وإذا أنكرناه وكفرنا به استوجبنا عقابه فما بالنا نتبع بشراً مثلنا؟ فإنه إن كان يأمرنا بما يخالف ذلك كان قوله دليلاً ظاهراً على كذبه.
And among them is that he said: Reason has indicated that God Most High is wise, and the wise does not obligate the creation except by what their minds indicate. And rational proofs have indicated that the world has a powerful, wise Maker, and that He has bestowed upon His servants blessings that necessitate gratitude. So we consider the signs of His creation with our minds and thank Him for His favors upon us. If we know Him and thank Him, we deserve His reward. If we deny Him and disbelieve in Him, we deserve His punishment. So why do we follow a human like us? For if he commands us with that which contradicts that, his statement is a clear proof of his lie.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument: reason is sufficient for knowing God and fulfilling our duty of gratitude. The argument begins by asserting that reason proves God is wise, and a wise being would only obligate actions that reason itself endorses. Reason also proves the existence of a powerful, wise Creator who grants blessings that require thankfulness. By using reason to observe creation and thank God, we earn reward; disbelief earns punishment. Therefore, there is no need to follow a human prophet, and if a prophet commands something contrary to reason, it proves he is lying.

Key Terms
عقل
حكيم
شكر
ثواب
عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that reason can fully determine all specific acts of worship and gratitude without any revelation.
Correction: The argument assumes reason suffices for gratitude, but it does not address how reason knows the particular forms of gratitude (e.g., prayer, fasting). Reason may know gratitude is required but not its details.

Checkpoint
According to the Barāhima's second argument, what does reason prove about God?
A) God is merciful and forgiving
B) God is wise, powerful, and bestows blessings
C) God is unknowable
D) God does not exist

Key Takeaway
The Barāhima argue that if reason suffices for knowing God and gratitude, then prophethood is redundant and any prophet contradicting reason is proven false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you examine your gratitude to God, do you find that your rational acknowledgment has actually translated into specific acts of thankfulness? Or have you used this argument to justify neglecting the details of worship that only revelation prescribes?
?
The Barāhima say following a human like us is unnecessary. But in your heart, do you truly believe that your reason has ever led you to the same level of humility and repentance as following the Prophet ﷺ has? Or does your reason actually rebel against the very idea of a prophet commanding you?

Moral Judgments Depend on Revelation, Not Reason
Baqillani asserts that moral judgments are established by revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's Ash'ari position that moral categories are determined solely by divine revelation, and his response to the objection that this would allow God to thank an idle person.

Core Idea
Revelation alone determines moral value; reason cannot judge.

Imam al-Baqillani makes a foundational point for Ash'ari ethics: the intellect by itself cannot declare an action good or evil, obligatory or forbidden. Only divine revelation can assign these categories. In this passage, he states this principle, addresses a powerful objection, and shows that the objection collapses because it would attribute lying to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and its English translation to get the overall argument. Notice the strong opening prohibition.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify Baqillani's main claim
Locate where he says judgments are not established by reason but only by revelation. Understand that this is his central thesis.
3
Follow the objection and reply
The opponent raises a scenario: if revelation alone decides, then God could thank an idle person. Baqillani answers by showing that would be lying, which is impossible for God.
Note how he connects lying to divine attributes.
4
Reflect on the methodological implication
Consider why Baqillani insists on revelation over reason. This shapes the entire discussion of divine justice (ta'dil wa tajwir) in later chapters.
This is a key Ash'ari principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : نحن نمنعكم أشد المنع من أن يكون في العقل بمجرده طريق لقبح فعل أو لحسنة أو حَظْرِه أو إباحته أو إيجابه؛ ونقول : إن هذه الأحكام بأسرها لا تثبت للأفعال إلا بالشرع دون قضية العقل ؛ وسنتكلم على هذا الباب وما يتصل به في باب التعديل والتجوير من كتابنا هذا إن شاء الله فإن قالوا : لو حَسُنَ من الله ما قلتم لحَسُنَ من الله أن يُشكر ويُثنى على مَنْ لم يعمل شيئاً أو مَنْ قَلَّ فِعْلُ البِرِّ منه بأكثر مما يَشْكُرُ ويُثْنِي على العامل الزاهد المجتهد، قيل لهم : لم قلتم ذلك؟ ثم يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم أن يكون الفرق بينهما أن الشكر والثناء على المرء بما لم يكن منه كَذِبٌ ؛ والكَذِب مستحيل على الله تعالى إذ كان الصدق من صفات نفسه، ولغير ذلك كما يستحيل عليه الجهل والعجز؟ والتفضُلُ على من لم يعمل أو على من عمل أقل من عمل غيره بأكثر من التفضل
And it is said to them: We forbid you with the strongest prohibition that there is in the intellect by itself any way to the ugliness of an act or to its goodness or its prohibition or its permission or its obligation. And we say: Verily these judgments entirely are not established for actions except by revelation (al-shar') without the verdict of reason. And we will speak on this chapter and what pertains to it in the chapter on declaring just and unjust (al-ta'dil wa al-tajwir) from this book of ours, God willing. For if they say: If what you said were permissible from God, then it would be permissible from God to thank and praise one who did nothing, or one whose amount of piety was little, more than He thanks and praises the zealous worshipper, it is said to them: Why did you say that? Then it is said to them: What prevented you from [acknowledging] that the difference between them is that thanking and praising a person for what he did not do is lying; and lying is impossible for God the Exalted since truthfulness is from the attributes of His self, and for other reasons just as ignorance and incapacity are impossible for Him? And preferring one who did nothing or who did less than others with more favor...
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
In this passage, Baqillani makes a crucial methodological statement: he asserts that the intellect alone cannot determine the moral value of actions (goodness, badness, obligation, prohibition, permission). Such judgments are only established by divine revelation (shar'). He promises to discuss this more fully in a later chapter on 'declaring just and unjust' (al-ta'dil wa al-tajwir). Then he anticipates an objection: if we say God can prefer whomever He wills, then He could also thank and praise a person who did nothing more than a hardworking worshipper, which seems absurd. Baqillani replies by pointing out the difference: thanking someone for what they did not do is lying, and lying is impossible for God. Thus the objection fails. The passage shows Baqillani's Ash'ari stance that ethics are grounded in revelation.

God's Favoritism and the Limits of Reason in Morality
Baqillani refutes the objection that God's favoritism is foolish and asserts reason alone cannot judge good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's refutation of the objection that God's favoritism implies foolishness and his assertion that reason alone cannot determine moral rulings.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot declare an action good or evil—only revelation can.

In this passage, Baqillani defends God's absolute freedom to favor some over others by challenging the objectors' assumption that such favoritism is foolish. He then makes a deeper point: human reason is not the source of moral values—they come from divine revelation. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objectors' claim that favoring one equal over another is foolish in human experience, and that this must apply to God as well.
The objection is based on analogy from human to divine.
3
Examine Baqillani's counterexample
Notice how he responds by giving an example where favoritism is not foolish (e.g., giving more to a loyal servant). This shows that the objectors' premise is flawed.
He uses a scenario from human wisdom to refute the objection.
4
Understand the epistemological claim
Read the part where Baqillani denies that reason alone can judge good or evil, prohibition, permissibility, or obligation. He asserts that only revelation determines these rulings.
This is the key theological point: morality is revealed, not rational.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument defends God's absolute freedom and establishes revelation as the sole source of moral knowledge.
Baqillani will expand on this in the chapter on justice and injustice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يختص بفضله مَنْ يشاء مِنْ خَلْقِه ، وله التسوية بين سائرهم ؛ فإن ذلك أجمع عَدْلٌ منه وصواب من تدبيره؟ . فإن قالوا : لأن تفضيل أحد المتجانسين على الآخر في الشاهد سَفَة منـا فوجب القضاء بذلك على القديم تعالى ، قيل لهم : ولم قلتم : إن ذلك سفه ، وما أنكرتم من أنه جائز لنا وصواب في حكمتنا أن نَحْبُو بعض عبيدنا وأصدقائنا والمتصرفين معنا كتصرف غيره بأكثر مما نحبو به غيره ونفضّله بعطاء وتشريف لا يستحقه أكثر مما نحبو به غيره، فلم قلتم إن هذا سفه وقبيح من فعلنا؟ ويقال لهم : نحن نمنعكم أشد المنع من أن يكون في العقل بمجرده طريق لقبح فعل أو لحسنة أو حَظْرِه أو إباحته أو إيجابه؛ ونقول : إن هذه الأحكام بأسرها لا تثبت للأفعال إلا بالشرع دون قضية العقل ؛ وسنتكلم على هذا الباب وما يتصل به في باب التعديل والتجوير من كتابنا هذا إن شاء الله
That He singles out with His favor whom He wills from His creation, and He has the right to equalize between all of them; for all of that is justice from Him and correctness of His governance. If they say: 'Because favoring one of two similar individuals over the other in human experience is foolish from us, so it must be judged accordingly for the Eternal, Exalted.' It is said to them: 'Why did you say that is foolish? And what prevents you from recognizing that it is permissible for us and correct in our wisdom that we bestow upon some of our servants and friends and those who act with us as others act, with more than we bestow upon another and we favor him with a gift and honor he does not deserve more than we bestow upon another? So why did you say this is foolish and ugly from our action?' And it is said to them: 'We firmly deny that in reason alone there is a way to the ugliness of an act, or its goodness, or its prohibition, or its permissibility, or its obligation. And we say: These rulings entirely are not established for actions except by revelation, not by the judgment of reason. And we will discuss this topic and what relates to it in the chapter on just and unjust from this book of ours, God willing.'
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The objectors claim that since favoring one equal over another is foolish among humans, it must be foolish for God. Baqillani first counters by giving examples where such favoritism is not foolish (e.g., giving more to a loyal servant). He then makes a stronger epistemological claim: reason alone cannot determine the moral status of actions—good, evil, prohibited, permitted, or obligatory. These values come only from divine revelation (shar'). He promises to discuss this further in the chapter on justice and injustice. Thus, the objection's premise that favoritism is inherently foolish is denied because reason is not the arbiter of morality.

Correction: He denies that reason alone can determine moral rulings without revelation; reason still has a role in understanding revelation and its implications.

Reason Does Not Determine Prohibition and Permission
Baqillani argues against reason determining prohibition and permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Imam Baqillani, prohibition and permission are determined solely by divine revelation, not by reason.

Core Idea
Reason cannot declare anything prohibited or permitted; only God's speech can.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani presents a direct challenge to those who claim that reason can independently determine moral categories. He argues that prohibition (ḥaẓr) and permission (ibāḥa) are not inherent in rational judgment but are rather established by the clear statement (al-qawl al-mubīn) of the Owner of all entities (Mālik al-aʿyān). This lesson examines this argument and its implications for the role of reason in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the rhetorical structure: a question posed to opponents.
2
Identify the key terms
Understand the terms ḥaẓr (prohibition), ibāḥa (permission), ʿaql (reason), al-qawl al-mubīn (the clear statement), and Mālik al-aʿyān (Owner of entities). These are central to the argument.
These terms are foundational in Ashʿarī discussions of moral epistemology.
3
Analyze the argument
Baqillani challenges the assumption that reason can determine prohibition and permission. He asserts that these categories come only from divine speech. Ask: Why does he use a rhetorical question? How does this refute the opponent's position?
This argument is part of a broader refutation of rationalist ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: ما أنكرتم أن يكون جميع ما ادعيتم حظره في العقل غير محظور فيه ولا مباح أيضاً وأن الحظر والإباحة إنما هما ورود القول المبين مالك الأعيان بإباحة ما أباحه وحظر ما حظره، فلم قلتم إن في العقل إباحة وحظراً؟
Then it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that everything you claim to be prohibited by reason is neither prohibited nor permitted in it, and that prohibition and permission are only [determined by] the coming of the clear statement from the Owner of entities, permitting what He permits and prohibiting what He prohibits? So why do you say that there is permission and prohibition in reason?
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This is a second argument in Baqillani's refutation. He directly challenges the opponents' assumption that reason itself can declare things prohibited or permitted. Instead, he asserts that prohibition (حظر) and permission (إباحة) are solely derived from divine revelation—the clear statement (القول المبين) from the Owner of all entities (مالك الأعيان). Reason, on its own, has no authority to label actions as prohibited or permitted. The rhetorical question 'why do you say that there is permission and prohibition in reason?' is a powerful rebuttal, forcing the opponents to justify their foundational premise.

Key Terms

إباحة
عقل
القول المبين
مالك الأعيان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that reason alone can determine moral categories (prohibition and permission) independent of revelation.
Correction: Baqillani teaches that prohibition and permission come only from divine speech, not from reason. Reason has no inherent authority to declare something prohibited or permitted.

Key Takeaway

?

The Rationally Prohibited Is Conditional, Not Absolute
Baqillani answers the objection that the rationally prohibited is always prohibited.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that rational prohibitions are conditional on factors such as need and permission, and are not absolute or immutable.

Core Idea
Rational judgments change with circumstances; prohibition is not forever.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange in which Imam al-Baqillani refutes the claim that whatever reason prohibits is prohibited forever. He uses concrete examples to show that actions can be prohibited, permissible, or even obligatory depending on need and permission. As you read, follow how he turns the opponent's own logic against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The opponent asserts that rational prohibition is absolute and unchanging. Note the phrasing: 'محظوراً أبداً' (prohibited forever). Reflect on whether this matches everyday experience.
Baqillani begins by questioning the basis of this claim.
2
Identify the condition Baqillani introduces
Baqillani shifts the discussion to the condition of the owner's permission. The prohibition was never unconditional; it was tied to the absence of permission. Under that condition, it indeed never changes, but the condition itself can change.
The key term here is 'شرط' (condition).
3
Work through the everyday examples
Baqillani lists examples: eating when full, warming by fire with fever, cooling with snow in extreme cold. These are prohibited when there is no need but become necessary when need arises. See if you can think of similar examples.
The examples show that rational judgment recognizes context.
4
Follow the dilemma and conclusion
Baqillani forces the opponents into a dilemma: either they deny obvious needs (abandoning reason and religion) or they admit that rational judgments can change. The latter undermines their original claim.
The conclusion is that rational prohibitions are conditional, not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : المحظور في العقل محظوراً أبداً وكيف تَصَرَّفَتْ به الحال، قيل لهم : لم قلتم ذلك؟ ثم يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم من أن ذلك كان محظوراً بشريطة عدَم إِذْنِ مَالِكِهِ فيه وإطلاقه وحظره في العقل بهـذا الشرط لا ينقلب أبداً؟ . ثم يقال لهم : أليس الأكل والشرب والاصطلاء بالنار والتبرُّدُ بالثلج قبيحاً مع الشبع والرّيّ التامين اللذين يُخاف الضرر فيما يُتَناوَلُ بعدهما وكذلك الاصطلاء بالنار مع الحُمَّى والتبَرِّدُ بالثلج مع شدة البرد محظور مع الغنى عنه ؟ فإذا قالوا : أجل ، ولا بدّ لهم من ذلك ، قيل لهم : فيجب أن يكون ذلك أجمع محظوراً مع حصـول الحاجة إليه وشدة لهب الجوع والظمأ والحر والقُرّ وخوف الضرر بتركه ؛ فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا دينهم، وإن أبوه وأباحوا هذه الأمور وأوجبوها أيضاً عند الحاجة إليها، قيل لهم : فقد صار المحظور في العقل مباحاً وانقلبت قضايا العقول وهذا ما تكرهون .
If they say: The prohibited in reason is prohibited forever no matter how the situation changes, it is said to them: Why do you say that? Then it is said to them: What prevents you from [acknowledging] that that was prohibited on the condition of lack of permission of its owner and his release, and its prohibition in reason with this condition never changes? Then it is said to them: Is not eating and drinking and warming by fire and cooling with snow ugly when fully satiated and replete, such that harm is feared from what is consumed after them? Similarly, warming by fire with fever and cooling with snow with intense cold are prohibited when free of need for them? If they say: Yes, and they must [say] that, it is said to them: Then it must be that all that is prohibited when there is need for it and the intense flame of hunger, thirst, heat, cold, and fear of harm from leaving it? For if they pass over that, they abandon their religion, and if they refuse and permit these things and even obligate them when there is need for them, it is said to them: Then the prohibited in reason has become permissible and the judgments of reason have changed, and this is what you dislike.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument about the nature of rational prohibition. The author, Imam al-Baqillani, presents a dialectical exchange. First, an opponent claims that whatever is prohibited by reason is absolutely prohibited and never changes regardless of circumstances. Baqillani responds by questioning the basis of this claim. He then proposes that the prohibition in question was conditional from the start: it depended on the owner not granting permission and release. Under that condition, the prohibition never changes, but the condition may cease. To illustrate, he gives examples: eating and drinking are harmful when one is already full, but become necessary when one is hungry and thirsty; warming by fire is harmful with fever but needed in cold; cooling with snow is harmful in extreme cold but needed in heat. If the opponents concede that these actions become prohibited when there is no need and permissible or obligatory when there is need, then they must accept that rational judgments can change. If they stubbornly insist that the actions are always prohibited even in dire need, they contradict their own religion. If they instead permit them when needed, then they admit that the rationally prohibited can become permissible, which overturns their original claim. Thus, Baqillani concludes that the opponents' position is self-refuting: they must either abandon their religion or accept that rational judgments are conditional.

Key Terms
المحظور (al-mahzur: the prohibited)
المباح (al-mubah: the permissible)
العقل (al-aql: reason)
الإذن (al-idhn: permission)
المالك (al-malik: owner)
الحاجة (al-haja: need)
الشرط (al-shart: condition)
انقلاب (inqilab: change)
قضايا العقول (qadaya al-uqul: rational judgments)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational judgments are absolutely immutable and cannot change regardless of circumstances.
Correction: Baqillani shows that rational prohibitions are conditional on factors like need and permission; they can change to permissibility or obligation when conditions shift.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's argument, what is the condition that makes an action rationally prohibited?
A) The action is always prohibited regardless of circumstances.
B) The action is prohibited when there is no need and no permission from the owner.
C) The action is prohibited only if it causes harm.
D) The action is prohibited if it is disliked by the majority.

Key Takeaway
Rational prohibitions are not absolute; they depend on conditions such as need and permission, and can change to permissibility or obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you identify a moral rule you've always treated as absolute, without considering the condition of need? When has that rigidity led you to neglect someone's genuine need? What will you do differently the next time you face such a situation?
?
The passage argues that the rationally prohibited can become obligatory when need is dire. Think of a time when you avoided doing something you thought was wrong, but it turned out to be required because of someone's need. How did you feel? How will you respond if a similar situation arises again?
?
Baqillani forces the opponent to admit that denying the conditional nature of rational prohibitions would lead to abandoning religion. Consider an area where you've held a strict position that, if applied consistently, would contradict your faith (e.g., insisting on a rule that harms others). What would it cost you to change that position? Are you willing to pay that cost?

Applying Conditional Rational Judgment to Animal Pain and Duress
Baqillani applies the conditional principle to animal harm and apostasy under threat of death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani extends the conditional rational judgment to new cases, including causing pain to animals and apostasy under duress, and how necessity can alter the rational ruling.

Core Idea
Rational rulings shift with necessity, even in matters of life, faith, and animal welfare.

Baqillani now puts his principle to the test with harder cases. If actions are rationally prohibited or permitted based on need, what happens when we face threats to life or the suffering of animals? This passage uses a dialectical method to show that the same conditional logic applies, and the opponents cannot escape its implications.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the dialectical structure
This passage is an argument with opponents. Baqillani presents their possible responses and counters them. Notice how he anticipates and dismantles objections.
The structure is: claim, opponent objection, rebuttal, further nuance.
2
Understand the animal pain case
Baqillani states that the same conditional principle applies to causing pain to animals: it is prohibited when needless but permissible out of necessity. This is an important extension.
3
Analyze the duress scenario
A person is threatened with death unless he blasphemes. Work through the dialectic: opponents say blasphemy is obligatory (makes prohibited permissible), or death is required (makes self-destruction permissible). Baqillani uses their own logic against them.
4
Reflect on the final rebuttal
Baqillani suggests that uttering blasphemy with inner faith intact may be preferable to self-destruction, as God knows the heart and life is valuable. The opponents are left with no answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا : كل شيء مما سألتم عنه مباح بشرط الحاجة إليه ومحظور بشرط الغنى فيه وخوف الضرر بتناوله وفعله قيل لهم مثل ذلك في إيلام الحيوان . وكذلك يُسألون عمّن هدّده الملحدون بالقتل إن لم يُلْحِدْ بربه ويشتمه ويُسيء الثناء عليه وخاف نزول القتل به إن لم يَفْعَلْ كَلِمَة الكفر وشتم ربّ العالمين ورجاء البقاء والحياة إن فعله ما الذي يجب عليه؟ فإن قالوا : يجب عليه فِعْلُ شَتْم ربّ العالمين وسُوءِ الثناء عليه، يقال لهم : فقـد صـار المحظور في العقل مباحاً وكذلك إن قالوا يلزمه ألا يَكْفُر وإن أدى ذلك إلى تلف نفسه، قيل لهم : فقد صار قتل نفسه وإلقاؤها في التَّهلُكَةِ مباحاً بعد أن كان محظوراً وهذا ما كرهتم المصير إليه . ويقال لهم، إن قالوا : فعل كلمة الكفر أولى : فما أنكرتم أن يكون الكف مع القتل أولى لأنه يكف عن شتم ربه وليس هو القاتل لنفسه؟ فإن قالوا : فالكفّ عمـا قلتم أولى ، قيل لهم : ما أنكرتم أن يكون إظهار كلمة الكفر أولى إذا لم يَشْرَحْ بالكفر صدراً لحفظ نفسه وعِلْمِهِ بأن الله سبحانه عالم باعتقاده وأنه مُخلِص في وحدانيتـه وأنـه لا سبحانه بإظهار ما يظهره وأنه هو يَستَضِرُّ بترك إظهاره ويُطَرِّقُ إلى قتل نفسه وتَعَدّي الحق في إتلاف مِلْكِ ربِّه وفعل المحظور عليه فعله ؛ ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك. فإن هم قالوا إن إلقاء النفس في التهلكة : محظور في العقل إذا لم يُؤدِّ إلى الكفر بصانعها وجحد نعمه وإن أدى إلى ذلك كان مباحاً ؛ أو قالوا : إن الكفر بالصانع محظور في العقل إذا لم يؤد إلى تلف النفس فإن أدى إليه كان مباحاً من غير أن ينقلب المباح في العقل محظوراً ، قيل لهم : وكذلك إتلاف الحيوان وإيلامه
And if they say: Everything you asked about is permissible on condition of need for it and prohibited on condition of being free of it and fear of harm from consuming and doing it, it is said to them: The same applies to causing pain to animals. Similarly, they are asked about the one whom the atheists threaten with killing if he does not become an atheist regarding his Lord and insult Him and speak ill of Him, and he fears being killed if he does not utter the word of disbelief and insult the Lord of the worlds, and hopes for survival and life if he does it: what is obligatory upon him? If they say: It is obligatory upon him to insult the Lord of the worlds and speak ill of Him, it is said to them: Then the prohibited in reason has become permissible. And likewise, if they say: It is incumbent upon him not to disbelieve even if that leads to his own destruction, it is said to them: Then his killing himself and casting himself into destruction has become permissible after it was prohibited, and this is what you disliked to arrive at. And it is said to them, if they say: Uttering the word of disbelief is preferable: Then what prevents you from [saying] that forbearance with killing is preferable because it refrains from insulting his Lord and he is not the killer of himself? If they say: Forbearance from what you said is preferable, it is said to them: What prevents you from [saying] that uttering the word of disbelief is preferable if he does not feel contentment with disbelief in his heart, in order to save his life, and because of his knowledge that God, exalted, knows his belief and that he is sincere in His oneness, and that He — exalted — is not harmed by what he manifests, and that he himself would be harmed by not manifesting it and would expose himself to killing himself and transgressing the right in destroying the property of his Lord and doing what is prohibited for him to do? And they have no answer for that. For if they say: Casting oneself into destruction is prohibited in reason if it does not lead to disbelief in its Maker and denial of His blessings, but if it leads to that it is permissible; or they say: Disbelief in the Maker is prohibited in reason if it does not lead to destruction of the self, but if it leads to that it is permissible, without the permissible in reason becoming prohibited, it is said to them: And likewise, destroying animals and causing them pain [is subject to the same condition].
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical argument, applying the same principle of conditional rational judgment to two new cases: causing pain to animals and apostasy under duress. First, Baqillani anticipates an opponent who concedes that actions like eating and drinking are permissible with need and prohibited without need, but might hesitate to apply the same logic to causing pain to animals. Baqillani asserts that the same rule applies. He then introduces a more challenging scenario: a person threatened with death by atheists unless he blasphemes and insults God. The question is: what is obligatory? If the opponents say blasphemy is obligatory to save one's life, Baqillani points out that this makes the rationally prohibited (blasphemy) become permissible, contradicting their earlier stance. If they say he must not blaspheme even at the cost of his life, then allowing oneself to be killed becomes permissible, which also overturns a former prohibition. Baqillani then presents a further subtlety: if they claim that uttering blasphemy is preferable, he counters that perhaps forbearance leading to death is even better because it avoids insulting God and the person is not directly killing himself. If they then say forbearance is preferable, Baqillani argues that uttering the word of disbelief could be better if the person does not internally accept disbelief, knowing that God knows his true faith, and that his life is valuable while God is not harmed by outward utterances. By not speaking, he would cause his own death, which is prohibited and transgresses against God's property (his life). The opponents are left with no answer. Finally, Baqillani considers their possible responses: they might say self-destruction is prohibited unless it avoids disbelief, or disbelief is prohibited unless it avoids self-destruction. Either way, Baqillani concludes that the same conditional logic applies, and the same principle holds for causing pain to animals: it is prohibited without need but permissible out of necessity.

الملحدون
كلمة الكفر
شتم
التَّهلُكَة
إلقاء النفس
الغنى
الحاجة
العقل
محظور
مباح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think rational judgments are absolute and cannot change under any circumstances.
Correction: This lesson shows that necessity can alter the ruling: what is prohibited can become permissible when needed, and vice versa, applied consistently across cases including animal pain and life-threatening duress.

Checkpoint
In the scenario of the atheist threat, what does Baqillani argue happens if the opponents say that blasphemy is obligatory to save one's life?
A) That proves that the rationally prohibited is always prohibited.
B) That shows that the rationally prohibited has become permissible, which contradicts their position.
C) That blasphemy is still prohibited despite the threat.
D) That the person must accept death.

Key Takeaway
The conditional principle of rational judgment applies consistently to all actions, including causing pain to animals and life-or-death choices under duress, showing that necessity can change the ruling while preserving logical coherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were threatened with death unless you publicly denied God, would you do it? Would your answer change if you knew your heart remained faithful? Do you truly believe that God knows your inner state and that your life is worth preserving?
?
Have you ever justified a forbidden action because of necessity? How do you ensure that your heart does not become content with the sin, even if the outward action becomes permissible?
?
The passage argues that God is not harmed by outward blasphemy, but you are harmed by death. Do you believe your life has value to God, or do you sometimes act as if your life is worthless in your devotion? What would you be willing to die for, and is that based on this lesson's teaching?

Reason Cannot Establish Obligation
Baqillani refutes the sufficiency of reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that reason cannot impose obligation, prohibition, or permission, and that revelation is necessary for such judgments.

Core Idea
Reason guides, but only revelation commands.

This lesson addresses the claim that human reason is sufficient, making prophets unnecessary. Baqillani defends the necessity of revelation by arguing that reason alone cannot establish moral-legal rulings. He insists that obligation, prohibition, and permission come only from divine communication (sam‘).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the structure: opponent's claim then refutation.
The passage is one continuous argument; read carefully.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponents argue that God perfected reason, making it sufficient to know all needs, so prophets are unnecessary. Find this claim in the passage.
3
Understand Baqillani's refutation
Baqillani denies that reason can obligate, prohibit, or permit. Only revelation can do that. He concludes that prophets are necessary.
Note the phrase 'لا سبيل من ناحية العقل إلى إيجاب شيء ولا إلى حظره ولا إلى إباحته'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا : الدليل على منع إرسال الرسل والغنى عنهم أن الله سبحانه أكمل العقول وحسن فيها الحسن وقبح فيها القبيح وجعلها دلالة على مراشد الخلق ومصالحهم ومنع بها من التظالم وجعلها دلالة وذريعة إلى علم كل ما يُحتاج إليه . وليس يجوز أن يأتي الرسل بغير ما وضع في العقل فدل ذلك على الغنى عنهم وعدم حاجة الخلق إليهم . فيقال لهم : ما أنكرتم أنه لا سبيل من ناحية العقل إلى إيجاب شيء ولا إلى حظره ولا إلى إباحته وأن ذلك لا يَثْبُتُ في أحكام الأشياء إلا من جهة السمع وأن التعريض للثواب لا يقع بالأفعال الواقعة مع فقد السمع لأنها لا تكون مع طاعة الله سبحانه ولا قُربةً إليه ولا يُثاب صاحبها وإذا كان ذلك كذلك فلا بدّ من سمع يأتي على لسان رسول بفعل قرَّرَ السمعُ وُجوبه فعلم أن العلم بالقُرَب وحصول الثواب عليها لا يجوز أن يثبت عقلاً فقد بطل قولكم جميع ما يحتاج إليه العباد من المراشد والمصالح مُدْرَك العقول فدُلّوا على صحة ما تدعونه من إيجاب العقل لشيء الأفعال وحظر شيء منها وإباحته حتى يَسْلمَ لكم ما بنيتم عليه . فإن قالوا أو قال إخوانهم من المعتزلة الدليل على ذلك علمنا بوجوب النظر عند قرع الخواطر لقلوبنا وتخويف بعضها من الضرر بترك النظر ونعلم أيضاً وجوب شكر المنعم وترك الكفر به ووجوب معرفة الله وحُسْنَ العدل والإنصاف وقبح الظلم والعدوان فوجب تقرير الفرائض من ناحية العقول يقال لهم : أما قولكم إنكم تعلمون وجوب النظر اضطراراً عند اختلاج الخواطر على القلوب فإنه باطل لأن ذلك لو كان كذلك لاشترك العاقلين، ولم يسع جَحْدُه من قوم بهم ثبَتَتْ الحجة
And if they say: The evidence for preventing the sending of messengers and being without need of them is that God—glorified is He—perfected the intellects, made good good in them and evil evil, and made them an indication to the guidance of creation and their welfare, and prevented by them from mutual oppression, and made them an indication and a means to knowledge of everything that is needed. And it is not permissible for messengers to bring anything other than what is established in the intellect; that indicates being without need of them and the creation's lack of need for them. It is said to them: Why do you deny that there is no way from the side of the intellect to make anything obligatory, nor to prohibit it, nor to permit it, and that that is not established in the rulings of things except from the side of revelation (sam‘)? And that exposure to reward does not occur through actions taking place in the absence of revelation because they are not acts of obedience to God—glorified is He—nor a means of drawing near to Him, and their performer is not rewarded. And if that is so, then there must be a revelation coming upon the tongue of a messenger with an act whose obligation the revelation has established. So it is known that knowledge of acts of drawing near and the attainment of reward for them cannot be established by reason. Then your statement that all that the servants need of guidance and welfare is grasped by intellects is invalidated. So present evidence for the correctness of what you claim—that reason makes some actions obligatory, prohibits some, and permits some—so that what you have built upon it stands for you. If they say—or their brothers among the Mu‘tazila say—the evidence for that is: we know by necessity the obligation of reflection (nazar) when thoughts strike our hearts and some of them frighten us from harm by abandoning reflection, and we also know the obligation of thanking the Benefactor and abandoning ingratitude toward Him, and the obligation of knowing God, and the goodness of justice and fairness, and the evil of injustice and transgression; thus the establishment of duties from the side of intellects becomes necessary. It is said to them: As for your statement that you know the obligation of reflection necessarily when thoughts conflict upon the hearts, it is false, because if that were the case, all rational beings would share in it, and it would not be possible for a people through whom the proof is established to deny it.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponents first argue that God perfected reason, making it sufficient to know good and evil, so there is no need for prophets. Baqillani responds by denying that reason can establish obligation, prohibition, or permission; these come only from revelation (sam‘). Actions without revelation cannot lead to reward. Therefore, revelation via a messenger is necessary. He then addresses a specific Mu‘tazilī argument: they claim that the obligation of reflection (nazar) is known necessarily when thoughts arise, along with other moral duties. Baqillani refutes this by pointing out that if it were necessary, all rational beings would agree, but in fact some deny it, so it is not a necessary knowledge.

Key Terms

السمع
إيجاب
حظر
إباحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that rational knowledge of good and evil entails obligation without revelation.
Correction: Baqillani argues that reason alone does not impose obligation, prohibition, or permission; these require revelation.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, from where do obligation, prohibition, and permission come?
A) Reason alone
B) Revelation (sam‘)
C) Consensus
D) Personal judgment

Key Takeaway
Reason cannot establish obligation, prohibition, or permission; only revelation can, making prophets necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that no action can be a means of drawing near to God or earn reward unless it is based on revelation. Think of a good deed you did this week—did you check whether it had a basis in revelation? If not, what makes you think it will be rewarded?
?
If reason cannot obligate, then every obligation you feel as a Muslim must come from revelation. Are there obligations you follow simply because 'everyone does them' or because your reason tells you they are good, without verifying them from the Quran and Sunnah? How will you change that starting today?
?
This passage says that exposure to reward does not occur through actions without revelation. Consider your daily acts of worship and kindness: do you perform them with the certainty that they are commanded, or do you assume they are good on your own? What is one action you will stop assuming is good and instead seek a revealed basis for?

Refuting the Claim of Necessary Knowledge
Baqillani refutes the Mu‘tazilī claim of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's critique of the Mu‘tazilī claim that the obligation of reflection (nazar) is known necessarily.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge cannot be denied by rational beings.

The Mu‘tazila assert that the obligation to reflect when thoughts arise is known by necessity. Baqillani refutes this by pointing out that if it were necessary, all rational people would agree, but in fact some deny it. This undermines their entire argument for rational moral obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage where the Mu‘tazila present their evidence for necessary knowledge of reflection and other duties.
Pay attention to their claim of 'ilḍtirār' (necessary knowledge).
2
Understand the Mu‘tazilī claim
They claim to know necessarily the obligation of reflection (nazar), thanking the Benefactor, knowing God, and the goodness of justice and evil of injustice.
3
Analyze Baqillani's refutation
Baqillani argues that if it were necessary knowledge, all rational beings would share it and none could deny it. Since some do deny it, it is not necessary.
He uses the principle that necessary knowledge is universal and indisputable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if they say: The evidence for preventing the sending of messengers and being without need of them is that God—glorified is He—perfected the intellects, made good good in them and evil evil, and made them an indication to the guidance of creation and their welfare, and prevented by them from mutual oppression, and made them an indication and a means to knowledge of everything that is needed. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that a moral duty or belief is 'obvious' to everyone, only to find that others sincerely reject it? How does Baqillānī's test of universal agreement challenge your confidence in that assumption? Will you now examine your own convictions to see which ones are truly necessary and which are merely assumed?
?
The Mu‘tazila claimed that thanking the Benefactor is known necessarily. Yet, how often do you fail to thank God in your daily life? Does your neglect reveal that this duty is not as 'necessary' in your heart as you might claim? What will you do today to turn that theoretical knowledge into actual practice?
?
Baqillānī shows that some people deny the obligation of reflection. Reflect on your own life: do you avoid deep reflection (nazar) about God, your purpose, or your actions? What excuse do you give? If this obligation is not necessary by mere thought-striking, what will move you to engage in it deliberately from now on?
