Rational Judgments
beginner
5 min
Reason Cannot Establish Moral Obligations
Refutes the claim that reason alone can know moral guidance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Imam Baqillani, reason alone cannot determine moral obligations, prohibitions, or permissions, and why revelation is necessary for these rulings.

Core Idea
Reason cannot impose moral categories; only revelation can.

This lesson examines a key argument from Imam Baqillani's Tamhid al-Awa'il. He refutes the claim that human reason suffices for moral guidance, asserting that reason cannot establish obligation, prohibition, or permission. Only divine revelation through messengers can provide these moral rulings. The passage challenges the rationalist view and underscores the necessity of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Note the structure of the argument.
Pay attention to the flow: opponent's claim, then refutation.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the sentence where the opponent asserts that messengers need not bring anything beyond reason. Understand why this implies independence from messengers.
3
Identify the three rational judgments
The author denies reason can make anything obligatory, prohibited, or permitted. Locate these three terms in Arabic and note their significance.
These are the core moral categories.
4
Understand the condition for reward
Notice why actions without revelation cannot yield reward. This ties the discussion to eschatology and obedience to Allah.
The author says they are not obedience nor nearness to Allah.
5
Conclude the necessity of revelation
See how the author concludes that revelation through a messenger is indispensable, thus refuting the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من فقده على مراشد الخلق ومصالحهم ومنع بها من التظالم وجعلها دلالة وذريعة إلى علم كل ما يُحتاج إليه . وليس يجوز أن يأتي الرسل بغير ما وضع في العقل فدل ذلك على الغنى عنهم وعدم حاجة الخلق إليهم . فيقال لهم : ما أنكرتم أنه لا سبيل من ناحية العقل إلى إيجاب شيء ولا إلى حظره ولا إلى إباحته وأن ذلك لا يثبت في أحكام الأشياء إلا من جهة السمع وأن التعريض للثواب لا يقع بالأفعال الواقعة مع فقد السمع لأنها لا تكون مع طاعة الله سبحانه ولا قربة إليه ولا يثاب صاحبها وإذا كان ذلك كذلك فلا بد من سمع يأتي على لسان رسول بفعل قرر السمع وجوبه فعلم أن العلم بالقرب وحصول الثواب عليها لا يجوز أن يثبت عقلا فقد بطل قولكم جميع ما يحتاج إليه العباد من المراشد والمصالح مدرك العقول فدلوا على صحة ما تدعونه من إيجاب العقل لشيء الأفعال وحظر شيء منها وإباحته حتى يسلم لكم ما بنيتم عليه
From its absence upon the guidance of creation and their interests, and He prevented thereby from mutual injustice, and made it an indication and a means to knowledge of everything needed. And it is not permissible that the messengers come with anything other than what is established in the intellect, so that indicates independence from them and the lack of need of creation for them. So it is said to them: What prevents you from acknowledging that there is no way from the side of the intellect to make anything obligatory, nor to prohibit it, nor to permit it, and that this is not established in the rulings of things except from the side of revelation, and that exposure to reward does not occur through actions that happen in the absence of revelation because they are not obedience to Allah the Exalted nor a means of nearness to Him, nor is their doer rewarded. And if that is the case, then there must be revelation coming upon the tongue of a messenger by an action whose obligation revelation has established, so it is known that knowledge of acts of nearness and attainment of reward upon them cannot be established intellectually. Thus your statement has been invalidated: 'All that the servants need of guidance and interests are comprehended by the intellects.' So prove the correctness of what you claim of the intellect making some actions obligatory, prohibiting some, and permitting some, so that what you have built upon it remains sound for you.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that human reason alone can determine moral obligations without revelation. The opponent's argument, given at the start, asserts that reason suffices for guidance and that messengers cannot bring anything beyond reason, making them unnecessary. The author replies by denying that reason can establish obligation, prohibition, or permission; these come only from revelation. Actions without revelation cannot be acts of obedience or nearness to Allah, and thus do not yield reward. Therefore, revelation through a messenger is necessary. The author then concludes that the opponent's view that all needs are rationally known is false, and challenges them to prove their claim that reason can impose moral categories.

Misconception: Reason alone can determine all moral obligations and reward.
Correction: Imam Baqillani argues that reason cannot impose obligation, prohibition, or permission; these require revelation, and actions without revelation cannot earn reward.

Checkpoint

A) Because reason can determine reward
B) Because they are not obedience to Allah nor nearness to Him
C) Because they are always sinful
D) Because revelation is only for prophets

Key Takeaway
Without revelation, no action can be an act of obedience or earn reward; therefore, reason alone cannot establish moral obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The lesson states that reason cannot make anything obligatory. Is there a 'should' in your life that you believe is obviously required by reason (e.g., charity, honesty) but you have not grounded in divine command? What would change if you started seeing it as a command from Allah, not just a rational duty?

Refuting Necessary Knowledge of Reflection
Refutes the claim that the obligation of reflection is known by necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation of reflection (al-nazar) cannot be known necessarily, as demonstrated by the fact that many rational people deny it.

Core Idea
If knowledge were necessary, all rational people would agree; but many reject reflection entirely.

This passage presents a central objection from the Mu'tazila, who claim that the obligation of reflection is self-evident and known by necessity. Imam al-Baqillani refutes this by invoking a simple test: necessary knowledge cannot be denied by any rational person. Yet materialists, dualists, and others explicitly deny the goodness of reflection, proving that its obligation is not a necessary truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Start by reading the Mu'tazila claim: they assert that the obligation of reflection is known necessarily when thoughts arise, along with other moral truths like gratitude to the Benefactor and the goodness of justice.
Notice the examples they give—they treat all of these as self-evident.
2
Identify the refutation's main argument
Imam al-Baqillani argues that if something were known necessarily, it would be universally admitted and impossible to deny. He then points to groups that deny the goodness of reflection outright: materialists (dahriyya), dualists (thanawiyya), and others.
The refutation uses the principle of universal agreement as a test for necessary knowledge.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the implication: since many rational people not only deny the obligation but also consider reflection evil, the claim of necessary knowledge collapses. The Imam calls this position 'utter absurdity'.
This shows that the obligation of reflection is a matter of rational argument, not self-evident intuition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا أو قال إخوانهم من المعتزلة الدليل على ذلك علمنا بوجوب النظر عند قرع الخواطر لقلوبنا وتخويف بعضها من الضرر بترك النظر ونعلم أيضاً وجوب شكر المنعم وترك الكفر به ووجوب معرفة الله وحسن العدل والإنصاف وقبح الظلم والعدوان فوجب تقرير الفرائض من ناحية العقول يقال لهم: أما قولكم إنكم تعلمون وجوب النظر اضطراراً عند اختلاج الخواطر على القلوب فإنه باطل لأن ذلك لو كان كذلك لاشترك العاقلين، ولم يسع جحده من قوم بهم ثبتت الحجة ونضطر إلى صدق نقلهم فيما أخبروا به عن مشاهدة واضطرار. وفي علمنا بخلاف ذلك من أنفسنا وعلمنا أن كثيراً من الدهرية وأهل الملل ينكر حسن النظر جملة وقول كثير من الثنوية إنه باطل وإنه سفه وشر وإنه من تدبير الظلام لأنه يورث العداوة والأحقاد ويخرج إلى الهرج والفساد واستحلال الدماء والأموال دليل على أن العلم بوجوبه أبعد عن أن يكون اضطراراً. وكيف يعلم وجوبه اضطراراً من لا يعلم حسنه اضطراراً أو يعتقد وجوب تركه وقبحه؟ هذا غاية البهت ممن صار إليه من البراهمة والمعتزلة
For if they say, or their brothers among the Mu'tazila say, 'The evidence for that is our knowledge of the obligation of reflection when thoughts knock upon our hearts and some of them frighten us of harm due to abandoning reflection, and we also know the obligation of thanking the Benefactor and refraining from ingratitude to Him, and the obligation of knowing Allah, and the goodness of justice and fairness, and the badness of injustice and aggression, so the establishment of obligations from the side of the intellects is necessary.' It is said to them: As for your statement that you know the obligation of reflection necessarily when thoughts stir upon the hearts, it is false, because if that were the case, then all rational people would agree, and it would not be possible for a people through whom the proof is established to deny it, and we would be compelled to believe the truthfulness of their report of what they have informed of observation and necessity. And our knowledge of the contrary from ourselves, and our knowledge that many of the materialists and people of [other] religions completely deny the goodness of reflection, and the statement of many of the Dualists that it is false, foolish, evil, and from the design of darkness because it begets enmity and grudges and leads to chaos, corruption, and legalizing blood and wealth — this is evidence that knowledge of its obligation is far from being necessary. And how can one know its obligation necessarily when he does not know its goodness necessarily, or he believes in the obligation of abandoning it and its badness? This is the utmost absurdity from those of the Brahmins and Mu'tazila who hold it.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage contains the Mu'tazila objection and the author's refutation of the first part. The Mu'tazila claim that we know by necessity the obligation of reflection (al-nazar) when thoughts arise, as well as other moral truths. The author refutes this by arguing that if such knowledge were necessary, all rational people would share it and none could deny it. But in fact, many materialists and dualists deny the goodness of reflection altogether, calling it evil and destructive. Therefore, the knowledge cannot be necessary. The author also points out the absurdity that someone who does not know its goodness necessarily cannot be said to know its obligation necessarily.

Misconception: Some might think the author is rejecting the obligation of reflection entirely.
Correction: The author only denies that its obligation is known necessarily; he does not deny the obligation itself, which is established by other proofs.

Checkpoint

B) Many people deny its goodness or even consider it evil
C) The Mu'tazila themselves disagree
D) Reflection is only required for prophets

Key Takeaway
The obligation of reflection is not known necessarily because many rational people deny its goodness, which would be impossible if the knowledge were necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that the need to seek knowledge of Allah is so obvious that everyone must accept it? This passage shows that many rational people deny even the obligation to reflect. Does that change how you view those who reject faith—are they simply ignoring the obvious, or is there more to their denial? What would it take for you to truly confront the possibility that you might be wrong about the foundations of your belief?
?
The materialists and dualists argue that reflection leads to enmity and chaos. Is there any truth to their claim in your own experience? Have you ever avoided deep reflection about God or the afterlife because it might disturb your comfort, challenge your assumptions, or demand change in your life? If so, what does that say about the state of your heart?
?
If the obligation of reflection is not known necessarily, then your own conviction to seek knowledge is not automatic—it is a choice. On what basis do you personally hold that reflection is obligatory? Is it because you were taught, because it makes sense, or because you truly cannot imagine abandoning it? What would it take for you to feel the weight of that obligation personally, right now, and act on it?

The Symmetry Problem: Refuting Necessary Knowledge of Gratitude
Symmetry argument against necessary gratitude knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila claim of necessary knowledge of gratitude fails due to the symmetry argument.

Core Idea
Any claim of necessary knowledge can be symmetrically reversed with no rational differentiator.

The Mu'tazila argued that we know the obligation to thank the Benefactor necessarily, without need for revelation. The author counters with a powerful logical move: if such knowledge were truly necessary, then someone could just as well claim the opposite with the same certainty. This symmetric challenge exposes the weakness of their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with the symmetry argument
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the first sentence. Notice how the author directly challenges the Mu'tazila claim by asking for a differentiator.
The phrase 'ما الفرق بينكم وبين من قال إنكم تعلمون بطلان ذلك اضطراراً' is the core of the argument.
2
Understand the logical structure
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if both claims are made with equal necessity, there is no way to decide between them, so the original claim cannot be inherently necessary.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this destroys the Mu'tazila position: they cannot provide a criterion to distinguish their necessary knowledge from a contradictory one.
This shows that the claim of necessity is baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم في قولهم: إنا نعلم وجوب شكر المنعم وترك الكفر به اضطراراً ما الفرق بينكم وبين من قال إنكم تعلمون بطلان ذلك اضطراراً؟ فلا يجدون لذلك مدفعاً. وكذلك يقال لهم لو علمتم حسن الذاذ غيركم لكم إذا قصد نفعكم وقبح إيلامه لكم إذا قصد الاضرار بكم، لوجب أن نعلم من حسن ذلك أو قبحه ما علمتم من غير سمع وتوقيف على حسن وقبحه، اللهم إلا أن يعنوا بالحسن ميل الطباع إلى فعل اللذات ونفورها عن فعل الآلام فهذا لعمري معلوم حساً ولكن ليس ميل الطباع إلى الشيء يقتضي شكر فاعله ولا نفورها عنه يقتضي قبحه وذمه على سبيل ما تدعونه فبطل ما تعلقتم
And it is said to them regarding their statement: 'We know the obligation of thanking the Benefactor and refraining from ingratitude to Him necessarily' — what is the difference between you and someone who says that you know the falsehood of that necessarily? They will find no answer to that. Likewise, it is said to them: If you knew the goodness of someone else's pleasure for you when he intends your benefit, and the badness of his paining you when he intends harm to you, then it would be necessary that we know of the goodness or badness of that what you know without revelation and an authoritative text on its goodness and badness. Unless they mean by 'good' the inclination of natures towards doing pleasures and their aversion from doing pains — this, by my life, is known by sense, but the inclination of natures towards a thing does not necessitate thanking its doer, nor does their aversion from it necessitate its badness and blame in the way you claim. So what you have adhered to is invalidated.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is the author's refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that the obligation of thanking the Benefactor (shukr al-mun'im) is known necessarily. The author argues that if it were necessary, then someone could equally claim that its falsehood is necessary, and the opponent cannot differentiate. Then he examines the claim about knowing the goodness of pleasure and badness of pain by reason alone. If such knowledge were inherent, it would have to be independent of revelation. But the only sense in which goodness and badness can be known naturally is the sensory inclination toward pleasure and aversion to pain, which does not imply moral obligation or blame. Thus the Mu'tazila position collapses.

Key Terms
شكر المنعم
اضطرار
فرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila position is self-evident and cannot be challenged.
Correction: The symmetry argument shows that if it were self-evident, no opposing self-evident claim could exist, but here it can.

Checkpoint
What does the author ask the Mu'tazila to differentiate?
A) Between their necessary knowledge and sensory knowledge
B) Between their claim and the opposite claim of necessary falsehood
C) Between the Benefactor and other benefactors
D) Between gratitude and ingratitude

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila claim of necessary knowledge fails because it can be symmetrically reversed with no rational differentiator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about your duty to God as 'obvious' or 'necessary' without examining whether someone else could reasonably claim the opposite? What might that belief be, and how does this argument challenge your certainty?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed their knowledge of gratitude was necessary, but this passage shows that assumption can be symmetrically reversed. Is there an obligation in your life you treat as unquestionable? What would it mean to test it with the same logical scrutiny?
?
This argument exposes the weakness of claims that lack evidence. In your own faith, where might you be relying on comfortable certainty rather than clear proof? What would it cost you to honestly examine that area today?

Sensory Inclination vs. Moral Obligation
Distinguishes sensory inclination from moral duty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between sensory inclinations and moral obligations, and see why the Mu'tazila confuse the two.

Core Idea
Sensory desire for pleasure and aversion to pain do not create moral duties.

Even if the Mu'tazila concede that we know goodness and badness directly, the author narrows the discussion. He argues that the only kind of 'good' and 'bad' known without revelation is sensory inclination toward pleasure and away from pain. But this natural inclination does not imply moral obligation to thank the doer or blame for causing pain. Thus, they fail to derive moral duties from natural feelings.

Guided Path
1
Read the extended argument
Read the Arabic and translation from 'وكذلك يقال لهم' to the end. Note the conditional statement and the exception.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'good'
The author distinguishes between moral goodness (which requires revelation) and sensory goodness (natural inclination). The Mu'tazila imply the former but only prove the latter.
Pay attention to 'اللهم إلا أن يعنوا بالحسن ميل الطباع'.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concedes that sensory good/bad is known by sense, but denies it carries moral weight. Therefore, the Mu'tazila argument fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

They will find no answer to that. Likewise, it is said to them: If you knew the goodness of someone else's pleasure for you when he intends your benefit, and the badness of his paining you when he intends harm to you, then it would be necessary that we know of the goodness or badness of that what you know without revelation and an authoritative text on its goodness and badness. So what you have adhered to is invalidated.

The author argues that if it were necessary, then someone could equally claim that its falsehood is necessary, and the opponent cannot differentiate. ?

?

?
If sensory aversion does not make something blameworthy, then your dislike of a command of Allah (e.g., giving charity when you are attached to wealth) is not a valid excuse. What command do you find yourself resisting because of natural aversion, and how will you now respond?

Refuting the Claim that Obligation of Nazar is Necessary Knowledge
Baqillani refutes the claim of necessary obligation of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Baqillani argues that the obligation to engage in rational reflection cannot be necessary knowledge, based on the existence of widespread denial.

Core Idea
Denial by multiple groups disproves any claim of necessary knowledge.

In this passage, al-Baqillani tackles a central claim of certain rationalists: that every sane person knows necessarily that they must engage in rational reflection (nazar). He counters by pointing to the real-world diversity of opinions, including entire groups who reject nazar outright. This lesson walks through his evidence and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic and translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the rhetorical question at the end.
2
Identify the groups mentioned
List the groups who deny nazar: Dahrīyah, Thunawiyyah, Barāhimah, Mu'tazila.
Understand what each group stands for.
3
Analyze the logic
Baqillani uses their denial as evidence that the obligation cannot be necessary knowledge. Trace his argument: if it were necessary, everyone would agree; but they don't, so it's not.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونُضطر الى صدق نقلهم فيما أخبروا به عن مشاهدة واضطرار. وفي علمنا بخلاف ذلك من أنفسنا وعلمنا أن كثيراً من الدهرية وأهل الملل ينكر حسن النظر جُملة وقول كثير من الثنوية إنه باطل وإنه سَفَهُ وشر وإنه من تدبير الظلام لأنه يورث العداوة والأحقاد ويُخرجُ إلى الهرج والفساد واستحلال الدماء والأموال دليل على أن العلم بوجوبه أبعد عن أن يكون اضطراراً. وكيف يعْلَمُ وجوبه اضطراراً من لا يعلم حُسنَه اضطراراً أو يعتقد وجوب تركه وقبحه؟ هذا غاية البهت ممن صار اليه من البراهمة والمعتزلة.
And we are compelled to the truthfulness of their reports regarding what they reported from direct observation and necessity. And in our knowledge of the contrary of that within ourselves, and our knowledge that many of the Dahriyyah (Materialists) and followers of religions deny the goodness of reflection (nazar) entirely, and the statement of many of the Thunawiyyah (Dualists) that it is false and that it is folly and evil and that it is from the planning of darkness because it causes enmity and grudges and leads to chaos and corruption and the making lawful of blood and property — all this is evidence that knowledge of its obligation is far from being necessitated (idtiraran). And how can one know its obligation necessarily while he does not know its goodness necessarily, or rather believes in the obligation of abandoning it and its evil? This is the height of absurdity on the part of those who have resorted to it among the Brahmins (Barāhimah) and the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the rationalist claim that the obligation to engage in rational reflection (nazar) is known necessarily (idtiraran) by every sane person. The author, Imam al-Baqillani, argues that if it were truly necessary knowledge, then everyone would agree upon it. But in reality, many groups deny it outright: the Dahriyyah (materialist atheists) reject the goodness of reflection, and the Thunawiyyah (dualists) call it evil and claim it originates from the principle of darkness, leading to enmity and bloodshed. This widespread denial proves that the knowledge of its obligation cannot be necessary. Furthermore, the author asks a rhetorical question: how can someone who does not even know the goodness of nazar necessarily, or who believes it is obligatory to abandon it and that it is evil, be said to know its obligation necessarily? The passage ends by branding this claim as the height of absurdity (bahat) on the part of the Brahmins and the Mu'tazila who hold it.

Key Terms
اضطرار (necessity)
نظر (rational reflection)
حسن (goodness)
وجوب (obligation)
دهرية (Dahriyyah)
ثنوية (Thunawiyyah)
براهمة (Brahmins)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The obligation to engage in rational reflection (nazar) is known necessarily (idtiraran) by all sane people.
Correction: Al-Baqillani shows that many groups deny nazar, which proves that knowledge of its obligation is not necessary; if it were necessary, everyone would agree.

Checkpoint
What evidence does al-Baqillani use to argue that knowledge of the obligation of rational reflection (nazar) is not necessary (idtirari)?
A) He claims that all people agree on it.
B) He points out that many people deny it, such as the Dahriyyah and Thunawiyyah.
C) He argues that it is known only through revelation.
D) He states that it is known by intuition.

Key Takeaway
The existence of widespread denial of nazar proves that its obligation is not a matter of necessary knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you assume that everyone who disagrees with you on a religious obligation is simply ignorant or stubborn? How does this passage's exposure of the absurdity of that assumption challenge your interactions with those who deny aspects of faith? What can you do today to humbly reconsider a conviction you hold as 'obvious'?
?
The Thunawiyyah claimed that nazar leads to chaos and evil. Have you ever avoided serious reflection on your faith because it might unsettle comfortable routines or require difficult changes? What would engaging in deep nazar cost you, and are you willing to pay that price?
?
If the obligation to reflect on your beliefs is not something you can take for granted, then you must actively choose it. Will you commit to setting aside time this week for unhurried, honest reflection on one aspect of your theology? What specific question will you start with?

The Stalemate Over Necessity
Baqillani uses a rhetorical question to show the stalemate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming necessary knowledge of moral obligation does not settle the debate, as a similar claim can be made against it.

Core Idea
Claiming necessity cuts both ways — it proves nothing.

Baqillani begins by addressing the opponents' assertion that they know the obligation to thank the Benefactor necessarily. He points out that this claim is no more valid than someone claiming the opposite necessarily. This creates an impasse that the opponents cannot escape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp its structure. It is a single rhetorical question and its consequence.
Notice the phrase 'ما الفرق بينكم وبين من قال'.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents claim they know 'وجوب شكر المنعم' necessarily (اضطراراً). Understand what they are asserting.
The claim is about gratitude to the Benefactor and avoiding ingratitude.
3
Analyze Baqillani's counter-question
He asks: what distinguishes your claim from someone who claims to know its falsity necessarily? This undermines the claim's uniqueness.
This is a form of reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the stalemate
The opponents cannot refute the counter-claim, so their argument collapses. The claim of necessity is not decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم في قولهم : إنا نعلم وجوب شكر المنعم وترك الكفر به اضطراراً ما الفرق بينكم وبين من قال إنكم تعلمون بطلان ذلك اضطراراً؟ فلا يجدون لذلك مُدْفَعاً. وكذلك يقال لهم لو علمتم حُسن الذاذ غيركم لكم إذا قصد نفعكم وقبح إيلامه لكم إذا قصد الاضرار بكم، لوجب أن نعلم من حسن ذلك أو قبحه ما علمتم من غير سمع وتوقيف على حسن وقبحه، اللهم إلا أن يعنُوا بالحَسَنَ مَيْلَ الطباع إلى فعل اللذات ونُفُورَهـا عن فعل الآلام فهذا لعمري معلوم حِسّاً ولكن ليس ميل الطباع إلى الشيء يقتضي شكر فاعله ولا نُفُورُها عنه يقتضي قبحه وذمه على سبيل ما تدعونه فبطل ما تعلقتم.
And it is said to them regarding their statement: 'We know the obligation of thanking the Benefactor and refraining from ungratefulness to Him necessarily (idtiraran)': what is the difference between you and one who says that you know the falsity of that necessarily? Thus they find no escape from that. And likewise it is said to them: if you knew the goodness of another's giving you pleasure when he intends benefit to you, and the evil of his causing you pain when he intends harm to you, then it would be necessary for us to know of the goodness of that or its evil that which you know without hearing (sam') and specification (tawqif) regarding its goodness and evil. Unless they mean by 'goodness' (al-hasan) the inclination of natures (mayl al-ṭabā'i') towards performing pleasures and their aversion (nufūruhā) from performing pains — this, by my life, is known by sense (ḥissan), but the inclination of nature towards a thing does not necessitate gratitude to its doer, nor does its aversion from it necessitate its evil and blame in the way you claim. So what you clung to is nullified.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer argument. The objectors (the Brahmins and Mu'tazila) claim that they know the obligation to thank the Benefactor and to avoid ingratitude necessarily (idtiraran). The author responds by first arguing that this claim is indistinguishable from someone who claims to know its falsity necessarily — a clear stalemate. Then he offers a second argument: if they claim to know the goodness of pleasure and the evil of pain based on their own experience, then everyone would know this without revelation. But then the author clarifies that if by 'goodness' they merely mean natural inclination towards pleasure and aversion from pain, that is sensory knowledge, not rational obligation. Such natural inclinations do not entail that the agent deserves gratitude or blame. Thus their position collapses.

Key Terms
وجوب (obligation)
شكر المنعم (gratitude to the Benefactor)
اضطرار (necessity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something feels necessarily true, it must be universally accepted.
Correction: Baqillani shows that differing claims of necessity cancel each other out.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say about the opponents' claim of necessary knowledge of gratitude?
A) He accepts it as true.
B) He asks how it differs from someone claiming the opposite necessarily.
C) He argues that gratitude is not obligatory.
D) He says it is known by sense.

Key Takeaway
Necessity claims in moral knowledge are not self-certifying; they can be met with counter-claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain of a moral conviction, only to meet someone equally certain of the opposite? Did that shake your certainty, or did you dismiss it? How does Baqillani's argument challenge your own claims of 'obvious' moral truths?
?
When you disagree with someone on a matter of right and wrong, do you tend to assume they are simply stubborn or ignorant? This lesson shows that both sides can claim 'necessity'. Are you willing to genuinely consider that your moral intuition might not be universally self-evident? What would that require you to do differently?
?
Baqillani's point is that claiming necessity is not a winning argument. In your own debates or inner convictions, where might you be using 'I just know it' as a shield against deeper reflection? Will you submit your most cherished beliefs to the same scrutiny you apply to others'?

Natural Inclination vs. Rational Obligation
Baqillani clarifies that natural inclination is sensory, not a basis for moral obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between natural inclination towards pleasure and rational moral obligation, and why the former does not entail the latter.

Core Idea
Natural inclination is sensory, not moral; it does not demand gratitude.

After the stalemate, Baqillani offers a second argument. He distinguishes between two meanings of 'goodness' — one based on sensory natural inclination, and the other based on rational obligation. He concedes the former but denies that it implies moral duty.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the conditional structure and the exception.
Look for 'اللهم إلا أن يعنوا' which signals an exception.
2
Understand the conditional argument
Baqillani says: if you know goodness and evil through pleasure/pain, then everyone would know without revelation.
This challenges the opponents' claim to unique knowledge.
3
Identify the alternative interpretation
If by 'goodness' they mean natural inclination (ميل الطباع), then it is sensed, not rationally known.
4
Recognize why natural inclination does not entail obligation
Inclination towards pleasure does not mean the one who causes it deserves gratitude; aversion from pain does not mean the one who causes it deserves blame.
This is the core distinction.
5
Conclude the nullification
The opponents' position is based on a confusion between sensory inclination and rational obligation. Therefore it fails.

So what you clung to is nullified.

B) It is known by sense but does not necessitate gratitude to the doer.
C) It is identical to rational obligation.

Sensory natural inclination does not create rational moral obligations like gratitude.

Pre-Revelation Moral Knowledge is Taqlid, Not Knowledge
Baqillani responds to the objection that pre-revelation moral knowledge proves its rationality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Baqillani refutes the claim that moral knowledge before revelation proves its rationality by arguing that such beliefs are based on imitation, not genuine knowledge.

Core Idea
Moral beliefs without revelation are imitation, not rational knowledge.

This passage presents a crucial objection: if moral knowledge depends on revelation, then how can people before revelation know right from wrong? Al-Baqillani's answer sharpens the distinction between true knowledge and mere conjecture. As you read, notice how he uses analogies to clarify the epistemic status of such pre-revelation moral beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the argument that moral knowledge is known before revelation, so it cannot depend on revelation. The objectors claim that since we and many religious followers know good and evil before revelation reaches us, such knowledge must be rational.
Notice the phrase 'before knowledge of the soundness of revelation' which sets up the objection.
2
Identify Baqillani's key response
He concedes the fact of pre-revelation moral beliefs but challenges their status as knowledge. Find the terms he uses to describe them: 'conjecture' (ẓann) and 'imitation' (taqlīd).
Baqillani does not deny the beliefs are correct; he denies they are knowledge.
3
Analyze the analogies
Baqillani gives two analogies: (1) believing something correctly without necessity or deduction is not knowledge, and (2) believing a description/judgment is established for a thing while ignorant of its cause ('illa) is not true knowledge. Reflect on how these support his claim that pre-revelation moral beliefs are not knowledge.
These analogies show that correct belief must be justified properly to count as knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا: لو كان العلم بوجوب هذه الأمور وقبح القبيح الذي ذكرناه منها وحسن الحسن لا يُعْلَم إلا من طريق السمع لم يعلم قبحَ ذلك لا حُسنَه إلا من عَلِمَ السَّمْعَ وعرفه فلما كنا نعلم ذلك ويعلمه كثير من أهل الملل قبل العلم بصحة السمع وبلوغه إلينا ثبت أن العلم بما وصفنا ليس بموقوف على ورود السمع ; يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم ألا يعلم ذلك إلا مَنْ علم السمع وعرف وجوبه وأن يكون من اعتقد قبح القبيح وحسن الحسن من غير علم بما له كان حسناً وقبيحاً فإنه معتقد للشيء على ما هـو به وإن لم يكن اعتقاده ذلك علماً بل هو ظن وتقليد وعلى سبيل المتابعة لأهل الشرائع كما أن المُعْتَقِدَ للشيء على ما هو به من غير جهة الاضطرار والاستدلال غير عالم به وإن كان مُعْتَقِداً له على ما هو به وكما أن المعتقد لكون الوصف والحكم ثابتاً للشيء مع الجهل بعلته التي كان لها غيرُ عالم به في الحقيقة ; وهذا يبطل تعلقهم.
And if they say: 'If the knowledge of the obligation of these matters and the evil of the evil we mentioned from them and the goodness of the good were not known except by way of revelation (sam'), then no one would know its evil nor its goodness except one who knows revelation and recognizes it. Since we know that and many followers of religions know it before knowledge of the soundness of revelation and its reaching us, it is established that the knowledge of what we described is not dependent on the advent of revelation.' It is said to them: 'What prevents you [from admitting] that this is not known except by one who knows revelation and recognizes its obligation, and that whoever believes in the evil of the evil and the goodness of the good without knowledge of what made it good and evil — then he is a believer in the thing as it is, even if his belief is not knowledge, but rather it is conjecture (ẓann) and imitation (taqlīd), and on the way of following (mutāba'a) the people of religious laws; just as the one who believes in a thing as it is without the mode of necessity (iḍṭirār) and deduction (istidlāl) is not a knower of it even if he believes it as it is; and just as the one who believes that the description and judgment are established for a thing while being ignorant of the cause (ʿilla) due to which it is so is not truly a knower of it. And this nullifies what they cling to.'
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage contains another objection and its answer. The objectors argue: if moral knowledge (obligation, good, evil) came only from revelation, then no one before revelation would have it. Yet many people, including followers of religions, do know these moral values before revelation reaches them personally. Therefore, such knowledge cannot depend on revelation — it must be rational. Al-Baqillani answers by distinguishing between true knowledge (ʿilm) and imitation (taqlīd) or conjecture. He says that people may hold correct beliefs about good and evil through following the previous prophets or religious communities, without having rational demonstration or necessary knowledge. This is akin to someone who believes a proposition correctly but without proper epistemic justification — that belief is not considered real knowledge. Therefore, the fact that people have moral beliefs before revelation does not prove that they are rationally known; they may be acquired through tradition and imitation, which does not constitute rational knowledge.

Key Terms
سمع (revelation/sam')
تقليد (imitation/taqlid)
ظن (conjecture)
متابعة (following)
علة (cause/ʿilla)
اضطرار (necessity)
استدلال (deduction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That having a true belief about good and evil automatically qualifies as knowledge.
Correction: Al-Baqillani shows that true belief can be based on imitation (taqlīd) or conjecture, which lacks the epistemic justification required for knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does al-Baqillani explain the fact that many people know moral values before revelation?
A) He admits that reason alone is sufficient.
B) He claims that they have innate knowledge.
C) He argues that their belief is based on imitation (taqlid) of earlier prophetic traditions.
D) He says they are actually ignorant of real good and evil.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani argues that pre-revelation moral beliefs are not genuine knowledge but imitation, thus the objection that they prove rationality of moral knowledge fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a moral position, is it based on genuine knowledge or mere imitation of your community? What would it take to move from imitation to knowledge?
?
Think of a moral conviction you hold strongly. Can you trace its origin? Is it from revelation, rational deduction, or just taken for granted? How does this passage challenge you?
?
Have you ever assumed that simply believing the right thing is enough to claim knowledge? What would it mean for you to truly know something rather than just believe it?

Refuting the Objection: Revelation-Based Morality Does Not Entail All Knowledge from Revelation
Al-Baqillani refutes the objection that revelation-based morality would generalize to all knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Baqillani refutes the objection that if moral knowledge is known only through revelation, then all rational knowledge (e.g., eternity of the eternal) would also have to be from revelation.

Core Idea
Unwarranted generalizations cannot overturn consistent premises.

In this passage, al-Baqillani addresses a key objection from the Brahmins and Mu'tazila: if we say moral good and evil are known by revelation, then wouldn't all rational truths—like the eternity of the eternal—also have to come from revelation? He shows the flaw in this reasoning by pointing out that it is a mere claim, and he begins to draw an analogy with necessary versus acquired knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Note how the objectors (Brahmins and Mu'tazila) frame their argument: if moral values are known by revelation, then all rational truths (eternity, temporality, substance, accident) would also be known by revelation, which they deem impossible.
The objectors are trying to force a contradiction.
2
Identify al-Baqillani's initial response
Al-Baqillani immediately dismisses the objection as a mere claim (daʿwā) without proof. This is a key move: he refuses to accept the premise that moral knowledge being from revelation forces all knowledge to be from revelation.
The burden of proof is on the objectors.
3
Examine the counter-analogy
Al-Baqillani then begins a counter-analogy: just as some knowledge is necessary (iḍṭirārī) and some is acquired through deduction (istidlālī), without one implying the other for all knowledge, so too some knowledge can be from revelation without all having to be from revelation.
The passage cuts off, but the logical move is clear.
4
Reflect on the fallacy
The objectors' reasoning is a false generalization. Al-Baqillani shows that the fact that one kind of knowledge comes from revelation does not entail that all knowledge must come from revelation, any more than necessary knowledge entails that all knowledge is necessary.
This is a common fallacy in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال من الفريقين قائل أعني البراهمة والمعتزلة : لو كان قبح هذه الأمور وحسنها غير معلوم بالعقل بل بالسمع لوجب أن يكون العلم بقدم القديم وحدوث المحدّثِ وحقيقة الجوهر والعَرَض والعلم بكل معلوم غير مُدْرَك من ناحية العقل بل بحجة السمع فلما لم يجز ذلك بطل ما قلتم , قيل لهم : لم قلتم هذا فلا يجدون في ذلك سوى الدعوى. ثم يقال لهم : ما الفصل بينكم وبين من زعم أنه لو جاز أو وجب أن يُعْلَمَ بعض المعلومات اضطراراً لا استدلالاً لجاز أو وجب أن يُعلم سائر المعلومات اضطراراً لا استدلالاً وكذلك لو جاز أن يُعْلَمَ بعض المعلومات نظراً
Then if a speaker from the two groups — I mean the Brahmins and the Mu'tazila — says: 'If the evil of these matters and their goodness were not known by reason (ʿaql) but rather by revelation (samʿ), then it would be necessary that knowledge of the eternity of the eternal, the temporality of the originated, the true nature of substance and accident, and knowledge of every known thing would not be attained from the side of reason but rather by the proof of revelation. Since that is not allowed, what you have said is nullified.' It is said to them: 'Why do you say this?' Thus they find nothing in that except mere claim (daʿwā). Then it is said to them: 'What is the distinction between you and one who claims that if it is permitted or obligatory to know some information necessarily (iḍṭirāran) without deduction (istidlālan), then it would be permitted or obligatory to know the rest of information necessarily without deduction — and likewise if it is permitted to know some information by reflection (naẓaran)...'
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents the final objection and the beginning of the author's response. The objectors (Brahmins and Mu'tazila) argue by analogy: if moral good and evil are known only through revelation, then by extension, all rational knowledge — such as the eternity of the eternal, the temporality of the contingent, the true nature of substance and accident — would also have to be known through revelation. Since that is absurd (as these are rational truths), the premise that moral values are known only by revelation must be false. Al-Baqillani responds by dismissing the objection as a mere claim (daʿwā) without proof. He then begins to offer a counter-analogy: just as it is possible for some knowledge to be necessary (iḍṭirārī) while other knowledge requires deduction (istidlālī), so too some knowledge can be from revelation while the rest is not. The argument is cut off, but the method of the response is clear: the objector's generalization is unwarranted.

Key Terms
عقل (reason)
سمع (revelation)
قدم القديم (eternity of the eternal)
حدوث المحدث (temporality of the originated)
جوهر (substance)
عرض (accident)
دعوى (mere claim)
اضطرار (necessity)
استدلال (deduction)
نظر (reflection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If one type of knowledge (moral) comes from revelation, then all knowledge must come from revelation.
Correction: Al-Baqillani shows that this is a false generalization; different types of knowledge can have different sources, just as some knowledge is necessary and some is deduced.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised by the Brahmins and Mu'tazila in this passage?
A) That moral knowledge is known by reason, not revelation.
B) That if moral knowledge were from revelation, then all knowledge, including the nature of substance and accident, would be from revelation, which is false.
C) That revelation is unnecessary for moral knowledge.
D) That the Brahmins and Mu'tazila agree with al-Baqillani.

Key Takeaway
Generalizing from one case without proof is a fallacy; al-Baqillani refutes the objection by showing it is a mere claim and then offering a better analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you, like the objectors, assumed that if one thing is true then much more must also be true without evidence? How did that assumption lead you away from the truth? Commit now to examining one such assumption you hold.
?
Al-Baqillani calls the objector's argument a 'mere claim'. In your pursuit of knowledge, how often do you accept claims without demanding proof? Which belief in your life is most vulnerable to being a mere claim, and what would it take to test it?
?
This lesson distinguishes between necessary and acquired knowledge. In your daily life, do you treat all knowledge as equally accessible, or do you recognize that some truths require effort (like study and reasoning) while others are given (like revelation)? Which category do you neglect, and how will you restore balance?

Moral Knowledge Before Revelation Is Not True Knowledge
Baqillani refutes the claim that moral values are known without revelation by showing it is taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why moral judgments made without revelation are considered opinion and imitation (taqlid) rather than true knowledge (ʿilm) in classical Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Believing correctly without proper reasoning is not knowledge.

The author presents a common objection: people know good and bad even before verifying revelation. How can such knowledge be denied? Baqillani responds by distinguishing between true knowledge (ʿilm) and mere opinion or imitation (taqlid). He uses two analogies to show that correct belief without proper justification does not count as knowledge. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Focus on the objection-and-answer structure.
The passage is dense. It helps to first grasp the opponent's claim before examining the response.
2
Identify the opponent's objection
Find the sentences where the opponent argues that moral values are known before revelation. Note the evidence they offer.
The opponent cites that many religious people know good and bad before verifying revelation.
3
Understand Baqillani's answer
Read how the author refutes the objection. He introduces a distinction: correct belief without proper reasoning is not knowledge. Look for the two analogies he uses to clarify this.
The analogies involve belief without necessary/inferential proof and belief without knowledge of the cause.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the opponent's objection fails because the claimed 'knowledge' is only opinion and imitation. Consider how this reinforces the role of revelation in moral knowledge.
This passage defends the necessity of revelation for true moral knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكرناه منها وحسن الحسن لا يُعْلَم إلا من طريق السمع لم يعلم قبحَ ذلك لا حسنه إلا من عَلِمَ السمعَ وعرفه فلما كنا نعلم ذلك ويعلمه كثير من أهل الملل قبل العلم بصحة السمع وبلوغه إلينا ثبت أن العلم بما وصفنا ليس بموقوف على ورود السمع ؛ يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم ألا يعلم ذلك إلا من علم السمع وعرف وجوبه وأن من اعتقد قبح القبيح وحسن الحسن من غير علم بما له كان حسناً وقبيحاً فإنه معتقد للشيء على ما هو به وإن لم يكن اعتقاده ذلك علماً بل هو ظن وتقليد وعلى سبيل المتابعة لأهل الشرائع كما أن المعتقد للشيء على ما هو به من غير جهة الاضطرار والاستدلال غير عالم به وإن كان معتقداً له على ما هو به وكما أن المعتقد لكون الوصف والحكم ثابتاً للشيء مع الجهل بعلته التي كان لها غيرُ عالم به في الحقيقة ؛ وهذا يبطل تعلقهم
We mentioned from it: the good of the good is not known except through the way of revelation; he does not know the badness of that nor its goodness except he who knows revelation and recognizes it. Since we know that and many people of religions know it before knowledge of the validity of revelation and its reaching us, it is established that knowledge of what we described is not dependent on the arrival of revelation. It is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that it is not known except by him who knows revelation and recognizes its obligation, and that he who believes the badness of the bad and the goodness of the good without knowledge of why it is good or bad is indeed a believer in the thing as it is, even if his belief is not knowledge but rather opinion and imitation and on the way of following the people of laws, just as the believer in a thing as it is without the mode of necessity and inference is not knowing it even if he believes it as it is, and just as the believer in the existence of the description and judgment for a thing while ignorant of its cause by which it exists is not knowing it in reality; and this invalidates their attachment.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent argues that moral judgments (good and bad) are known before revelation, citing that many religious people know them before verifying revelation. The author replies that such belief is not genuine knowledge (ʿilm) but mere opinion (ẓann) and imitation (taqlīd). The author draws two analogies: (1) someone who believes a thing as it is without necessary or inferential knowledge is not truly knowledgeable; (2) someone who believes a description holds for a thing while ignorant of its cause is not truly knowledgeable. Thus, the opponent's claimed 'knowledge' is not real knowledge, so their objection fails.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simply believing correctly (e.g., that lying is bad) without proper reasoning constitutes knowledge.
Correction: Baqillani argues that correct belief without justification (from necessity, inference, or revelation) is not knowledge but opinion or imitation.

Checkpoint

A) They have knowledge
B) They have opinion and imitation

D) They are considered scholars

Key Takeaway

Refutation of the Parallelism Objection
Imam Baqillani refutes the parallelism objection using reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani refutes the objection that if moral values are known by revelation, then all knowledge would require revelation, and why his reductio ad absurdum argument is effective.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge comes from the same source; the objector's analogy is a false generalization.

Imam Baqillani now addresses a key objection from the Brahmins and Mu'tazila: if moral values are known only by revelation, then by analogy all knowledge would require revelation — which is absurd. To this, he responds not by defending the claim, but by exposing the logical flaw in the objection itself. In this passage, you will see how he uses a reductio argument to show that the objector's principle leads to unacceptable consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand what the Brahmins and Mu'tazila are claiming: they argue that if moral values are known only by revelation, then all knowledge (like the eternity of the eternal) must also come from revelation, which they find false.
This is a common objection based on analogical reasoning.
2
Note the author's initial response
Imam Baqillani first dismisses the objection as a mere claim without proof. He challenges the objector to provide evidence for their analogy.
This sets the stage for a deeper logical counter.
3
Examine the first counter-analogy
The author argues that if the objector's principle were applied to necessary vs. inferential knowledge, it would lead to absurd conclusions (e.g., all knowledge must be inferential or all must be necessary). Since not all knowledge is of one type, the principle is false.
This shows that different types of knowledge can have different sources.
4
Examine the second counter-analogy
Similarly, if testimonial knowledge were analogized, it would imply that all knowledge must come from testimony or none can. Since we have knowledge from both testimony and other sources, the objection fails.
The reductio is complete: the objector's analogy leads to contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال من الفريقين قائل أعني البراهمة والمعتزلة : لو كان قبح هذه الأمور وحسنها غير معلوم بالعقل بل بالسمع لوجب أن يكون العلم بقدم القديم وحدوث المحدّثِ وحقيقة الجوهر والعَرَض والعلم بكل معلوم غير مُدْرَك من ناحية العقل بل بحجة السمع فلما لم يجز ذلك بطل ما قلتم ، قيل لهم : لم قلتم هذا فلا يجدون في ذلك سوى الدعوى. ثم يقال لهم : ما الفصل بينكم وبين من زعم أنه لو جاز أو وجب أن يُعْلَمَ بعض المعلومات اضطراراً لا استدلالاً لجاز أو وجب أن يُعْلَمَ سائر المعلومات اضطراراً لا استدلالاً وكذلك لو جاز أن يُعْلَمَ بعض المعلومات نظراً واستدلالاً لا اضطراراً لوجب أن يُعْلَمَ سائر المعلومات نظراً واستدلالاً ، وكان يجب أن يكون العلم بسائر المشاهدات والمحسوسات علماً واقعاً عن نظر واستدلال وهذا جهل من راكبه. وكذلك يقال لهم : لو جاز أو وجب العلم ببعض هذه الأمور من ناحية الخبر كالعلم بالصين وخراسان والسير والممالك لجاز أن يكون سائر الأمور معلومة خبراً وإذا جاز أو وجب أن يُعْلَمَ بعض الأمور بغير خبرٍ استحال العلم بشيء من جهة الخبر أصلاً فإن لم يجب هذا أجمع لم يجب إذا علم بعض الأمور عقلاً أن يُعْلَمَ سائرها من هذه الجهة ولا إذا علم بعض الأمور اضطراراً وجب العلم بسائرها من هذه الطريقة
For if a speaker from the two parties – I mean the Brahmins and the Mu'tazila – says: If the badness and goodness of these things were not known by reason but by revelation, then knowledge of the eternity of the eternal and the creation of the created and the reality of substance and accident and knowledge of every known thing would have to be not attained from the side of reason but by a revealed proof. Since that is not permissible, what you have said is invalid. It is said to them: Why do you say this? They will find nothing in that except mere claim. Then it is said to them: What distinction is there between you and one who claims that if it is permissible or necessary that some known things be known necessarily not by inference, then it is permissible or necessary that all known things be known necessarily not by inference; and likewise if it is permissible that some known things be known by reflection and inference not necessarily, then it would be necessary that all known things be known by reflection and inference; and it would be necessary that knowledge of all observed and sensible things be knowledge occurring from reflection and inference – and this is ignorance on the part of its holder. And likewise it is said to them: If knowledge of some of these things by report is permissible or necessary, like knowledge of China, Khurasan, the biographies, and kingdoms, then it would be permissible that all things be known by report. And if it is permissible or necessary that some things be known without report, then it would be impossible to know anything by report at all. If all this is not necessary, then it is not necessary that if some things are known by reason, all of them are known from that side; nor if some things are known necessarily, all of them must be known in that way.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector (Brahmins and Mu'tazila) argues: if moral values are known only by revelation, then by analogy all rational knowledge (like the eternity of the eternal, creation of the created, substance, accident) would also require revelation, which is false; therefore the claim is false. The author responds by pointing out that the objector's reasoning is merely a claim without proof. Then the author offers a counter-argument: the objector's analogy is invalid because different types of knowledge can have different sources. For instance, some knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī) and some inferential (istidlālī); it does not follow that all knowledge must be of one type. Similarly, some knowledge is from testimony (khabar) and some from reason; it does not follow that all must be from one source. The author argues by reductio: if the objector's principle were applied universally, it would lead to absurd consequences (e.g., all sense-perception would require inference, or all testimony would be impossible). Therefore, the objector's argument fails.

Key Terms
براهمة
معتزلة
عقل
سمع
اضطرار
استدلال
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if some knowledge comes by revelation, then all knowledge must come by revelation.
Correction: Knowledge can come from different sources: necessary, inferential, testimonial, and rational. The objector's analogy fails because it assumes a uniformity that does not exist.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that if moral values are known by revelation, then all rational knowledge would be?
A) By arguing that moral values are not knowledge at all
B) By showing that the objector's analogy leads to absurd consequences
C) By claiming revelation is the only source of knowledge
D) By denying the objector's premise

Key Takeaway
The diversity of knowledge sources (necessary, inferential, testimonial) refutes the claim that if one thing is known by revelation then everything must be.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever assume that because you know something through one method (e.g., science), all knowledge must come from that method? Where might this false generalization be affecting your openness to revealed truths? What is one step you can take this week to recognize the diversity of knowledge sources?
?
When you encounter a theological objection that sounds logical, like the parallelism objection, how do you instinctively respond? Do you often accept analogies without examining their limits? This lesson shows the importance of scrutinizing analogies. Identify a current belief you hold that might be based on a false analogy. Will you examine it today?

Baqillani refutes the objector by pointing out that their claim is 'mere claim' without proof. Are there areas of your faith where you hold positions based on 'mere claim' rather than evidence? Commit to seeking out the rational or scriptural basis for one of your beliefs this week.

The Self-Defeating Claim: Are All Duties Known by Revelation Alone?
Imam al-Baqillani uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute the claim that all moral duties are known only by revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the Mu'tazila and Brahmins by showing that if all moral duties were known only by revelation, then even clearly rational duties would require revelation, thus exposing their inconsistency.

Core Idea
If every duty comes only by revelation, then reason has no role — and the opponents lose their own rational basis.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani targets a specific argument from the Mu'tazila and Brahmins who claim that all moral knowledge comes exclusively from revelation. He first lists numerous duties — like the specifics of prayer and zakat — that indeed are known only by revelation. Then he turns the argument against them: if that principle were universal, then even duties like gratitude to God and justice would also need revelation. But the Mu'tazila themselves affirm these are known by reason. The result is a powerful refutation that exposes the flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the passage and note what position the Imam is refuting. The opponents (Mu'tazila and Brahmins) assert that all moral duties are known only by revelation.
The author refers to them as 'حشو أصحاب هذه المقالة' (the hollow ones among the followers of this opinion).
2
List the duties known by revelation only
The Imam gives examples of duties whose obligation, goodness, or badness can only be known through revelation (سمع). Find these examples in the text. They include specifics of prayer, zakat, blood-money, kissing the black stone, etc.
These are duties that even the opponents would agree are revealed.
3
Understand the consequential argument
The Imam argues: if the opponents' principle were true, then duties like gratitude to God (شكر المنعم) and justice (العدل) would also need revelation. But the opponents believe these are known by reason. Therefore, they face a dilemma: either accept the consequence (contradicting themselves) or reject the principle (invalidating their argument).
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويخص حشو أصحاب هذه المقالة من أتباع المجوس البراهمة وهم المعتزلة إن استدلوا بهذه الدّلالة بأن يقال لهم : لو كان ما قلتموه صحيحاً لوجب، إذا كان العلم بوجوب بعض الواجبات وحسن بعض المحسنات وقبح بعض المقبّحات لا يُدْرَكُ وينال إلا سمعاً، نحو وجوب الصلاة وتقديرها والزَّكوات ونصابِها وحُسنِ إيجاب الدّية على العاقلة وتقبيل الحجر والسعي بين الصفا والمروة وقبح شرب الخمر والوطء بغير عقد ولا ملك يمين وقبح ترك الصلوات وما جرى مجرى ذلك مما لا سبيل إلى علم وجوبه وقبحه وحسنه من ناحية العقل أن يكون العلم بوجوب النظر عند الخاطر ووجوب المعرفة وحُسنِ العدل والإنصاف وقبح الظلم والعدوان ووجوب شكر المنعم وترك الكفر به مُدْرَكاً كالعلم بسائره من جهة السمع دون العقل فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا قولهم وإن أبوه أبطلوا استدلالهم
And it specifically targets the hollow ones among the followers of this opinion from the followers of the Magians, the Brahmins, and they are the Mu'tazila – if they use this evidence, it is said to them: If what you said were correct, then it would be necessary, since knowledge of the obligation of some obligations, the goodness of some good things, and the badness of some bad things is not attained and reached except by revelation – such as the obligation of prayer and its specific form, the zakats and their nisab, the goodness of imposing blood-money on the clan, kissing the black stone, and running between Safa and Marwa, and the badness of drinking wine and intercourse without a contract or right hand possession, and the badness of abandoning prayers, and what runs in that course, for which there is no way to know their obligation, badness, or goodness from the side of reason – that knowledge of the obligation of reflection at the moment of a thought, the obligation of knowledge, the goodness of justice and fairness, the badness of injustice and aggression, the obligation of thanking the Benefactor and abandoning disbelief in Him would be attained, like the knowledge of all that, from the side of revelation without reason. If they agree to that, they abandon their statement; and if they reject it, they invalidate their inference.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a specific refutation aimed at the Mu'tazila and Brahmins. The author first lists a series of duties whose obligation, goodness, or badness is known only by revelation (e.g., specifics of prayer, zakat, blood-money, rituals, prohibition of wine, etc.). Then the author argues: if the opponents' claim that moral values are only known by revelation were correct, then even clearly rational duties—such as the obligation to reflect (al-naẓar), the obligation of knowledge (al-maʿrifa), the goodness of justice and fairness, the badness of injustice, gratitude to the Benefactor, and avoiding disbelief—would also have to be known only by revelation. But the Mu'tazila themselves hold that these latter duties are known by reason. Therefore, if they accept this consequence, they contradict their own position; if they reject it, they invalidate their argument. Thus, the attack fails.

Refuting Objective Good and Evil from Truthfulness
Baqillani refutes the rationalist claim that reason judges good and evil based on the truthfulness example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani’s refutation of the rationalist claim that reason can judge good and evil, and why the truthfulness example fails to prove its case.

Core Idea
The rationalist argument assumes the very moral preference it tries to prove.

This passage from Baqillani’s Tamhid addresses a central debate in Islamic theology: can reason alone determine good and evil? The rationalists (Mu‘tazila and Brahmins) argue that our innate preference for truth over falsehood proves reason can judge. Baqillani counters by showing that this preference is not universal; it depends on prior belief, not reason. We will see how he deconstructs their proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the rationalist argument: a person must choose truth over falsehood to achieve a goal, implying truth is good and falsehood evil.
This is the proof claiming reason can judge.
2
Identify the refutation
Notice how Baqillani introduces a counterexample: a person without any belief in the superiority of truth is indifferent.
This shows the choice depends on belief, not reason.
3
Examine the analogies
Study the examples of two dirhams, speech/silence, and moving hands—all cases where equal utility removes preference.
These illustrate indifference when no prior value is ascribed.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Understand that the argument collapses because it assumes what it tries to prove: that truth is preferable.
The rationalist proof is circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال الفريقان ومن تابعهم: الدليل على أن قضايا العقول تُحَسِّنُ وتُقَبِّحُ علمنا بأن من أمكنه التوصل إلى غرضه بالصدق والكذب وجب عليه أن يتوصل إليه بالصدق دون الكذب وأنه لا يقع منه إلا ذلك؛ وليس يترك إلى الغرض في هذه الأمور بالكذب إلى الصدق إلا لِحُسْنِ الصدق وقبح الكذب؛ فوجب قضاء العقل على حُسْن الحَسَنِ وقبح القبيح، فيقال لهم: ما أنكرتم من أنه إن كان القاصد إلى التوصل إلى غرضه ممن لا يعتقد تفضيل الصدق على الكذب ولا هو بين قوم يعتقدون ذلك ولا يرون في الكذب عاراً ولا في الصدق مدحاً ولا تعظيماً ولا يفرقون في التفضيل بين الكذب والصدق ولا يدينون بذلك أنه مَخَيَّر في التوصل إلى غرضه بين الصدق والكذب. كما أن المعتقد للتوصل إلى غرضه بكل واحد من الدرهمين اللذين معه على وجه واحد وبالكلام والسكوت على حد غير مختلف وبحركة يمينه وشماله والدفع بهما من غير مزية تحصل في الدفع بإحداهما مخيّر بين إنفاق أي الدرهمين شاء وبين السكوت والكلام والتحريك باليمين والشمال إذ استوت هذه الحال عنده في ذلك واعتدلت في نفسه؛ وإذا كان ذلك كذلك، سقط ما اعتللتم به.
And if the two groups and their followers say: the proof that judgments of reason deem good and evil is our knowledge that whoever can achieve his purpose through truthfulness and falsehood must achieve it through truthfulness rather than falsehood, and that he only does that; and he does not abandon achieving his purpose through falsehood to truthfulness except because of the goodness of truthfulness and the evil of falsehood; therefore reason's judgment on the goodness of the good and the evil of the evil is necessary. It is said to them: what prevents you from [accepting] that if the one seeking to achieve his purpose is someone who does not believe in the superiority of truthfulness over falsehood, nor is he among a people who believe that, nor do they see any shame in falsehood nor any praise or veneration in truthfulness, nor do they distinguish in preference between falsehood and truthfulness, nor do they hold that as a religion, then he is free to choose between truthfulness and falsehood to achieve his purpose. Just as the one who believes that achieving his purpose can be done equally with either of two dirhams he has, and with speech and silence as equally effective, and with moving his right or left hand and pushing with them without any advantage in pushing with one over the other, he is free to spend whichever dirham he wishes, to remain silent or speak, and to move his right or left hand—since these situations are equal in his view and balanced in his mind. And if that is the case, your argument collapses.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and refutation. The rationalists (the 'two groups' likely referring to the Mu‘tazila and Brahmins) argue that reason judges good and evil. Their proof: a person who can achieve a goal by either truth or falsehood must choose truth; this shows truth is inherently good and falsehood evil. Baqillani refutes by pointing out that this choice only occurs if the person already believes truth is superior. If a person does not hold that belief, and lives in a society that does not value truth over falsehood, then he is indifferent and may choose either. Baqillani gives analogies: with two equally useful dirhams, or with equally effective speech and silence, or with either hand, a person is free to choose because there is no preference. The rationalists' argument thus assumes what it tries to prove—that truth is preferable—and fails to show that reason apprehends an objective moral quality. Their proof collapses when we consider scenarios where such beliefs are absent.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
عقل
صدق
كذب
اعتقاد
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani denies that truthfulness has any moral value.
Correction: He only denies that reason alone can judge good and evil without reliance on prior beliefs or revelation.

Checkpoint
What is the key flaw Baqillani identifies in the rationalist argument from the truthfulness example?
A) Truthfulness is not actually good.
B) The argument assumes without proof that truthfulness is better.
C) The argument applies only to religious matters.
D) The argument is too weak to show anything about reason.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that the rationalist argument for objective good and evil fails because it implicitly relies on a prior moral belief rather than pure reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose truth over falsehood in your daily life, do you do so because reason dictates it, or because you have been taught to value truth? What does this lesson reveal about the source of your own moral convictions?
?
If you were raised in a society that saw no shame in lying, would you still feel compelled to tell the truth? What does that imagined scenario expose about the foundation of your ethics?
?
Are there moments when you treat truth and falsehood as equally useful, such as in 'white lies' or social niceties? How does Baqillani's argument challenge you to examine whether your choices are based on objective moral standards or mere convenience?

Objection and Rebuttal on Choosing Truth
Objection that truth must be chosen for its goodness, with Baqillani's beginning reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationalist objection that truth must be chosen for its goodness and Baqillani's critique that this reasoning is circular.

Core Idea
Goodness cannot be proven by the obligation it supposedly justifies.

This passage presents an exchange between rationalists and Imam Baqillani. The rationalists insist that even if a person is indifferent, he must choose truth because truth is inherently good. Baqillani's reply exposes a logical flaw: they are using the very obligation they need to prove as evidence for the goodness of truth. This lesson examines both sides of the argument and the beginning of Baqillani's rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and rebuttal
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the structure: first the rationalist objection, then Baqillani's blunt response.
Notice the author uses 'قيل لهم' to signal his reply.
2
Identify the circular reasoning
Baqillani accuses them of making 'وجوب التوصل إلى الغرض بفعل الصدق دون الكذب' into an evidence. Understand why this is circular: they assume the obligation (choice of truth) to prove the goodness, but the obligation itself needs proof.
The Arabic ends abruptly; the full explanation likely continues on the next page.
3
Reflect on the methodological point
Baqillani calls their approach 'ignorance of discourse and departure from reflective consideration'. Consider why Kalam must avoid such circular arguments.
This illustrates a key methodological rule: no argument can take its conclusion as a premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا بعد هذا يجب على هذا الإنسان أن يختار الصدق على الكذب لحسنه، قيل لهم: ذلك جهل من الكلام وعدول عن النظر وذلك أنهم جعلوا وجوب التوصل إلى الغرض بفعل الصدق دون الكذب دلالةً
If they then say: after this, this person must choose truth over falsehood because of its goodness. It is said to them: that is ignorance of discourse and a departure from reflective consideration, and that is because they have made the obligation to reach the goal by performing truth rather than falsehood an evidence
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is an objection and the beginning of Baqillani's answer. The rationalists respond to the previous refutation by insisting that even if the person is indifferent, he still ought to choose truth because truth is good—objectively. Baqillani's initial reply is blunt: this is ignorance and a departure from proper reasoning. He begins to explain why: the opponents have taken the very thing that needs to be proven (the obligation to choose truth) and made it into evidence for the goodness of truth. That is circular reasoning, though the sentence is cut off. The argument likely continues to show that they are using the obligation to choose truth (the judgment) as a proof for the goodness (the attribute), which is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's initial reply shows that the rationalist argument is circular: it uses the obligation to choose truth as evidence for the very goodness that is supposed to justify the obligation.

The rationalists in this passage assumed that 'goodness' justifies obligation, but they used obligation to prove goodness. In your daily life, do you ever uncritically accept that something is 'good' simply because you feel you 'should' do it?
