The Circularity of Reason-Based Morality
Baqillani presents a logical argument against the Brahmana's rationalist ethics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Baqillani argues that knowing moral obligations through reason alone leads to a vicious circle, making it impossible to establish even basic ethical truths.

Core Idea
Moral knowledge cannot be derived from reason because it leads to circular dependency.

Imam al-Baqillani begins his refutation of the Brahmana by exposing a logical flaw in their position. They claim that the goodness of an action indicates its obligation, but this creates a paradox: each depends on the other for proof. This lesson walks through that argument, showing why reason alone cannot ground moral knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the analogy about Zayd and Amr.
2
Identify the circular reasoning
Notice how al-Baqillani shows that knowing obligation requires knowing goodness, and knowing goodness requires knowing obligation—a vicious circle.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the consequence
If both conditions are mutually dependent, neither can be known. This proves that reason cannot reach moral judgments.
Al-Baqillani concludes that revelation is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما أبطلنا ذلك عليهم رجعوا يجعلون الدلالة على وجوب فعل الصدق دون الكذب حُسْنه وهذا يُؤدِّي إلى أن لا يَثْبُتَ حُسْنُ الصدق ولا وجوب فعله وذلك أنا إذا لم نعلم وجوب فعل الصدق إلا إذا علمنا حُسْنَه، ولم نعلم حسنه إلا إذا علمنا وجوبه لم يكن لنا طريق إلى العلم بوجوبه ولا بحسنه كما أن قائلاً لو قال: إني لا أعلم أن زيداً في الدار أعلم أن عمراً فيها ولا أعلم أن عمراً فيها حتى أعلم أن زيداً فيها لم يجز أن يَعْلَمَ أن زيداً في الدار ولا عمراً لأنه قد جَعَلَ شَرْطَ علمه بالشيء شرطاً لما هو شرط له وذلك ما يُحِيلُ وقوع كل واحد من المشروطين وإذا كان ذلك كذلك ثبت بهذه الجملة أن العلم بوجوب الأفعال وحظرها وإباحتها غير مدرك بقضايا العقول وثبت أنه لا بد من سمع يكشف عما ينال به الثواب والعقاب ويُحَضِّر الله تعالى به الجهل بوجوده وترك النظر فيما يؤدي إلى معرفته على من كلفه ذلك من خلقه وهذا أعظم الأمور وأجسمها خطراً؛ وهذا غير مُدْرَك علمه من جهة العقول فبطل قول البراهمة إن العقل يُستغنى به في إدراك جميع المراشد والمصالح. ثم يقال لهم: خبرونا من أين عَرَفَتْ العقلاء الأغذية من الأدوية والسموم القاتلة الوَحِيَّة منها وغير الوَحِيَّة؟ وإنما هجموا على العالم بَغْتَةً وليس في دلائل عقولهم ما يعرفون به الأغذية والأدوية والسموم القاتلة ولا مشاهداتهم وسائر حواسهم ما يدل على ذلك أو يحس به معرفة ما يحتاج إليه من هذا الباب، ولا هو مما يُعرف باضطرار. فإن قالوا: إنما أدرك الناس ذلك قديماً وعرفوه بالامتحان والتجربة على أجسامهم وأجسام أمثالهم من نسل آدم عليه السلام قيل لهم: فهذا مُخْرِجٌ للقديم عن الحكمة لأنه قد كان قادراً عندكم وعندنا أن يُعَرِّفهم السمومات ويوقفهم على الاغتذاء بما فيه صلاح أجسامهم والأدوية التي عند تناولها تزول أمراضهم وأسقامهم فيغنيهم ذلك عن إتلاف أنفسهم وأمثالهم وذَهَابِ كثير منهم بالامتحان وطول التجربة وليس بحكيم عندكم من قدر أن يوقف أولاده وضَعَفَته ومن يحب مصلحته على تجنب ما فيه هَلَكَتُه وتناول ما فيه سلامته وبقاء مُهْجَتِه فلم يفعل وأحالهم على التجربة والامتحان الذي فيه عَطَبُ البعض منهم والبوار وهذا ما لا حيلة لهم فيه. فإن قالوا: إنما أُدْرِكَ علم ذلك بالامتحان على أجسام غير الناس من الحيوان نحو الذئاب والكلاب وأجناس الطير وغيرهم من الحيوان قيل لهم فالمسألة بحالها لأن إتلاف جميع الحيوان عندكم قبيح؛ فإذا أباحكم الله إتلاف بعضه بالتجربة والمحنة وهو قادر على توقيفكم على ما يغني عن إتلاف الحيوان فقد سفة على أوضاعكم وخرج
When we refuted that upon them, they returned to making the indication of the obligation of truthfulness without lying its goodness, and this leads to the non-establishment of the goodness of truthfulness nor the obligation of its action. And that is because if we do not know the obligation of truthfulness except when we know its goodness, and we do not know its goodness except when we know its obligation, we have no way to the knowledge of its obligation nor its goodness, just as if someone said: I do not know that Zayd is in the house until I know that Amr is in it, and I do not know that Amr is in it until I know that Zayd is in it, it is not possible that he knows that Zayd is in the house nor Amr, because he has made the condition of his knowledge of a thing a condition of that which is a condition for it, and that renders impossible the occurrence of each one of the two conditionals. And when that is so, it is established by this summary that knowledge of the obligation of actions, their prohibition, and their permission is not attained by the judgments of intellects, and it is established that there is no alternative to a revelation (sam') that discloses what reward and punishment are attained by, and God—exalted—warns by it of ignorance of His existence and of abandoning reflection on what leads to knowledge of Him upon whomever He has obligated that among His creation. This is the greatest of matters and the most enormous in danger; and this knowledge is not attained by way of intellects. So the statement of the Brahmana (al-Barahima) is invalid: that the intellect is sufficient for attaining all guidance and benefits. Then it is said to them: Tell us, from where did the intelligent people know foods from medicines and deadly poisons—the swift ones and the non-swift? They plunged into the world suddenly, and there is not in the evidences of their intellects that by which they know foods, medicines, and deadly poisons, nor do their observations and all their senses indicate that or perceive by it the knowledge of what is needed from this category, nor is it known by necessity. If they say: People knew that in the past and knew it by testing and experience on their bodies and the bodies of their likes from the progeny of Adam, peace be upon him, it is said to them: This removes the Ancient from wisdom, because He—according to you and us—was able to make them know the poisons and stop them at nourishment that contains the well-being of their bodies and medicines by whose consumption their illnesses and sicknesses disappear, thus making them independent of destroying themselves and their likes and the going of many of them by testing and long experience. And he is not considered wise among you who, being able to make his children, weak ones, and those whose benefit he loves avoid what causes their destruction and consume what contains their safety and the preservation of their life, does not do so but refers them to testing and experience which contains harm and ruin for some of them—and this is something for which they have no stratagem. If they say: That knowledge was attained by testing on the bodies of non-humans from animals, such as wolves, dogs, kinds of birds, and other animals, it is said to them: The question remains the same, because destroying all animals is ugly according to you. So if God permits you to destroy some of them by testing and trial while He is able to make you stop at what suffices from destroying animals, then your own systems become foolish and out [of wisdom].
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Brahmana (a group of Indian rationalists) who claim that reason alone is sufficient for attaining all moral and practical knowledge. The author, Imam al-Baqillani, presents a two-part argument: first, a logical proof that moral obligations cannot be known by reason because it leads to circularity; second, a practical argument that even mundane knowledge like distinguishing food from poison requires experience and divine guidance.

The argument proceeds as follows: The Brahmana initially argued that the goodness of an action (like truthfulness) indicates its obligation. But al-Baqillani shows this leads to a vicious circle: we cannot know the obligation of truthfulness without knowing its goodness, and we cannot know its goodness without knowing its obligation. This circularity makes both impossible to know through reason alone. He illustrates with an analogy: if someone says he cannot know Zayd is in the house without knowing Amr is there, and cannot know Amr is there without knowing Zayd is there, then he can never know either. Therefore, knowledge of moral categories (obligation, prohibition, permission) cannot be derived from rational judgments; it requires divine revelation (sam'). This revelation also provides knowledge of God and the consequences of actions.

Having established that reason fails for moral knowledge, al-Baqillani then challenges the Brahmana on practical knowledge. He asks how humans originally knew which substances are food, medicine, or poison. They did not have innate rational knowledge of this; they had to learn by experience. But this casts doubt on divine wisdom: if God could have directly taught humans these distinctions, why allow them to suffer and die through trial and error? This would be inconsistent with the wisdom attributed to God. The Brahmana might counter that they learned from animals, but al-Baqillani responds that killing animals for experimentation is also morally problematic (ugly) according to their own principles. So the argument concludes that even practical knowledge ultimately points to the necessity of divine guidance.

Key Terms
البراهمة
وجوب الأفعال
حُسْن
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the logical consequence al-Baqillani draws from the circular dependency between knowing an action's goodness and its obligation?
A) Both become known through intuition.
B) Neither can be known through reason alone.
C) The goodness must be known first, then obligation.
D) The obligation must be known first, then goodness.

Key Takeaway
Reason alone cannot establish moral obligations because it falls into a vicious circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you relied on your own reasoning to justify an action, only to later realize it contradicted a clear divine command? How did you react, and what did you learn about the limits of your own intellect?
?
In what area of your life are you currently trusting your sense of 'goodness' over the revealed text? What step will you take this week to submit that area to revelation, even if your reasoning resists?
?
If reason alone cannot establish moral obligations, then your moral framework must be anchored in revelation. Are there moral beliefs you hold that have no scriptural basis? How will you reconcile that with Baqillani's argument?

Revelation Needed for Practical Knowledge
Baqillani presents a practical proof for the need of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's argument that even practical knowledge (like distinguishing food from poison) requires divine revelation or guidance, not just reason or experience.

Core Idea
Practical necessities like knowing food from poison show the need for divine guidance.

Having established that reason fails for moral knowledge, al-Baqillani now challenges the Brahmana on practical knowledge. He asks how humans originally learned to distinguish food from poison. Their answer—trial and error—raises problems of divine wisdom and morality. This lesson examines that exchange and its implications.

If they say: People knew that in the past and knew it by testing and experience on their bodies and the bodies of their likes from the progeny of Adam, peace be upon him, it is said to them: This removes the Ancient from wisdom, because He—according to you and us—was able to make them know the poisons and stop them at nourishment that contains the well-being of their bodies and medicines by whose consumption their illnesses and sicknesses disappear, thus making them independent of destroying themselves and their likes and the going of many of them by testing and long experience. And he is not considered wise among you who, being able to make his children, weak ones, and those whose benefit he loves avoid what causes their destruction and consume what contains their safety and the preservation of their life, does not do so but refers them to testing and experience which contains harm and ruin for some of them—and this is something for which they have no stratagem. If they say: That knowledge was attained by testing on the bodies of non-humans from animals, such as wolves, dogs, kinds of birds, and other animals, it is said to them: The question remains the same, because destroying all animals is ugly according to you. So if God permits you to destroy some of them by testing and trial while He is able to make you stop at what suffices from destroying animals, then your own systems become foolish and out [of wisdom].
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Brahmana (a group of Indian rationalists) who claim that reason alone is sufficient for attaining all moral and practical knowledge. The author, Imam al-Baqillani, presents a two-part argument: first, a logical proof that moral obligations cannot be known by reason because it leads to circularity; second, a practical argument that even mundane knowledge like distinguishing food from poison requires experience and divine guidance.

The argument proceeds as follows: The Brahmana initially argued that the goodness of an action (like truthfulness) indicates its obligation. But al-Baqillani shows this leads to a vicious circle: we cannot know the obligation of truthfulness without knowing its goodness, and we cannot know its goodness without knowing its obligation. This circularity makes both impossible to know through reason alone. He illustrates with an analogy: if someone says he cannot know Zayd is in the house without knowing Amr is there, and cannot know Amr is there without knowing Zayd is there, then he can never know either. Therefore, knowledge of moral categories (obligation, prohibition, permission) cannot be derived from rational judgments; it requires divine revelation (sam'). This revelation also provides knowledge of God and the consequences of actions.

Having established that reason fails for moral knowledge, al-Baqillani then challenges the Brahmana on practical knowledge. He asks how humans originally knew which substances are food, medicine, or poison. They did not have innate rational knowledge of this; they had to learn by experience. But this casts doubt on divine wisdom: if God could have directly taught humans these distinctions, why allow them to suffer and die through trial and error? This would be inconsistent with the wisdom attributed to God. The Brahmana might counter that they learned from animals, but al-Baqillani responds that killing animals for experimentation is also morally problematic (ugly) according to their own principles. So the argument concludes that even practical knowledge ultimately points to the necessity of divine guidance.

Key Terms
سمع
المراشد والمصالح
السموم
الامتحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Brahmana believe that reason and experience are sufficient for all practical and moral knowledge.
Correction: Al-Baqillani shows that even practical knowledge like identifying poison requires divine guidance, because trial and error contradicts divine wisdom and morality.

Checkpoint
What is the first reply al-Baqillani addresses from the Brahmana regarding how humans learned to distinguish food from poison?
A) They used logical deduction.
B) They learned from previous prophets.
C) They learned through testing on their own bodies.
D) They observed animals.

Key Takeaway
Even practical knowledge like distinguishing food from poison shows that a wise God would provide revelation, not leave humans to learn through trial and error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Imagine you knew a truth that could save someone from harm, but you chose to let them figure it out through painful trial and error. Would you consider yourself wise? How does this relate to God's wisdom in sending revelation?
?
When you face a difficult decision, do you instinctively turn to your own reasoning, books, or people before turning to God's book and the teachings of the Prophet? What would it take for you to change that priority starting today?

Refuting the Objection of Contemporary Denial Against Ahad Reports
Baqillani refutes an objection against ahad reports using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's refutation of the claim that denial by contemporaries falsifies ahad reports of miracles.

Core Idea
Denial by witnesses does not automatically falsify a report; the same logic would undermine established miracles.

Baqillani addresses a key objection against the reliability of individual reports (khabar al-āḥād) concerning miracles. The objector argues that if the miracles were true, contemporary non-Muslims would not have denied them. Baqillani dismantles this objection by showing its logical consequences would also negate well-known miracles of earlier prophets, which is unacceptable. This passage sharpens your understanding of how evidence from ahad reports is defended in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection as presented by Baqillani. Understand the claim: that denial by those who lived with the Prophet proves ahad reports are false.
Notice the objector uses the silence of groups (jamāʿāt) as a premise.
2
Identify Baqillani's first argument
Baqillani argues that if silence indicates falsehood, then active testimony would also be vulnerable to the same logic. This parity refutes the objection's foundation.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Study the second argument
Baqillani extends the logic to Moses and Jesus: denial by their contemporaries would then falsify their miracles, which is absurd. This shows the objection is inconsistent.
The examples come from earlier prophets and different sects.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that denial itself is not evidence of falsehood. Baqillani establishes that ahad reports can be valid even when some contemporaries deny them.
This reinforces the epistemological value of ahad reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال آخر على ما قدمناه
فإن قال قائل : فما أنكرتم أن يكون ما نقله الآحاد كَذِباً وإن سلَّمَت الجماعات نقلهم بدلالة إنكار اليهود والنصارى والمجوس الذين عاصروا محمداً صلى الله عليه وسلم وجحـدهـم لها وقولهم : قد رأيناه وعاصرناه فما ظهر على يده مما نقله الآحاد من متبعيه شيء ولو كان ذلك صحيحاً لم يتهيأ لهم جحده وإنكاره فدل ذلك على بطلانه؟ يقال لهم : هذا باطل من وجهين: أحدهما أن نقول : لو دل إنكار من ذكرتم على كذب نقل الآحاد مع إمساك الجماعات عن ردّه لدل ذلك أيضاً على كذب النقل ولو شهدت الجماعات بصحته بدلاً من سكتها عليه إذ لا فرق بين إمساكها عن إنكاره وإنكار ما ادعي عليها وبين تصويبها له على ما بيناه ولو كان ذلك كذلك لدلّ إنكار البراهمة والمجوس وأهل التثنية والإلحاد والتنجيم والطبائعيين لأعلام موسى وعيسى وقول من شاهدهما وعاصرهما من هذه الفرق : إنّا قد شاهدنا هذين الرجلين وعاصر ناهما فلم نَرَ مما ادعِي لهما من هذه الأمور التي هي فلق البحر وإخراج اليد بيضاء وإحياء ميت وإبراء أكمة وأبرصَ وزمن ومن مشي على الماء وغير ذلك على بطلان ما ادعِي ونقل لهما فلما لم يكن هذا عندنا وعندهم كذلك لم يكن في إنكار الفِرَق لِمَا قام الدليل على صحته وثبوته دليل على بطلانه وكذب ناقليه
Another objection to what we have presented: If a questioner says: 'Why do you not accept that what the individuals (al-āḥād) transmitted is a lie, even if the groups (al-jamāʿāt) conceded their transmission, based on the evidence of the denial of the Jews, Christians, and Magians who were contemporaries of Muḥammad (peace be upon him) and their rejection of it, and their saying: We saw him and were his contemporaries, and nothing of what his followers transmitted as individuals appeared from him; and if that were true, it would not have been possible for them to reject and deny it, so that indicates its falsehood?' It is said to them: This is invalid from two aspects: The first is that we say: If the denial of those you mentioned indicated the falsehood of the transmission of individuals (khabar al-āḥād) while the groups refrained from rejecting it, then it would also indicate the falsehood of the transmission even if the groups had testified to its correctness instead of remaining silent, for there is no difference between their refraining from rejecting it and their denying what was claimed against them, and between their approving it, according to what we have explained. And if that were so, then the denial of the Brahmins, Magians, Dualists, Atheists, Astrologers, and Naturalists of the miracles of Moses and Jesus, and the saying of those who saw them and were their contemporaries from these sects: 'We have seen these two men and were their contemporaries, and we did not see any of these things claimed for them, such as the splitting of the sea, the whitening of the hand, reviving the dead, healing the blind and leprous, and walking on water, etc.' would indicate the falsehood of what was claimed and transmitted for them. Since this is not the case with us or with them, the denial of the sects of what proof has established its correctness and existence is not evidence for its falsehood or the falsehood of its transmitters.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the reliability of reports transmitted by individuals (khabar al-āḥād) concerning miracles. The objector argues that the denial of miracles by contemporary non-Muslims—Jews, Christians, and Magians who lived during the Prophet's time—proves that the aḥād reports are false, because if the miracles had really occurred, these witnesses would not have denied them. Baqillani answers by stating that this objection is invalid on two counts. First, he uses a parity argument: if the silence of groups (jamāʿāt) regarding denial indicates falsehood, then even if the groups had actively testified to the truth, the same logic would be applied—thus the objection is self-defeating. Second, he extends the argument to earlier prophets: the denial by Brahmins, Magians, and others of the well-known miracles of Moses and Jesus (like the splitting of the sea) does not prove those miracles false, since they are established by proof. Therefore, the denial of sects does not constitute evidence against the truth of aḥād reports or the veracity of their transmitters.

Key Terms
الآحاد (al-āḥād)
الجماعات (al-jamāʿāt)
الخبر (khabar)
الإمساك (imsāk)
الإنكار (inkār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denial by contemporaries is sufficient evidence to reject a report.
Correction: Baqillani shows that denial does not falsify a report; the same logic would falsify established miracles, so denial alone is not decisive.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why does the denial of contemporary non-Muslims not prove that the ahad reports of miracles are false?
A) Because the non-Muslims were biased and could not be trusted.
B) Because the same logic would imply that denial of earlier prophets' miracles by other sects would also prove those miracles false, which is absurd.
C) Because the ahad reports are not important for establishing miracles.
D) Because the non-Muslims never actually saw the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Denial by contemporaries does not falsify ahad reports; Baqillani's arguments show such denial is irrelevant when proof supports the report.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When has someone's denial of your sincere testimony made you question its truth? How can the logic in this passage reassure you that proof, not popularity, determines reality?
?

The Meaning of 'Body' Cannot Be Stretched to God
Baqillani refutes interpreting 'body' as any attribute other than composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects applying the term 'body' to God even when 'body' is reinterpreted as self-subsistence, thinghood, attribute-bearing, or independence, because the core meaning of 'body' is composition.

Core Idea
'Body' linguistically means composition, not any other attribute.

This passage presents a third objection against Baqillani's denial that God is a body. The objector suggests that 'body' could simply mean self-subsistent or thing, which are attributes of God. Baqillani's response is careful: he concedes the attributes but denies the name, because the term 'body' in Arabic has a specific meaning—composition. This distinction between name (ism) and meaning (ma'na) is central to kalam methodology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's structure
Read the opening sentence carefully: 'If a speaker says... it is said to him.' Notice how the objector proposes four alternative interpretations of 'body': self-subsistent, thing, bearer of attributes, or independent in existence.
The objector is trying to make 'body' a generic term for any positive attribute.
2
Identify Baqillani's concession and denial
Baqillani first concedes that God possesses all these judgments and descriptions. Then he states the real issue: 'We deny your naming of the one who possesses them as 'body' while not being composed.' This is the key move.
He does not deny the attributes; he denies the label.
3
Examine the definitions of key terms
Baqillani then defines 'body' (composed), 'thing' (established existent), 'substance' (individual part), 'accident' (subsisting by substance), and 'self-subsistent' (not needing something to exist). Notice that each term has a distinct meaning.
These definitions are standard in kalam.
4
Trace the conclusion
The passage ends: 'This is not in the way of the meaning of our saying 'body' and 'composed'. So what you said is invalidated.' Understand why the objection fails: because the literal meaning of 'body' is composition, and God is not composed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : ما أنكرتم أن يكون جسماً على معنى أنه قائم بنفسه أو بمعنى أنه شيء أو بمعنى أنه حامل للصفات أو بمعنى أنه غير محتاج في الوجود إلى شيء يقوم به؟ قيل له: لا ننكر أن يكون الباري سبحانه حاصلاً على جميع هذه الأحكام والأوصاف وإنما ننكر تسميتكم لمن حَصَلَتْ له بأنه جسم وإن لم يكن مُؤلَّفاً فهذا عندنا في التسمية دون المعنى لأن معنى الجسم أنه المُؤلَّف على ما بيناه ومعنى الشيء أنه الثابت الموجود وقد يكون جسماً إذا كان مؤلفاً ويكون جوهراً إذا كان جزءاً منفرداً ويكون عَرَضاً إذا كان مما يقوم بالجوهر ومعنى القائم بنفسه هو أنه غير محتاج في الوجود إلى شيء يُوجَدُ به ومعنى ذلك أنه مما له الوجود وإن لم يفعل صانعه شيئاً غيره إذا كان محدثاً ويصح وجوده وإن لم يوجد قائم بنفسه سواه إذا كان قديماً وليس هذا من معنى قولنا «جسم» و «مُؤلَّفٌ» بسَبيل فبطل ما قلتم
If a speaker says: 'What do you deny that He is a body in the sense that He is self-subsistent, or in the sense that He is a thing, or in the sense that He is a bearer of attributes, or in the sense that He is not in need in existence of something that subsists by means of Him?' It is said to him: We do not deny that the Creator, exalted, possesses all these judgments and descriptions. Rather, we deny your naming of the one who possesses them as 'body' while not being composed. This, with us, is in the name, not the meaning, because the meaning of 'body' is the composed, as we have explained. And the meaning of 'thing' is the established existent. And it may be a body if it is composed, and a substance if it is an individual part, and an accident if it is something that subsists by the substance. And the meaning of 'self-subsistent' is that it is not in need in existence of something by which it exists. And the meaning of that is that it is something that has existence even if its maker did not make anything other than it, if it is originated. And its existence is sound even if no other self-subsistent exists besides it, if it is eternal. And this is not in the way of the meaning of our saying 'body' and 'composed'. So what you said is invalidated.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This is a third objection-and-answer. The objector suggests that 'body' could mean self-subsistent, thing, bearer of attributes, or independent in existence. The answer concedes that God possesses these attributes but denies that this makes Him a 'body', because 'body' has the specific meaning of composition. The author then defines 'body', 'thing', 'substance', 'accident', and 'self-subsistent', showing that they are distinct concepts. Thus the term 'body' cannot be applied to God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some might think that since 'body' can be defined as 'self-subsistent', it is permissible to call God a body.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that the linguistic meaning of 'body' is composition, not self-subsistence. Applying the term to God with a different meaning is invalid because words have fixed meanings.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani reject calling God a 'body' even if 'body' is interpreted as 'self-subsistent'?

C) Because the term 'self-subsistent' is also prohibited
D) Because God is not self-subsistent

Key Takeaway
The term 'body' in Arabic has a fixed meaning of composition, and applying it to God would imply composition, which is impossible for God.

Rational Proof That Attributes Are Not Speech
Baqillani proves that attributes cannot be identical to speech by reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's rational proof that attributes are real qualities inhering in the essence, not merely statements about the subject.

Core Idea
Attributes are real, not words—contradictory speech can't make a thing contradictory.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani uses a logical reductio to refute the claim that an attribute (ṣifa) is identical to the act of describing (waṣf) or the spoken statement (qawl). He shows that if attributes were only speech, then contradictory descriptions from different speakers would force the subject to possess contradictory attributes at the same time—an impossibility. Read on to see how he establishes that attributes are real entities that exist in the essence of the described.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's logical rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani uses a conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the reductio structure
The proof assumes the opposite (attribute equals speech), derives a contradiction (Zayd would be both living and dead), and concludes the assumption is false.
This is a classic argument form in kalām.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Baqillani ends by comparing attributes to motion and color—they are real qualities in the object, not statements about it. Consider why this analogy reinforces his point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على ذلك أيضاً من جهة المعنى أنه لو كانت الصفة في الحقيقة ليست بمعنى أكثر من وصف الواصف وقوله: «إن زيداً عالم قادر حي»، لوجب أن يكون لزيد في الحقيقة في حالة واحدة صفتان متضادّتان. ولوجب أن يكون حيّاً ميتاً وقادراً عاجزاً وعالماً جاهلاً، إذا قال بعض الواصفين له إنه حي، وقال آخر إنه ميت، وقال قائل: إنه قادر عالم، وقال آخر: إنه عاجز جاهل. فيجب أن لا يكون العلم من صفته أولى من الجهل لأنه قد وجدت له الصفتان؛ فيجب أن يكون زيد عالماً جاهلاً وحيّاً ميتاً لوجود صفتيه اللتين هما القول. فلما لم يجز ذلك، وكان العالم في الحقيقة هو من وجد به العلم ولوجوده به صار عالماً، بطل أن تكون الصفة هي القول، وثبت أنها ما يوجد بذات الموصوف، كما أن الحركة واللون هما ما وجدا بذات المتحرك والمتلوّن دون القول «إنه متحرك متلوّن».
And among what also indicates that from the aspect of meaning is that if the attribute in reality were not more than the description of the describer and his statement: 'Indeed Zayd is knowing, powerful, living,' then it would be necessary that Zayd in reality in one state have two contradictory attributes. And it would be necessary that he be living and dead, powerful and incapable, knowing and ignorant, if some describers say he is living, and another says he is dead, and one says he is powerful and knowing, and another says he is incapable and ignorant. Thus knowledge would not be more deserving of being his attribute than ignorance, because both attributes [i.e., the statements] have been found for him; so it would be necessary that Zayd be knowing and ignorant, living and dead, due to the existence of his two attributes which are the speech. Since that is not permissible, and the knower in reality is the one in whom knowledge exists and through its existence he becomes knowing, it is invalid that the attribute be speech, and it is established that it is what exists in the essence of the described, just as motion and color are what exist in the essence of the moving and colored thing, not the statement 'he is moving, colored.'
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī) refuting the claim that attributes (ṣifa) are identical to speech (qawl) or description (waṣf). The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if an attribute were merely the describer's statement, then contradictory descriptions from different speakers would force the subject to possess contradictory attributes simultaneously (e.g., living and dead). Since this is impossible, the attribute cannot be the statement; rather, it is a real quality inhering in the essence (dhāt) of the described, like motion in a moving body. The proof is structured as a conditional: if A (attribute = speech), then B (contradictory attributes), but B is impossible, so not-A. The conclusion is that attributes exist in the subject independently of anyone's speech.

Key Terms
الصفة
الوصف
القول
الذات
المتناقضتان
الحركة
اللون
العالم
القادر
الحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Baqillani's argument denies the reality of attributes altogether or reduces them to mere mental concepts.
Correction: The argument only denies that attributes are identical to speech; it affirms that they are real qualities that exist in the essence of the described, like motion and color.

Checkpoint
What is the central rationale in Baqillani's proof that attributes cannot be speech?
A) Speech is created, while attributes are eternal.
B) If attributes were speech, contradictory statements would entail contradictory attributes in one subject.
C) The Qur'an uses the word 'sifa' only for God's attributes.
D) Linguistic evidence proves 'sifa' and 'wasf' are different.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's proof demonstrates that attributes are real qualities existing in the essence, not merely statements, using the impossibility of contradictory attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof shows that an attribute is what exists in the essence, not what is merely said. Is your knowledge of God an authentic part of who you are, or is it merely a description you recite? What concrete step can you take today to make your faith a real quality in your heart, not just a word on your lips?

The Opponents' Claim: Finitude of Events and Denial of Pleasure
Reports the opponents' claim that events are finite and that beings lack movement and pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the heretical claim that temporal events are finite and that beings in the afterlife lack movement and pleasure, and why this claim is problematic for orthodox theology.

Core Idea
The opponents argue that because events have a beginning, they must also have an end — denying eternal reward.

This lesson presents a reported statement from a heretical group. They claim that all occurrences are finite — they have a starting point and an ending point. From this, they conclude that creatures in the afterlife cannot experience pleasure or movement, and that even Allah has no power over such states. Understanding this claim is essential for recognizing the stakes in classical theological debates about divine power and the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is fragmentary — it may be continuing from a previous page.
2
Identify the two parts of the claim
The passage contains two related claims: one about the state of bodies in the afterlife (no movement, pleasure, or power) and another about the finitude of events.
Notice the conjunction 'because' (لأن) linking the two.
3
Analyze the argument from finitude
The opponents argue that just as events must have a first term from which they begin, they must have a last term at which they end. This is an analogy.
Ask yourself: Is this conclusion logically necessary?
4
Reflect on why this is problematic
This claim denies the possibility of eternal reward or punishment. Orthodox theologians affirm that Allah can sustain pleasure and movement eternally.
The phrase 'nor their Lord' suggests the opponents even limit Allah's power — a major theological error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحركة، ولا يلتذون بلذة، ولا يقدرون ولا ربهم على شيء من ذلك . لأن الحوادث زعم كما لا بد لها من أوّل تنتهي إليه لم يكن قبله شيء، فكذلك لا بد لها من آخر تنتهي إليه لا يكون بعده شيء.
Without movement, and they do not enjoy pleasure, and they are not capable, nor their Lord [is able to do] anything of that. Because occurrences, as they claim, just as they must have a first [term] that they end at, before which there was nothing, so likewise they must have a last [term] that they end at, after which there is nothing.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a reported statement of a heretical view. The author quotes the opponents as making two claims: (1) that beings in the afterlife (presumably the denizens of Paradise or Hell) are without movement, do not experience pleasure, and are powerless, and that even their Lord (Allah) has no power over such things; (2) that temporal events (al-ḥawādith) are necessarily finite—they have a starting point before which nothing existed, and an ending point after which nothing exists. The grammar suggests the opponents are using the finitude of events to argue for the impossibility of eternal reward or punishment. The phrase 'wa lā rabbuhum' (nor their Lord) is problematic; the author may be misrepresenting the opponents as denying Allah's power over these states.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All temporal events necessarily have an end because they have a beginning.
Correction: While events may have a beginning, it does not logically follow that they must have an end; the afterlife is eternal by divine will.

Checkpoint
What do the opponents claim about the 'last term' of occurrences?
A) It is eternal
B) After it there is something
C) After it there is nothing
D) It does not exist

Key Takeaway
The opponents' argument from the finitude of events to the denial of eternal pleasure and power is a key heresy that orthodox theologians refute by affirming the possibility of eternal reward and Allah's unrestricted power.

?
You have read a heresy that denies eternal pleasure and divine power over it. Does your heart truly long for an eternal pleasure that only Allah can give, or have you settled for temporary satisfactions? What would it take for you to prioritize that eternal hope over immediate gratification?

Refutation of Rational Objection Against Seeing God
Baqillani refutes the rational objection by asserting that intellects permit seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the rational objection that reason disproves the possibility of seeing God, by affirming that reason actually permits it.

Core Idea
Reason does not disprove seeing God; it permits it.

In this lesson, we encounter a classic objection from rationalist theologians: they claim that reason itself proves the impossibility of seeing God. Baqillani responds by flipping the argument on its head, asserting that in the Ash'ari view, reason actually permits vision of God and invalidates the opponents' claim. This short but dense exchange sets the stage for a deeper discussion of the rational and revealed evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the direct style of the objection and the immediate response.
Pay attention to the key terms: إحالة العقول and تُجيز.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify what the objectors claim (that reason proves impossibility) and what Baqillani asserts (that reason permits vision and invalidates their claim).
The word تبطل (invalidates) is crucial.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider why this exchange matters: it shows that reason alone does not settle the issue; revelation and further argument are needed.
This connects to the Ash'ari principle that reason can permit but not require or negate certain divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم أن تكون الحجّة في ذلك إحالة العقول لرؤيته؟ قيل لهم : العقول عندنا تُجيز رؤيته وتبطل دعواكم، وقد بينا ذلك فيما سلف، وسنفسد ما تتعلقون به فيما بعد إن شاء الله.
For they said: What prevents you from [saying] that the proof in that is the impossibility [as judged] by intellects of seeing Him? It was said to them: Intellects in our view permit seeing Him and invalidate your claim, and we have demonstrated that previously, and we will nullify what you cling to afterwards, God willing.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the rational evidence. The objectors argue that reason (العقول) declares the impossibility (إحالة) of seeing God, and this is the proof that vision of God is impossible. The author responds by stating that, in the Ash'ari view, intellects actually permit (تُجيز) seeing God, and in fact they invalidate the opponents' claim. The author refers to previous arguments where this was demonstrated and promises to refute any further objections later. Thus, the rational objection is dismissed.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason conclusively proves the impossibility of seeing God.
Correction: The Ash'ari position is that reason permits seeing God; it does not provide a proof against it.

D) They require it

Key Takeaway
Reason does not disprove seeing God; it permits it, so the rational objection fails.

The Certainty of Non-Existence Despite God's Power
Baqillani refutes an argument that we cannot be certain of non-existence by giving examples of God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani defends the concept of necessary knowledge of non-existence against the objection that God's power makes everything possible.

Core Idea
We know with certainty that some things do not exist without limiting God's power.

Baqillani addresses a common objection: if God can do anything, how can we be sure something does not exist? He responds that we have necessary knowledge (علم ضروري) that certain things have not occurred, even though they are possible. This lesson unpacks his refutation and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is introduced: 'If we were not compelled to know that does not exist, we would allow it.'
The objection assumes that without necessary knowledge, we would allow anything.
2
List the examples of necessary knowledge
Identify each example Baqillani gives of things we know necessarily do not exist, even though they are possible. Examples include a human without parents, a horse without breeding, etc.
These examples show that we have certainty about specific non-existences.
3
Understand the conclusion
Notice how the argument ends: 'So what you asked about is invalidated.' Baqillani concludes that the objection fails because we do have necessary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : لولا أننا مضطرون إلى العلم بأن ذلك ليس بموجود، لأجزناه وليس يجب أن نشك اليوم في أن الله تعالى قد فعل كل مقدور من عنده، كما لا يجب أن نشك في أنه قد خلق اليوم إنساناً لا أبوين وفرساً لا من نتاج وناراً غير محرقة لشيء وتمراً لا من نخل ولبناً لا من ضرع، وأنه قد أحيا الأموات بسائر الأقطار وعرج بنا البارحة إلى ملكوت السموات ثم ردّنا إلى مضاجعنا، وأنه قد أمات كل من فارقناه يوماً أو ساعةً من أقاربنا وأصدقائنا ألف مرة ثم أحياهم بعد ذلك وإن كان ذلك أجمع مقدوراً لله تعالى . فبطل بذلك ما سألتم عنه.
It was said to them: If we were not compelled to know that that does not exist, we would allow it. And it is not necessary for us to doubt today that God Almighty has done every possible thing from Himself, just as it is not necessary for us to doubt that He has created today a human without parents, a horse without breeding, a fire not burning anything, dates without palm trees, milk without udders, and that He has revived the dead in all regions, and took us last night to the kingdom of the heavens then returned us to our beds, and that He has caused to die everyone we parted with, be it a day or an hour, from our relatives and friends, a thousand times, then revived them after that, even if all that is possible for God Almighty. So what you asked about is invalidated.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal to an objection about necessary knowledge of non-existence. The author begins by stating the condition: 'If we were not compelled to know that that does not exist, we would allow it.' The opponents had likely argued that we cannot be certain something does not exist because God could always create it. Baqillani responds that we do have necessary knowledge of specific non-existences, so the objection is not valid. He then enumerates examples: we know necessarily that God has not created a human without parents today, a horse without breeding, etc., even though these are possible for God. He extends the examples to reviving the dead everywhere, ascending to heaven, and repeatedly causing death and resurrection of loved ones. All of these are possible for God, yet we know they have not happened. Therefore, the objection that we cannot be certain of non-existence is false. The conclusion: 'So what you asked about is invalidated.'

Key Terms
العلم الضروري (necessary knowledge)
مقدور (possible/potential)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because we cannot imagine something, it is impossible; Baqillani clarifies that necessary knowledge is based on evidence, not imagination.
Correction: Baqillani shows that we have necessary knowledge of non-existence through common sense and observation, not by imagining possibilities.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why is the objection that we cannot be certain of non-existence invalid?
A) Because we have no evidence for non-existence.
B) Because we are compelled by necessary knowledge that certain things do not exist.
C) Because God's power is limited.
D) Because everything is possible.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends necessary knowledge of non-existence by showing that we are compelled to know that certain possible events have not occurred, thereby refuting the objection that God's power prevents certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the many things God could have done in your life but did not, do you feel compelled to recognize His wisdom in the natural order? How does that recognition affect your gratitude today?
?
Baqillani says we are 'compelled' to know certain things. Is there an area in your life where you are avoiding the certainty that evidence demands, because it would require you to change a belief or action?
?
If you truly believed that God could have created you in any way but chose your specific circumstances, would you live differently today? What would you start doing or stop doing to honor that specific design?

The Self-Evident Distinction Proves Ability with the Act
Baqillani argues that the distinction between voluntary and forced actions proves ability is concurrent with the act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the inherent distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions demonstrates that human ability (qudra) is concurrent with the act.

Core Idea
Your own experience of choice proves ability is with the act.

Imam Baqillani presents a proof from immediate self-awareness: every person can tell the difference between an action they choose and one forced upon them. He argues that this distinction cannot be explained by genus, knowledge, locus, or will alone. Therefore, the defining factor must be the presence of power (qudra) at the moment of the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the question-and-answer format and the key distinction Baqillani draws from human self-awareness.
Pay attention to the four alternatives he eliminates.
2
Identify the core distinction and rejected factors
Baqillani points to a self-evident difference between voluntary actions (standing, sitting, speaking by choice) and involuntary ones (illness, paralysis). He then rules out four possible causes for this difference: genus, knowledge, locus, and will. Mark these in the text.
Understanding why these are rejected is crucial.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the only remaining explanation is the presence of power (qudra) with the chosen act, Baqillani concludes that ability must be concurrent with the act. Reflect on how this supports the Ash'ari view of kasb.
This conclusion is the main theological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : أجل . فإن قال : ولم قلتم ذلك؟ قلنا لأن الإنسان يعرف من نفسه فرقاً بين قيامه وقعوده وكلامه إذا كان واقعاً بحسب اختياره وقصده، وبين ما يضطر إليه مما لا قدرة له عليه من الزمانة والمرض والحركة من الفالج وغير ذلك . وليس يفترق الشيئان في ذلك الجنسهما، ولا للعلم بهما، ولا لاختلاف محلّهما، ولا للإرادة لأحدهما . فوجب أن يحصل مع كسبه على هذه الصفة لكونه قادرا عليه .
It was said to him: Yes. Then if he says: And why did you say that? We say: Because a person knows from himself a difference between his standing and sitting and his speech when it occurs according to his choice and intention, and between what he is compelled to from that which he has no power over, such as chronic illness, sickness, and movement from paralysis and other than that. And the two things do not differ in their genus, nor by knowledge of them, nor by difference of their locus, nor by will for one of them. So it is necessary that [the act] occurs along with his acquisition in this manner because he is capable of it.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a proof defending the Ash'ari position that human ability (istita'a) is inseparable from the act itself. The opponent asks why they claim that ability is with the act. The answer begins by pointing to an immediate, self-evident distinction every human finds within themselves: there is a clear difference between actions we do by choice (like standing, sitting, speaking intentionally) and things that happen to us without any power on our part (like involuntary shaking from illness or paralysis). The argument then eliminates alternative explanations for this distinction: it is not due to the genus of the actions, nor due to knowledge of them, nor due to the locus (the body part involved), nor due to the presence or absence of will for one of them. Since these factors do not account for the difference, the only remaining explanation is that the distinguishing factor is the presence of power (qudra) concurrently with the chosen action. Therefore, the power must be present at the moment of acquisition (kasb), making the person capable of that action at that moment.

Key Terms
اختيار
قدرة
كسب
اضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument relies on external observation of actions.
Correction: Baqillani grounds the distinction in immediate self-awareness, not external observation.

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis for distinguishing voluntary actions from involuntary ones according to Baqillani?
A) The knowledge of the action
B) The locus of the action
C) The person's own self-awareness of a difference
D) The will for the action

Key Takeaway
The self-evident difference between chosen and compelled actions shows that human ability must be present at the time of the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a decision you made today that required real effort—perhaps holding your tongue or choosing to pray. Did you feel your ability to do that action was a gift given at that exact moment, or did you assume you always had it stored inside you? How does Baqillani's argument shift the way you see your own acts of obedience?

?
Consider a trial you faced where you had no control, like an illness or an accident. Baqillani says you know the difference between that and your chosen actions by immediate self-awareness. Now ask yourself: do you truly live as if you are accountable for every deliberate move, knowing that your ability to act comes directly from Allah at each moment?

The Impossibility of Accidental Powers Enduring
Baqillani argues against accident endurance using a two-branch dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that accidental powers cannot endure and how his dilemma refutes the possibility.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot endure; their endurance would break their nature as transient entities.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a crucial question in kalam: why can't a power (like the power to act) persist before the act? The opponent challenges the Ash'ari position that powers are created only at the moment of action. Baqillani responds with a tight logical dilemma that exposes the impossibility of accident endurance. By analyzing this argument, you will see how kalam uses basic ontological categories to defend its view of divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice the objection-response format.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find the two branches: endurance by self or by cause.
3
Analyze self-endurance
Understand why self-endurance would require endurance from the first moment, which is impossible.
This relies on the definition of accidents as non-enduring.
4
Analyze caused endurance
Understand why a cause would turn the accident into a substance.
Only substances can have causes subsisting in them.
5
Reflect on broader theology
Consider why this argument is important for Ash'ari occasionalism.
It supports the idea that powers are created anew with each act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : ولم قلتم إنه لا يجوز أن تبقى إلى حين وجود الفعل، ولا شيء من الأعراض؟ قيل له : لأنه لو جاز بقاؤها، لكانت إنما تبقى لنفسها أو لعلة . فلو بقيت لنفسها، لبقيت في حال حدوثها وذلك محال ولو بقيت لعلة، لوجب أن تقوم بها العلل وذلك يوجب أن تكون جسماً أو جوهراً ليس بعرض وذلك فاسد لما قدمناه من قبل.
Then if a speaker says: And why did you say that it is not permissible for them [i.e., powers] to remain until the time of the act's existence, nor any of the accidents? It is said to him: Because if their endurance were permissible, they would either endure by themselves or by a cause. If they endured by themselves, they would have endured at the moment of their occurrence, and that is impossible. And if they endured by a cause, then it would be necessary that causes subsist in them, and that would require them to be a body or a substance, not an accident, and that is invalid according to what we have established previously.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the possibility that accidents (including the power for acquisition) can endure (baqa') across time. The opponent asks for the reason why powers—indeed any accident—cannot persist until the moment the act occurs.

Baqillani's answer is a two-branch dilemma: if an accident were to endure, its endurance would have to be either (1) self-subsisting (li nafsiha) or (2) caused by something else (li 'illa). 

- If self-subsisting, then the accident would have to endure from the very moment it comes into existence (fi hal huduthiha). But if it endured only later, not from its first moment, that would be arbitrary. However, an accident's existence and non-endurance are simultaneous by definition; hence enduring from the start is impossible because that would contradict its nature as a transient accident.
- If the endurance is caused by a cause, then that cause would need to subsist in the accident itself. But only bodies or substances can have causes subsisting in them (i.e., inhere in them). This would transform the accident into a body or substance (jism or jawhar), which contradicts its definition as an accident. This is invalid based on earlier established principles (that accidents do not subsist in other accidents, and a body is necessary for inherence).

Thus, the endurance of any accident is impossible, and therefore the power to act cannot persist before the act; it occurs anew at the time of the act.

Key Terms
بقاء
عرض
علة
جوهر
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents can endure if they have a sustaining cause.
Correction: Baqillani argues that a cause subsisting in an accident would make it a substance, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what would happen if an accident endured by a cause?
A) It would become a substance
B) It would cease to be an accident
C) It would require another accident to sustain it
D) It would become eternal

Key Takeaway
Accidental powers cannot endure because their endurance would either require impossible self-subsistence or transform them into substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very ability to act must be recreated every instant, how does that change your sense of independence from Allah? Would you still claim ownership over your achievements?
?
Consider a sin you committed in the past. According to this lesson, the power to commit that sin did not endure from the time of its potential to its actual moment. How does this fact make you accountable before Allah for that action, knowing He created the power at that instant?
?
This lesson shows that even your physical attributes are momentary creations. If you really believed your own strength and sight are constantly renewed, would you live in greater awareness of Allah's presence? What is one change you can make today to live that awareness?

Answering the Objection on Human Burdening Beyond Capacity
The author answers an objection about whether burdening beyond capacity is permissible for humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why burdening someone beyond their immediate capacity can be considered permissible in Islamic theology, as argued by Imam Baqillani.

Core Idea
Commanding the seated to stand while seated shows that impossible commands are not always unjust.

This lesson addresses a subtle objection: if God can command the impossible (taklif ma la yutaq), can humans do the same? The objector questions the moral goodness of such burdening. Imam Baqillani replies with a concrete everyday example that demonstrates its permissibility in human interactions. Understanding this example clarifies the rational basis for temporary or test-based commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and its premise
Read the objection carefully. What is the objector asking? Is he asking about moral goodness (yuhsinu) or permissibility (yasūghu)? Notice both terms.
The objector uses two terms: 'good' and 'permissible'.
2
Analyze the author's affirmative response
The author says 'Yes' (ajal). Then gives an example. Trace the logic: the example of commanding a seated person to stand while seated shows that the command is not inherently unjust.
The example relies on the fact that the person is unable at that precise moment.
3
Understand the role of 'already clarified proof'
The author references 'the proof we have clarified'. This links back to earlier discussions about capacity. Reflect on why this proof is invoked here.
This indicates the objection is answered within a systematic framework.
4
Formulate the conclusion in your own words
Write a short conclusion: burdening beyond capacity is not necessarily evil when done by humans, because it can serve a valid purpose (e.g., test, legal obligation).
The example demonstrates that the command is meaningful even if immediate performance is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب فإن قال قائل : وهل يحسن مثل هذا التكليف منا أو يسوغ لنا؟ قيل له : أجل، لأنَّا قد نكلف القاعد القيام والتصرف في حال قعوده، وهو لا يقدر في تلك الحال على ما نكلفه لما قد أوضحناه من الدليل فوجب صحة ذلك من فعلنا.
Chapter. If someone says: Is such burdening good from us or permissible for us? It is said to him: Yes, because we may burden the seated person with standing and acting while he is seated, and he is not able at that time to do what we burden him with, due to what we have clarified of the proof, so the correctness of that from our action is necessary.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer concerning whether it is good or permissible for humans to burden someone beyond their capacity. The objector asks if such an act is morally acceptable for humans. The author answers affirmatively, providing an example: we command a seated person to stand and act while he is still seated, even though at that moment he cannot do so. The author notes that the proof for this has already been clarified (referring to previous arguments about capacity and obligation). Thus, burdening beyond capacity is not inherently unjust when done by humans, as it can be a valid legal or test-based command. This response defends the consistency of divine and human commands regarding capacity.

Key Terms
تكليف
القاعد
تصرف
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that commanding the impossible is always unjust, whether from God or humans.
Correction: The author shows that it is not necessarily so; the command can be valid when it serves a purpose such as testing or establishing a legal obligation, as in the example of the seated person.

Checkpoint
What example does the author give to show that burdening beyond capacity is permissible from humans?
A) Commanding a blind person to see
B) Commanding a seated person to stand
C) Commanding a poor person to give charity
D) Commanding a child to fast

Key Takeaway
Burdening beyond capacity can be permissible even for humans, as the author demonstrates through the example of commanding a seated person to stand while seated, which is not inherently unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when Allah commanded you through the Qur'an or Sunnah to do something you felt utterly incapable of. Did you question His justice? How does this lesson challenge your assumption that your inability at that moment made the command unfair? What would it look like to obey even when you feel unable?

You've likely felt burdened by responsibilities from others—family, work, community. When you failed, did you blame the one who asked? This lesson shows that a command beyond present capacity can be just. Who have you accused of injustice? Will you ask their forgiveness today?
?
The author says commanding the seated to stand while seated is 'correct from our action.' If this is true for humans, how much more for Allah? What command of Allah have you been avoiding because you think it's 'too much'? What is one step you can take right now to begin obeying it, even if you cannot do it perfectly?

Definition of Ajal and Its Proof
Baqillani defines ajal and cites the Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's definition of 'ajal' (term) and the Quranic proof that every person dies at his fixed term.

Core Idea
The term of death is the time Allah knows, and it cannot be altered.

Baqillani begins by establishing the correct understanding of 'ajal'. He provides a Quranic proof and a clear definition that distinguishes the fixed term from mere possibility. This lesson will focus on that definition and proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the question and proof
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the passage begins with an objection and a request for proof. Then find the Quranic verse that provides the proof.
The verse is: 'When their term comes they will not delay it by an hour nor advance it.'
2
Understand the definition through analogy
Baqillani defines ajal of death as the time of death, similar to the due date of a debt. Reflect on how this analogy clarifies that the term is a fixed endpoint.
The term is not a flexible period but a fixed moment.
3
Note the distinction: fixed by divine knowledge, not by impossibility
Baqillani says the term cannot be delayed, 'not because it is not possible to delay it.' This means the fixity is due to Allah's knowledge, not because delaying is inherently impossible. Understand this nuance.
This distinction is important for later refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : بل يموت بأجله المقدور. فإن قال : وما الحجة في ذلك؟ قيل له : قوله عزّ وجلّ : فإذا جاء أجلهم لا يستأخرون ساعة ولا يستقدمون. وأجل الموت هو وقت الموت، كما أن أجل الدين هو وقت حلوله. وكل شيء وقت به فهو أجل له. وأجل الإنسان هو الوقت الذي يعلم الله أنه يموت فيه لا محالة. وهو وقت لا يجوز تأخير موته عنه، لا من حيث إنه ليس بمقدور تأخيره. وجل حياته هو مدة الزمان الذي علم الله عز وجل أنه يحيا إليه لا تجوز الزيادة عليه ولا الانتقاص منه. وقد قال كثير من المعتزلة إلا من شدّ منهم، إن المقتول مات بغير أجله الذي ضُرب له، وإنه لو لم يُقتل لحيي.
It was said to him: Rather, he dies by his predestined term. If he says: And what is the proof for that? It was said to him: His saying, Mighty and Majestic: 'When their term comes they will not delay it by an hour nor advance it.' And the term of death is the time of death, just as the term of a debt is the time of its payment. And everything for which a time is set, that time is its term. And the term of a human is the time that Allah knows he will die at, inevitably. And it is a time that cannot be delayed beyond it, not because it is not possible to delay it. And the term of his life is the duration of time that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, knows he will live to, without increase or decrease. And many of the Mu'tazila – except a few among them – have said that the murdered person died before his appointed term, and that if he had not been killed he would have lived.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage begins by citing an objection-answer: the opponent asserts that a person dies by his predestined term, and the response provides a Quranic proof: 'When their term comes they will not delay it by an hour nor advance it.' The author then defines 'ajal' (term) for death as the fixed time known only to Allah, which cannot be altered in any way. The definition is illustrated with an analogy to a debt's due date. After defining the inviolability of the term, the author reports the Mu'tazila position (except a few) that the murdered person dies before his assigned term, implying that if not killed he would have lived longer. This sets up the refutation to follow.

Key Terms
أجل
موت
مقدور
وقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'ajal' means a flexible duration that can be shortened by human actions like murder.
Correction: Baqillani defines ajal as a fixed endpoint known only to Allah, not a flexible period. It cannot be delayed or advanced, regardless of human actions.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the 'ajal' (term) of a human?
A) The maximum possible lifespan of a human.
B) The time Allah knows he will die at, which cannot be delayed or advanced.
C) The time of natural death, which can be shortened by murder.
D) The time of death as determined by the person's actions.

Key Takeaway
The ajal of death is the fixed time known only to Allah, neither delayed nor advanced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that your lifespan cannot be increased or decreased by even one hour, what would you do today that you have been putting off?
?
What would change in your daily life if you lived with the certainty that your term of life is exactly what Allah knows, no more and no less?
?
What is one thought or habit that stems from imagining you have more time than you actually do, and how can you replace it with the reality taught in this lesson?

The Mu'tazila Position on the Murdered Person
Baqillani reports the Mu'tazila position on the murdered person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that the murdered person dies before his term and how it contradicts Baqillani's definition of ajal.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila error claims murder ends life before its appointed time.

After defining ajal, Baqillani reports the view of many Mu'tazila that the murdered person dies before his term. This lesson examines that claim in light of the definition of ajal as a fixed endpoint.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Mu'tazila claim in the passage
Find the final sentence of the passage. It begins with 'And many of the Mu'tazila...'. Read it carefully.
2
Compare with the definition of ajal
Recall the definition from the previous lesson: ajal is the time Allah knows a person will die, which cannot be delayed or advanced. The Mu'tazila claim that the murdered person dies before his term directly contradicts this definition.
If the term is fixed, it cannot come earlier or later.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this claim is problematic. If murder could cut life short, then Allah's knowledge would be wrong. Baqillani will later refute this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : بل يموت بأجله المقدور. فإن قال : وما الحجة في ذلك؟ قيل له : قوله عزّ وجلّ : فإذا جاء أجلهم لا يستأخرون ساعة ولا يستقدمون. وأجل الموت هو وقت الموت، كما أن أجل الدين هو وقت حلوله. وكل شيء وقت به فهو أجل له. وأجل الإنسان هو الوقت الذي يعلم الله أنه يموت فيه لا محالة. وهو وقت لا يجوز تأخير موته عنه، لا من حيث إنه ليس بمقدور تأخيره. وجل حياته هو مدة الزمان الذي علم الله عز وجل أنه يحيا إليه لا تجوز الزيادة عليه ولا الانتقاص منه. وقد قال كثير من المعتزلة إلا من شدّ منهم، إن المقتول مات بغير أجله الذي ضُرب له، وإنه لو لم يُقتل لحيي.
It was said to him: Rather, he dies by his predestined term. If he says: And what is the proof for that? It was said to him: His saying, Mighty and Majestic: 'When their term comes they will not delay it by an hour nor advance it.' And the term of death is the time of death, just as the term of a debt is the time of its payment. And everything for which a time is set, that time is its term. And the term of his life is the duration of time that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, knows he will live to, without increase or decrease. And many of the Mu'tazila – except a few among them – have said that the murdered person died before his appointed term, and that if he had not been killed he would have lived.

This passage begins by citing an objection-answer: the opponent asserts that a person dies by his predestined term, and the response provides a Quranic proof: 'When their term comes they will not delay it by an hour nor advance it.' The author then defines 'ajal' (term) for death as the fixed time known only to Allah, which cannot be altered in any way. The definition is illustrated with an analogy to a debt's due date. After defining the inviolability of the term, the author reports the Mu'tazila position (except a few) that the murdered person dies before his assigned term, implying that if not killed he would have lived longer. This sets up the refutation to follow.

The Mu'tazila claim that murder ends life before its appointed term, contradicting the fixed definition of ajal.

?

God's Power to Extend Life: Q&A on Divine Decree and Knowledge
Baqillani responds to two objections about God's power to extend life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani reconciles God's omnipotence with His foreknowledge in determining the lifespan of a human being.

Core Idea
God's power to extend life is real, but actual events are fixed by His eternal knowledge.

In this passage, Baqillani engages with an imagined interlocutor who questions the relationship between God's power to prolong life and the actual occurrence of death. The dialogue clarifies that while God can do anything possible, what He actually does is determined by His foreknowledge. This lesson walks through the objections and responses to understand the Ash'ari position on divine decree and human lifespan.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once through. Notice the question-and-answer format and the two distinct objections raised.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection asks whether God could preserve a person to a later age. The second questions why we don't say the term was later if it was possible. Follow how Baqillani answers each.
The passage is incomplete at the end, but the main argument is clear.
3
Reflect on the key principle
Understand the distinction between possibility (imkan) and actuality (wujud). Baqillani argues that actual death is tied to what God knows He will do, not merely to what He can do.
This is a central Ash'ari principle: divine knowledge determines actual events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فهل كان جائزاً في قدرة الله تعالى أن يُبقي من أماته ابن عشرين سنة إلى ثلاثين سنة أو ما هو أكثر منها؟ قيل له : أجل، لو بقاه لبقي. وإن كان لا يفعل ذلك، كان المعلوم حاله أنه يميته ابن عشرين سنة، ولن يجوز ترك فعل ما في المعلوم أنه يفعل، وإن كان مقدوراً تركه على أنه لو ترك لكان السابق في المعلوم أنه يترك. فإن قال قائل : فما أنكرتم أن يكون أجله ثلاثين سنة، لأنه لولم يمته، لبقي إلى ذلك الوقت؟ قيل له : لا يجب ذلك، لأن أجل الإنسان وقت موته على ما بينا. وليس يجوز أن يكون ما لم يبق إليه، مما كان يصح في العقل أن [؟]...
If a questioner says: Was it possible, in the power of Allah Most High, to preserve one whom He caused to die at twenty years of age up to thirty years or more? It was said to him: Yes, if He preserved him, he would have remained. But if He does not do that, then the known state is that He will cause him to die at twenty years, and it is not permissible to abandon an act that is known that He will do, even though it is within His power to abandon it, on the basis that if He abandoned it, the preceding in knowledge would be that He abandons it. If a questioner says: Then why do you deny that his term was thirty years, because if He had not caused him to die, he would have lived to that time? It was said to him: That is not necessary, because the term of a human is the time of his death as we have explained. And it is not permissible that what he has not been preserved to, is something that was possible in reason to [؟]...
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer exchange about God's omnipotence. An opponent asks whether God could extend the life of someone He made die at twenty to thirty years. The answer is yes, but God's foreknowledge determines what He actually does. If He knows He will cause death at twenty, He cannot leave undone what He knows He will do. Another objection follows: if God could have made him live to thirty, why not say his term was thirty? The response reiterates that the term is defined by the actual time of death, not a hypothetical. The argument appears incomplete in the text. The author is emphasizing that actual divine decree defines the term, not possibilities.

Key Terms
قدرة
معلوم
إبقاء
ترك
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God can do anything, then any possible lifespan is actually attainable for a person.
Correction: According to Baqillani, God's power is unlimited, but what actually happens is determined by what He knows and wills. A possible lifespan that God does not will is not actual; the term is fixed by the actual time of death.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, if God could have made someone live to thirty but caused him to die at twenty, what is his term?
A) Thirty years, because God could have done it.
B) Twenty years, because that is when he actually died.
C) Depends on the person's actions.
D) It is uncertain.

Key Takeaway
For Baqillani, a person's term is defined by the actual time of death, not by what is merely possible in God's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation you wish were different—like a shorter life of a loved one—do you silently protest, thinking God could have done otherwise? How does Baqillani's teaching that God's knowledge determines actual events challenge you to submit to His decree without complaint? What concrete step will you take today to accept God's decree as the only reality?
?
If you truly believed that your lifespan is exactly the time of your death, not a matter of chance or possibility, how would it change the way you spend each day? What would you do differently starting tomorrow to live with that certainty?
?
Do you ever mentally escape into 'what if' scenarios, imagining a different life that might have been? How does this passage strip away those illusions? Will you repent from living in fantasy and embrace the actual life God has written for you, with all its hardships?
