The Three Meanings of Hasan and Qabih
Classifies three senses of 'good' and 'bad', placing the moral sense as revelational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of 'good' and 'bad' in Islamic theology and why only the third is disputed between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
‘Good’ and ‘bad’ have three meanings; only the reward-punishment sense is disputed.

Before we can discuss whether acts are good or bad by reason or revelation, we must clarify what these terms mean. Imam al-Baqillani distinguishes three senses of hasan and qabih: perfection, benefit, and moral value connected to reward and punishment. The first two are rational and agreed upon; the third is the real point of controversy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice how Baqillani first contrasts the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions before defining the three meanings.
2
Identify the three meanings
Find the three meanings listed in the passage: (1) perfection/deficiency, (2) benefit/harm, (3) reward/punishment. Note which one is disputed and which are agreed upon.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila argue that even the third meaning can be known by reason, sometimes by necessity or reasoning. Consider their examples of beneficial truthfulness and harmful truthfulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القبيح : ما نهى عنه شرعاً . والحسن بخلافه ولا حكم للعقل في حسن الأشياء وقبحها، وليس ذلك عائداً إلى أمر حقيقي في الفعل يكشف عنه الشرع ؛ بل الشرع هو المثبت له والمبين، ولو عكس القضية فحسن ما قبحه وقبح ما حسنه لم يكن ممتنعاً، وانقلب الأمر. وقالت المعتزلة : بل الحاكم بهما العقل والفعل حسن أو قبيح في نفسه والشرع كاشف ومبين وليس له أن يعكس القضية . ولا بد أولاً من تحرير محل النزاع فنقول : الحسن والقبح يقال لمعان ثلاثة : الأول : صفة الكمال والنقص ، يقال : العلم حسن والجهل قبيح، ولا نزاع أن مدركه العقل . والثاني : ملاءمة الغرض ومنافرته وقد يعبر عنهما بالمصلحة والمفسدة، وذلك أيضاً عقلي ويختلف بالاعتبار؛ فإن قتل زيد مصلحة لأعدائه ومفسدة لأوليائه. والثالث : تعلق المدح والثواب، أو الذم والعقاب، وهذا هو محل النزاع فهو عندنا شرعي، وعند المعتزلة عقلي. قالوا للفصل جهة محسنة أو مقبحة ثم إنها قد تدرك بالضرورة كحسن الصدق النافع وقبح الكذب الضار، وقد تدرك بالنظر كحسن الصدق الضار وقبح الكذب النافع مثلا. وقد لا تدرك بالعقل ولكن إذا ورد به الشرع علم أن ثمة جهة محسنة كما في صوم آخر يوم من رمضان أو مقبحة كصوم أول يوم من شوال.
The bad: what is prohibited by the Law. And the good is its opposite, and there is no ruling of the intellect regarding the goodness of things and their badness; that is not due to a real matter in the act that the Law reveals; rather the Law is the establisher of it and the clarifier, and if He reversed the matter and made good what He had made bad and made bad what He had made good, it would not be impossible, and the matter would be reversed. The Mu'tazila said: Rather the judge of them both is the intellect, and the act is good or bad in itself, and the Law is a revealer and clarifier and it cannot reverse the matter. And it is necessary first to define the point of dispute, so we say: 'Good' and 'bad' are applied for three meanings: The first: the attribute of perfection and deficiency; it is said: knowledge is good and ignorance is bad, and there is no dispute that its perceiver is the intellect. The second: compatibility with purpose and incompatibility; they are also called benefit and harm, and this too is rational and varies by consideration; for killing Zayd is a benefit for his enemies and a harm for his friends. The third: connection to praise and reward, or blame and punishment; and this is the point of dispute: it is revelational in our view, and rational according to the Mu'tazila. They (the Mu'tazila) said that there is for the act a cause that makes it good or bad; then it is sometimes perceived by necessity, like the goodness of beneficial truthfulness and the badness of harmful lying, and sometimes perceived by reasoning, like the goodness of harmful truthfulness and the badness of beneficial lying for example. And sometimes it is not perceived by the intellect, but when the Law comes, it is known that there is a cause that makes it good, such as fasting on the last day of Ramadan, or makes it bad, such as fasting on the first day of Shawwal.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a methodological classification of the terms 'good' (hasan) and 'bad' (qabih). It begins by summarizing the Ash'ari position: moral value is determined by divine law, not by reason. The intellect alone cannot judge acts; the Law establishes and clarifies value, and God could hypothetically reverse rulings. The Mu'tazila oppose this, asserting the intellect as the judge and acts having intrinsic value. The author then clarifies the three distinct meanings of these terms to isolate the real point of contention. First meaning: perfection vs. deficiency (e.g., knowledge is good, ignorance is bad) – unanimously rational. Second meaning: compatibility with purpose (benefit/harm) – also rational and relative. Third meaning: connection to reward or punishment – this is the disputed sense: Ash'aris hold it is known only through revelation; Mu'tazila hold it is knowable rationally, sometimes by necessity (e.g., truthfulness that benefits is good) and sometimes by reasoning (e.g., truthfulness that harms). The passage ends with an example where reason cannot judge and revelation is needed: fasting on the last day of Ramadan (good) and on the first day of Shawwal (bad).

Key Terms
الحسن
القبيح
الشرع
العقل
المصلحة
المفسدة
المدح والثواب
الذم والعقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All senses of 'good' and 'bad' are disputed between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila.
Correction: Only the third sense (connection to reward and punishment) is disputed; the first two (perfection and benefit) are agreed upon and rational.

Checkpoint
Which of the three meanings of 'good' and 'bad' is the point of contention between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila?
A) The first: perfection and deficiency.
B) The second: compatibility with purpose.
C) The third: connection to reward and punishment.
D) All three meanings are disputed.

Key Takeaway
The third meaning of 'good' and 'bad'—connection to reward and punishment—is the only one that is revelational according to Ash'aris and rational according to Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself justifying actions based on your own reasoning, rather than seeking what Allah has commanded? How does this lesson challenge that tendency?
?
When you consider the example of fasting on the last day of Ramadan—an act that reason might not deem good—how does it reshape your understanding of obedience? Are you willing to obey even when your intellect cannot perceive the benefit?
?
The Mu'tazila believed that good and bad are intrinsic; how does that affect the concept of divine command? Does your heart tremble at the thought that if you rely on your intellect, you might set yourself up as a judge over Allah's commands?

Second Objection: Inherent Badness of Pointless Pain
Objector argues that pain without benefit is inherently unjust; response denies its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school refutes the claim that pointless pain is inherently unjust by appealing to the test of necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
If pain were inherently bad, everyone would know it necessarily—but they don't.

This lesson examines a central objection against the Ash'ari position on divine justice. The objector asserts that any pain inflicted without any benefit or desert is intrinsically unjust. The response cuts to the heart of rationalist ethics: if such actions were truly evil in themselves, all rational beings would immediately recognize this. The fact that we do not universally agree undermines the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's statement: every infliction of pain without benefit or desert is unjust and bad in itself.
Notice the objector treats this as self-evident.
2
Understand the Ash'ari response
The response argues that if it were inherently bad, we would know it necessarily—but we don't. The absence of universal necessary knowledge disproves the claim.
The key term is 'اضطراراً' (necessarily).
3
Reflect on the implication
Why does the lack of necessary knowledge undermine the objector's position? Because if something were truly self-evident, no rational person could doubt it. Since we can doubt, it's not self-evident.
This argument protects God's actions from being judged by human rational standards.
4
Review the key term
Focus on 'الاضطرار' (necessity). In kalam, necessary knowledge is immediate, unavoidable, and universal. The objector's claim lacks this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فما (۸) أنكرتم أن يكون كل إيلام لا نفع (1) للمؤلم فيه في عاجل (١٠) ولا آجل ولا هو مستحق ظلماً في العقل و(١١) قبيحاً لنفسه؟ قلنا : من قبل ما بينا (١٢) أولاً من أن ذلك لو كان كذلك، لعلمنا قبح الضرر (۱۳) الجاري هذا المجرى اضطراراً. وفي كوننا غير مضطرين إلى ما وصفت (١٤) [؟]
For if a speaker says: What prevents you from holding that every infliction of pain that has no benefit for the one pained in the near or distant future, and is not deserved, is injustice in the intellect and bad in itself? We say: Because we clarified first that if that were the case, we would necessarily know the badness of the harm that runs in this course. And since we are not necessitated to what you described...
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a second objection-and-answer within the same discussion. The objector proposes that any pain inflicted without any benefit (immediate or deferred) and without being deserved is inherently unjust and intrinsically bad, according to reason. The Ash'ari response refutes this by appealing to the test of necessity: if such pain were inherently bad, all rational people would necessarily recognize its badness without reflection. Since we are not compelled to such knowledge (i.e., we can doubt or debate it), the claim fails. The response remains incomplete in the text, but the key point is that the absence of universal necessary knowledge disproves the objective inherent badness. This argument reinforces the Ash'ari position that moral values are not intrinsic but dependent on divine revelation or command.

Key Terms
الإيلام
الظلم
القبيح لنفسه
الاضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari response admits that some actions (like pointless pain) are intrinsically bad, but God is exempt due to His sovereignty.
Correction: The Ash'ari does not admit intrinsic badness; instead, it denies that we have necessary knowledge of any such intrinsic badness. The argument is epistemic, not ontological.

Checkpoint
What is the core evidence the Ash'ari uses to reject the claim that pointless pain is inherently bad?
A) The pain is actually beneficial in ways we cannot see.
B) God has commanded it, so it cannot be bad.
C) If it were inherently bad, we would all know it necessarily, but we do not.
D) The pain is compensated in the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response defeats the claim of inherent badness by showing that such badness is not universally and necessarily known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that feels completely pointless, does your heart immediately cry out that it is unjust? In that moment, do you trust your own sense of fairness more than God's wisdom? What would it cost you to surrender that judgment today?
?
You have learned that rational intuition alone cannot declare something as inherently bad. Are there any beliefs or complaints you hold against Allah that you have never examined through revelation? Will you search for His command before concluding something is wrong?
?
If the Ash'ari argument is true, then your emotional reaction to suffering—as powerful as it is—does not make it objectively evil. Can you accept that? Will you repent of any indignation you have harbored against Allah for trials He decreed, and instead ask for patience?

God’s Transcendence and the Basis of Moral Badness
Baqillani refutes the first objection about divine justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Baqillani, badness in human actions derives from divine prohibition and cannot be applied to God by analogy.

Core Idea
God’s absolute ownership makes His actions immune to human moral categories.

This opening section of Baqillani’s argument addresses a key Mu'tazilite objection: if causing harm is bad for humans, why is it not bad for God? Baqillani responds by grounding the badness of human actions in divine prohibition, and asserting God’s complete sovereignty, which prevents any analogy between human and divine actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim: that badness depends on divine command and prohibition.
The objection assumes that without God's prohibition, the action would not be bad.
2
Identify Baqillani's response
Notice how he distinguishes between human and divine actions. For humans, badness comes from God's prohibition; for God, there is no commander.
He uses the concept of God as absolute owner and subduer.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Ask yourself: why can't we compare God's actions to human actions? The passage gives the reason: God has no superior.
This is a core Ash'ari principle of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : إن ذلك إنما قبح منا وصار جوراً من فعلنا لأجل نهي مالك الأعيان والأشياء لنا عن فعله؛ فلولا تقبيحه لذلك ونهيه عنه، لما قبح منا . وقد أوضحنا ذلك فيما سلف لما قلنا إن ذلك ليس بقبيح في العقل لنفسه، لأنه كان أن يشترك في علمه العاقلين، ولكن يجب إذا كان الألم الموجود على هذه السبيل قبيحاً لكونه ألماً على هذه الصفة، أن لا يشركه في كونه قبيحاً إلا ما كان ألماً هذه صفته وذلك باطل باتفاق . وكذلك القول في كل ضرب من ضروب القبيح . والباري عز وجل هو المالك القاهر الذي الأشياء له وفي قبضته لا آمر عليه ولا حاظر . فلم يجب أن يقبح جميع ما ذكرناه من فعله قياساً على قبحه منا .

فإن قال قائل : فما أنكرتم أن يكون كل إيلام لا نفع للمؤلم فيه في عاجل ولا آجل ولا هو مستحق ظلماً في العقل وقبيحاً لنفسه؟ قلنا : من قبل ما بينا أولاً من أن ذلك لو كان كذلك، لعلمنا قبح الضرر الجاري هذا المجرى اضطراراً . وفي كوننا غير مضطرين إلى ما وصفت صفة الكمال والنقص، يقال : العلم حسن والجهل قبيح، ولا نزاع أن مدركه العقل . والثاني : ملاءمة الغرض ومنافرته وقد يعبر عنهما بالمصلحة والمفسدة، وذلك أيضاً عقلي ويختلف بالاعتبار؛ فإن قتل زيد مصلحة لأعدائه ومفسدة لأوليائه . والثالث : تعلق المدح والثواب، أو الذم والعقاب، وهذا هو محل النزاع فهو عندنا شرعي، وعند المعتزلة عقلي . قالوا للفصل جهة محسنة أو مقبحة ثم إنها قد تدرك بالضرورة كحسن الصدق النافع وقبح الكذب الضار، وقد تدرك بالنظر كحسن الصدق الضار وقبح الكذب النافع مثلا . وقد لا تدرك بالعقل ولكن إذا ورد به الشرع علم أن ثمة جهة محسنة كما في صوم آخر يوم من رمضان أو مقبحة كصوم أول يوم من شوال .

دليل على سقوط هذا السؤال . ولأن ذلك لو كان كذلك لوجب قبح هذا الضرر من كل من وجد منه، وكان لا معتبر باختلاف فاعليه وتباين محاله . ألا ترى أن الحركة التي تكون حركة لنفسها يجب أن تكون أبداً حركة حيث وجدت؟ وهذا يوجب أن يكون الكلب والسبع وسائر الحيوان الذي لا يعقل ظالماً راكباً للقبيح مستحقاً للذم والتأنيب، وأن يكون عاصياً فاسقاً بإيلامه للغير على هذه السبيل . وفي الاتفاق على فساد ذلك دليل على سقوط ما سألت عنه . ولأنه لو كان الأمر على ما وصفت، لم يكن الجهل والكذب قبيحين لأنهما ليسا بألم هذه سبيله . وقد بينا من أن الحكم العقلي الواجب لعلة ولوجه مخصوص لا يجوز ثبوته لبعض من هو حكم له بغير تلك العلة وذاك الوجه، لأن ذلك نقض للعلل وإبطال لها . فبطل بذلك ما قلت .
It was said to him: That thing is only bad from us and becomes injustice from our action because of the prohibition of the Owner of essences and things to us from doing it; so if not for His making it bad and His prohibition of it, it would not be bad from us. And we have clarified that previously when we said that that is not bad in the intellect in itself, because it would be that it is shared in its knowledge by rational beings, but it must be that when the existing pain in this way is bad due to its being a pain with this description, that only what is a pain with this description shares in its being bad, and that is false by consensus. And likewise the statement in every kind of badness. And the Creator, Mighty and Glorious, is the Owner, the Subduer, to whom belong things and in His grasp, no commander over Him nor prohibitor. So it is not necessary that all that we mentioned from His action be bad by analogy to its badness from us.

Then if a questioner says: Why do you deny that every infliction of pain that has no benefit for the inflicter in the near or distant future and is not deserved is an injustice in the intellect and bad in itself? We say: Because of what we first explained, that if that were the case, we would know necessarily the badness of harm proceeding in this way. And from the fact that we are not compelled to what you described: the attribute of perfection and imperfection; it is said: knowledge is good and ignorance is bad, and there is no dispute that its perceiver is the intellect. The second: suitability to purpose and opposition to it; they may be expressed as benefit and harm, and that is also rational and varies by consideration; for the killing of Zayd is a benefit for his enemies and a harm for his allies. The third: connection to praise and reward, or blame and punishment, and this is the point of contention; it is according to us revealed, and according to the Mu'tazila rational. They said for the distinction there is a beautifying or vilifying aspect; then indeed it may be perceived by necessity, like the goodness of beneficial truth and the badness of harmful lying, and it may be perceived by consideration, like the goodness of harmful truth and the badness of beneficial lying for example. And it may not be perceived by the intellect, but when revelation comes, it is known that there is a beautifying aspect, like fasting on the last day of Ramadan, or a vilifying aspect, like fasting on the first day of Shawwal.

Evidence for the fall of this question. And because if that were the case, the badness of this harm would be necessary from everyone from whom it proceeds, and there would be no consideration for the difference of its agents and the variety of their situations. Do you not see that motion which is motion in itself must always be motion wherever it exists? And this necessitates that the dog, the beast, and all animals that do not reason would be oppressors, committing the bad, deserving blame and reproach, and that they would be disobedient, sinful, by their inflicting pain on others in this way. And the consensus on the corruption of that is evidence for the fall of what you asked about. And because if the matter were as you described, ignorance and lying would not be bad because they are not pain in this way. And we have explained that the rational judgment necessitated for a cause and a specific aspect is not permissible to be established for some of those for whom it is a judgment without that cause and that aspect, because that invalidates the causes and nullifies them. So what you said is nullified by that.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that good and evil are intrinsic to actions and known by reason. It begins with an objection: why is it bad for humans but not for God? The author answers that badness in humans stems from divine prohibition; God is the absolute owner with no commander over Him, so His actions are not subject to human moral categories. A second objection follows: inflicting pain without benefit is intrinsically bad. The author responds with several arguments: (1) If it were intrinsically bad, we would know it necessarily, but we do not. (2) He then introduces a classification of good and evil into three categories: (a) perfection/imperfection (e.g., knowledge good, ignorance bad), which is rational; (b) suitability to purpose (benefit/harm), also rational but relative; (c) connection to praise/reward or blame/punishment, which is the disputed category: for Ash'aris it is based on revelation, for Mu'tazila on reason. He gives examples of how these are perceived (by necessity, by consideration, or only by revelation). (3) He provides a key argument: if pain were inherently bad, then any being causing pain would be evil, including animals, which is absurd. Also, ignorance and lying would not be bad since they cause no pain. He concludes that the objection fails because the rational judgment for a cause cannot be extended without that cause. The entire passage upholds the Ash'ari principle that moral obligations are known through revelation, not reason alone.

Key Terms
قبيح
نهي
مالك
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's actions are subject to the same moral standards as human actions, making Him unjust if He causes harm.
Correction: Baqillani shows that human badness depends on divine prohibition, and God has no commander, so analogy fails.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what makes an action bad for humans?
A) Its intrinsic nature
B) Divine prohibition
C) Societal consensus
D) The amount of harm caused

Key Takeaway
Human moral judgments cannot be applied to God because His sovereignty transcends any external command or prohibition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel that something in your life is unjust from God, are you in fact placing yourself as a 'commander over Him'? What would it take for you to fully submit to His absolute sovereignty today, even without understanding His wisdom?
?
This lesson says God has 'no commander nor prohibitor'. Does your heart truly accept that, or do you secretly hold God to a standard of fairness you think He should meet? Will you repent from that attitude and trust His perfect justice?
?
If badness is only defined by God's command for humans, does that make sin any less serious in your eyes? On the contrary, how does this truth deepen your sense of accountability to the One who alone determines right and wrong?

The Three Categories of Good and Evil
Baqillani classifies good/evil into three types with different epistemological bases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani’s threefold classification of good and evil and how it resolves the dispute between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Moral obligation is known through revelation, not reason alone—this is the core of the Ash'ari position.

Baqillani responds to a second objection by introducing a crucial distinction. He argues that good and evil are not monolithic; they fall into three categories, only one of which—moral obligation—is the subject of debate. This classification clarifies the Ash'ari stance that reason can know some values (like knowledge being good) but cannot determine moral duty without revelation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second objection
Read the question about inflicting pain without benefit being intrinsically bad.
The objector claims that such pain is inherently unjust and bad.
2
Learn the three categories
Baqillani lists three ways good/evil are understood: (1) perfection/imperfection, (2) benefit/harm, (3) reward/punishment. Identify each.
The third category is the point of contention.
3
Note the epistemological differences
See how each category is perceived: by necessity, by consideration, or only by revelation.
Fasting on Eid is given as an example of revelation-based judgment.
4
Connect to the Ash'ari-Mu'tazila debate
Realize that Baqillani is arguing that moral obligation (third category) is revealed, not rational.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite claim of intrinsic moral values.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary

Which category of good and evil does Baqillani say is the point of contention between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila?
A) Perfection and imperfection
B) Benefit and harm

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that only Allah determines what is truly good and bad. But when you feel angry at someone who wrongs you, do you check the Quran and Sunnah before judging their action, or do you rely on your feeling of being harmed? Which category from Baqillani's classification does your reaction belong to? Will you commit to pausing before condemning others and asking: 'Has Allah forbidden this?'
?
Baqillani says that the goodness of truthful speech is known by necessity when it benefits, but even when it harms, the intellect can still see it as good. I ask you: When telling the truth would cost you something precious—a friendship, a job, your reputation—do you still speak it? Or do you rationalize your way into silence? What does your answer reveal about the authority you actually give to revelation over your own convenience?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that reason alone can know moral obligation. Deep down, do you live as though some things are just obviously right or wrong without needing a divine command? When have you made a decision that contradicted a clear revealed ruling because you thought your reason was sufficient? Are you willing to submit your moral reasoning to the Qur'an and Sunnah starting today, even when it feels wrong?

Absurd Consequences of Intrinsic Moral Evil
Further arguments against intrinsic moral evil using absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the absurd implications of the claim that causing pain is intrinsically evil and the principle that rational judgments are tied to specific causes.

Core Idea
If pain were intrinsically evil, then animals would be sinners—an absurdity that refutes the claim.

Baqillani delivers a knock-down argument: if causing pain were inherently evil, then every being that causes pain—including animals—would be blameworthy. But we do not hold animals morally responsible. This shows that moral badness depends on additional factors, not on the pain itself. He also addresses ignorance and lying, and reinforces the principle of causal specificity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Baqillani argues that if pain were intrinsically bad, then animals causing pain would also be evil.
This is absurd because animals are not morally accountable.
2
Consider the counterexample of ignorance and lying
If only pain were bad, then ignorance and lying (which are not painful) would not be bad. But they are clearly bad.
This shows that badness is not limited to pain.
3
Learn the principle of causal specificity
Baqillani says a rational judgment for a specific cause cannot be extended without that cause.
This principle is used to deny the generalization from human to divine actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : إن ذلك إنما قبح منا وصار جوراً من فعلنا لأجل نهي مالك الأعيان والأشياء لنا عن فعله؛ فلولا تقبيحه لذلك ونهيه عنه، لما قبح منا . وقد أوضحنا ذلك فيما سلف لما قلنا إن ذلك ليس بقبيح في العقل لنفسه، لأنه كان أن يشترك في علمه العاقلين، ولكن يجب إذا كان الألم الموجود على هذه السبيل قبيحاً لكونه ألماً على هذه الصفة، أن لا يشركه في كونه قبيحاً إلا ما كان ألماً هذه صفته وذلك باطل باتفاق . وكذلك القول في كل ضرب من ضروب القبيح . والباري عز وجل هو المالك القاهر الذي الأشياء له وفي قبضته لا آمر عليه ولا حاظر . فلم يجب أن يقبح جميع ما ذكرناه من فعله قياساً على قبحه منا .

فإن قال قائل : فما أنكرتم أن يكون كل إيلام لا نفع للمؤلم فيه في عاجل ولا آجل ولا هو مستحق ظلماً في العقل وقبيحاً لنفسه؟ قلنا : من قبل ما بينا أولاً من أن ذلك لو كان كذلك، لعلمنا قبح الضرر الجاري هذا المجرى اضطراراً . وفي كوننا غير مضطرين إلى ما وصفت صفة الكمال والنقص، يقال : العلم حسن والجهل قبيح، ولا نزاع أن مدركه العقل . والثاني : ملاءمة الغرض ومنافرته وقد يعبر عنهما بالمصلحة والمفسدة، وذلك أيضاً عقلي ويختلف بالاعتبار؛ فإن قتل زيد مصلحة لأعدائه ومفسدة لأوليائه . والثالث : تعلق المدح والثواب، أو الذم والعقاب، وهذا هو محل النزاع فهو عندنا شرعي، وعند المعتزلة عقلي . قالوا للفصل جهة محسنة أو مقبحة ثم إنها قد تدرك بالضرورة كحسن الصدق النافع وقبح الكذب الضار، وقد تدرك بالنظر كحسن الصدق الضار وقبح الكذب النافع مثلا . وقد لا تدرك بالعقل ولكن إذا ورد به الشرع علم أن ثمة جهة محسنة كما في صوم آخر يوم من رمضان أو مقبحة كصوم أول يوم من شوال .

دليل على سقوط هذا السؤال . ولأن ذلك لو كان كذلك لوجب قبح هذا الضرر من كل من وجد منه، وكان لا معتبر باختلاف فاعليه وتباين محاله . ألا ترى أن الحركة التي تكون حركة لنفسها يجب أن تكون أبداً حركة حيث وجدت؟ وهذا يوجب أن يكون الكلب والسبع وسائر الحيوان الذي لا يعقل ظالماً راكباً للقبيح مستحقاً للذم والتأنيب، وأن يكون عاصياً فاسقاً بإيلامه للغير على هذه السبيل . وفي الاتفاق على فساد ذلك دليل على سقوط ما سألت عنه . ولأنه لو كان الأمر على ما وصفت، لم يكن الجهل والكذب قبيحين لأنهما ليسا بألم هذه سبيله . وقد بينا من أن الحكم العقلي الواجب لعلة ولوجه مخصوص لا يجوز ثبوته لبعض من هو حكم له بغير تلك العلة وذاك الوجه، لأن ذلك نقض للعلل وإبطال لها . فبطل بذلك ما قلت .
It was said to him: That thing is only bad from us and becomes injustice from our action because of the prohibition of the Owner of essences and things to us from doing it; so if not for His making it bad and His prohibition of it, it would not be bad from us. And we have clarified that previously when we said that that is not bad in the intellect in itself, because it would be that it is shared in its knowledge by rational beings, but it must be that when the existing pain in this way is bad due to its being a pain with this description, that only what is a pain with this description shares in its being bad, and that is false by consensus. And likewise the statement in every kind of badness. And the Creator, Mighty and Glorious, is the Owner, the Subduer, to whom belong things and in His grasp, no commander over Him nor prohibitor. So it is not necessary that all that we mentioned from His action be bad by analogy to its badness from us.

Then if a questioner says: Why do you deny that every infliction of pain that has no benefit for the inflicter in the near or distant future and is not deserved is an injustice in the intellect and bad in itself? We say: Because of what we first explained, that if that were the case, we would know necessarily the badness of harm proceeding in this way. And from the fact that we are not compelled to what you described: the attribute of perfection and imperfection; it is said: knowledge is good and ignorance is bad, and there is no dispute that its perceiver is the intellect. The second: suitability to purpose and opposition to it; they may be expressed as benefit and harm, and that is also rational and varies by consideration; for the killing of Zayd is a benefit for his enemies and a harm for his allies. The third: connection to praise and reward, or blame and punishment, and this is the point of contention; it is according to us revealed, and according to the Mu'tazila rational. They said for the distinction there is a beautifying or vilifying aspect; then indeed it may be perceived by necessity, like the goodness of beneficial truth and the badness of harmful lying, and it may be perceived by consideration, like the goodness of harmful truth and the badness of beneficial lying for example. And it may not be perceived by the intellect, but when revelation comes, it is known that there is a beautifying aspect, like fasting on the last day of Ramadan, or a vilifying aspect, like fasting on the first day of Shawwal.

Evidence for the fall of this question. And because if that were the case, the badness of this harm would be necessary from everyone from whom it proceeds, and there would be no consideration for the difference of its agents and the variety of their situations. Do you not see that motion which is motion in itself must always be motion wherever it exists? And this necessitates that the dog, the beast, and all animals that do not reason would be oppressors, committing the bad, deserving blame and reproach, and that they would be disobedient, sinful, by their inflicting pain on others in this way. And the consensus on the corruption of that is evidence for the fall of what you asked about. And because if the matter were as you described, ignorance and lying would not be bad because they are not pain in this way. And we have explained that the rational judgment necessitated for a cause and a specific aspect is not permissible to be established for some of those for whom it is a judgment without that cause and that aspect, because that invalidates the causes and nullifies them. So what you said is nullified by that.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that good and evil are intrinsic to actions and known by reason. It begins with an objection: why is it bad for humans but not for God? The author answers that badness in humans stems from divine prohibition; God is the absolute owner with no commander over Him, so His actions are not subject to human moral categories. A second objection follows: inflicting pain without benefit is intrinsically bad. The author responds with several arguments: (1) If it were intrinsically bad, we would know it necessarily, but we do not. (2) He then introduces a classification of good and evil into three categories: (a) perfection/imperfection (e.g., knowledge good, ignorance bad), which is rational; (b) suitability to purpose (benefit/harm), also rational but relative; (c) connection to praise/reward or blame/punishment, which is the disputed category: for Ash'aris it is based on revelation, for Mu'tazila on reason. He gives examples of how these are perceived (by necessity, by consideration, or only by revelation). (3) He provides a key argument: if pain were inherently bad, then any being causing pain would be evil, including animals, which is absurd. Also, ignorance and lying would not be bad since they cause no pain. He concludes that the objection fails because the rational judgment for a cause cannot be extended without that cause. The entire passage upholds the Ash'ari principle that moral obligations are known through revelation, not reason alone.

ضرر
ظالم
عاقل
علة
نقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That moral badness is an intrinsic property of actions like causing pain.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if it were intrinsic, animals would be blameworthy; since they are not, badness depends on conditions like divine command.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani use as a reductio ad absurdum against the claim that causing pain is intrinsically evil?
A) That humans would then be justified in causing pain
B) That animals would be considered evil for causing pain
C) That ignorance and lying would become good
D) That God would be unjust

Key Takeaway
The absurd consequences of the Mu'tazilite view show that moral badness cannot be intrinsic to actions but depends on divine command and context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face pain or hardship, do you instinctively label it as 'evil' or unjust? How does Baqillani's argument that pain is not intrinsically evil challenge you to seek God's wisdom behind your trials instead of complaining? What would change in your daily complaints if you truly believed this?
?
The argument shows that animals are not morally accountable, but you are. When you cause harm to others—intentionally or not—do you excuse yourself like an 'animal acting on instinct', or do you take responsibility before God? In what specific situation have you excused your bad behavior as 'just human nature' rather than owning it as a sin?
?
If evil is not intrinsic but depends on God's command, how does this affect your habit of judging others' actions as simply 'good' or 'evil'? Think of a person you recently criticized. Are you certain your judgment aligns with God's command, or could you be imposing your own preferences? Will you pause before condemning someone next time?

Proof That Harm Is Not Inherently Evil
Baqillani gives a proof that the question about harm is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why harm (ضرر) cannot be inherently evil according to rational judgment, and how Baqillani refutes the questioner's assumption.

Core Idea
If harm were evil by nature, it would be evil from every agent — including animals.

Imam Baqillani tackles a critical objection: if God inflicts harm, can that harm be considered evil? He dismantles the assumption that harm is inherently evil by showing its absurd consequences. This passage establishes a foundational principle of Ash'ari theology: ethical judgments depend on the agent, not the act alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional argument followed by an analogy and a reductio ad absurdum.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main argument
Baqillani argues that if harm were evil in itself, it would be evil from anyone who inflicts it, including animals. That leads to absurd conclusions, so the premise is false.
Consider the analogy of motion to see why essential properties cannot vary by agent.
3
Analyze the second argument
He also points out that if harm's evil were based on pain, then ignorance and lying would not be evil since they are not physically painful. This shows the opponent's criterion is flawed.
This reinforces that evil is not tied to pain but to specific rational aspects.
4
Understand the general principle
Baqillani states that a rational judgment (حكم عقلي) necessitated by a specific cause and aspect cannot apply without that cause. This prevents arbitrary extension of moral categories.
This principle is key to Ash'ari ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل على سقوط هذا السؤال. ولأن ذلك لو كان كذلك لوجب قبح هذا الضرر من كل من وجد منه، وكان لا معتبر باختلاف فاعليه وتباين محاله . ألا ترى أن الحركة التي تكون حركة لنفسها يجب أن تكون أبداً حركة حيث وجدت؟ وهذا يوجب أن يكون الكلب والسبع وسائر الحيوان الذي لا يعقل ظالماً راكباً للقبيح مستحقاً للذم والتأنيب، وأن يكون عاصياً فاسقاً بإيلامه للغير على هذه السبيل . وفي الاتفاق على فساد ذلك دليل على سقوط ما سألت عنه . ولأنه لو كان الأمر على ما وصفت ، لم يكن الجهل والكذب قبيحين لأنهما ليسا بألم هذه سبيله . وقد بينا من أن الحكم العقلي الواجب لعلة ولوجه مخصوص لا يجوز ثبوته لبعض من هو حكم له بغير تلك العلة وذاك الوجه، لأن ذلك نقض للعلل وإبطال لها . فبطل بذلك ما قلت.
A proof that this question is invalid. And because if that were the case, then this harm would necessarily be evil from everyone from whom it occurs, and no consideration would be given to the difference of its agents and their multiplicity. Do you not see that motion which is motion for its own sake must always be motion wherever it exists? And this necessitates that the dog, the predator, and all animals that do not reason would be wrongdoers, committing the ugly, deserving blame and rebuke, and would be disobedient, sinful for causing pain to others in this manner. And agreement on the corruption of that is evidence of the invalidity of what you asked. And because if the matter were as you described, then ignorance and lying would not be evil because they are not pain of this sort. And we have explained that the rational judgment that is necessary for a cause and a specific aspect cannot be established for some of those for whom it is a judgment without that cause and that aspect, because that would be a breach of causes and an invalidation of them. So what you have said is invalidated by that.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a proof (دليل) against an implicit question that assumes harm (ضرر) is inherently evil (قبيح). Baqillani argues that if harm were evil by its very nature, it would be evil from anyone who inflicts it, regardless of who they are. He uses the analogy of motion: motion that is essential to a thing remains motion wherever it occurs; similarly, if harm were essentially evil, it would be evil from every agent. This would lead to absurd conclusions: animals that inflict pain would be considered wrongdoers deserving blame, which is false by consensus. Then he argues that if the opponent's position were correct, ignorance and lying would not be evil, since they are not painful (ألم) like physical harm. He concludes that a rational judgment (حكم عقلي) that is necessary due to a specific cause and aspect cannot be transferred to other cases without that cause. Thus, the questioner's premise is invalidated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God’s actions are subject to human standards of good and evil in the same way as human actions.
Correction: Baqillani shows that harm is not inherently evil; its moral status depends on the agent and context. God, as Creator, is not bound by the same ethical categories as creatures.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does Baqillani derive from the assumption that harm is inherently evil?
A) That God would be unjust
B) That animals would be wrongdoers deserving blame
C) That motion would stop
D) That ignorance would become good

Key Takeaway
Harm is not inherently evil; its moral status depends on the agent and the specific rational cause (علة ووجه), so God’s infliction of harm is not subject to human ethical judgments.

Will you commit to pausing and trusting that God's wisdom may be beyond your understanding, even when you cannot see the good?

Think of a time you were critical of someone else's suffering, assuming it was a punishment or a sign of God's displeasure. How does Baqillani's argument about animals not being blameworthy for causing pain apply to your tendency to assign moral blame to events you don't understand? Will you ask God to forgive your hasty judgments and grant you deeper trust in His justice?
?
This lesson teaches that rational judgments require a specific cause and aspect. Where in your life have you applied a general moral rule (like 'pain is evil') without considering the context and the agent? Will you repent of such judgments and resolve to align your understanding with the Ash'ari principle that only God's command determines good and evil?

The Rational Permissibility of Divine Forgiveness
Baqillani argues that forgiveness is inherently good, citing Qur'anic praise of human forgiveness, and extends it to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that forgiveness is rationally good (حسن) for both humans and God, and that God's forgiveness is permissible unless prevented by revealed reports.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is inherently good, so God's forgiveness is rationally permissible.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani tackles a key question: is it rationally possible for God to forgive sinners? He argues that forgiveness is not evil (قبيح) but good (حسن), using Qur'anic praise of human forgiveness as evidence. Then he extends the same reasoning to God, concluding that divine forgiveness is permissible unless revelation specifies otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the rhythm of the argument come through.
Notice that the passage begins with a conclusion: 'So its doer is not evil.'
2
Identify the key claim
The passage asserts that forgiveness is not evil (قبيح). This is the foundation for the entire argument. Ask yourself: why is this claim important for divine forgiveness?
3
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
The author cites several verses that praise human forgiveness. Locate each one in the translation and consider: why does Baqillani use human examples to talk about God?
These verses establish that forgiveness is a praiseworthy action (حسن) in human conduct.
4
Understand the rational analogy
The passage asks: 'How is it not permissible to give away a right to one who has the power to take it or leave it?' This is an analogy: if a person can forgo a debt, it is good. By extension, God can forgo punishment.
5
Note the qualification about revelation
The author concludes that God's forgiveness is valid and permissible 'were it not for the report that He must punish some of them.' This shows that revelation can restrict rational permissibility.
This prevents the misunderstanding that the argument proves universal forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعله فليس بقبيح. قال الله تعالى: والكاظمين الغيظ والعافين عن الناس، ثم قال تعالى: والله يحب المحسنين يعني الواهبين لما استحقوه بما جني عليهم. وقال: وأن تعفوا أقرب للتقوى. وقال: وإن تعفوا وتصفحوا وتغفروا. وكيف لا تجوز هبة الحق لمن يملك أخذه وتركه؟ فدل جميع ما وصفناه على صحة عفو الله تعالى عن سائر المذنبين وجواز ذلك منه لو لم يرد الخبر بأنه لا بد أن يعاقب بعضهم.
So its doer is not evil. Allah Almighty said: 'and those who restrain anger and pardon people,' then He Almighty said: 'and Allah loves the doers of good' meaning those who give away what they had a right to because of what was committed against them. And He said: 'And that you pardon is nearer to piety.' And He said: 'And if you pardon, overlook, and forgive.' And how is it not permissible to give away a right to one who has the power to take it or leave it? So all that we have described indicates the validity of Allah Almighty's forgiveness of all sinners and the permissibility of that from Him, were it not for the report that He must punish some of them.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the rational permissibility of God's forgiveness. The author begins by establishing that forgiveness is not evil (قبيح) in the moral sense. He then cites Qur'anic verses that praise human acts of forgiveness: restraining anger and pardoning people (Al-Imran 134), loving those who are generous in forgiving what is owed to them, and the directive that pardoning is nearer to piety (Al-Baqarah 237). He also mentions the verse 'If you pardon, overlook, and forgive' (At-Taghabun 14). The key point is that if a person has a right to take something (like a debt) and also has the right to forgo it, then giving it up is a praiseworthy act. By analogy, God has the absolute right to punish sinners, so His forgiveness of them is rationally praiseworthy and permissible. The only restriction comes from revealed reports (الخبر) that He will punish some. Thus, the argument establishes the logical possibility of forgiveness, leaving the actual extent to revelation.

Key Terms
العفو
حسن
قبيح
الهبة
الخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this argument proves God must forgive all sinners because forgiveness is good.
Correction: The argument only proves forgiveness is rationally possible (جائز), not obligatory. The final sentence shows that revelation can restrict forgiveness.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main point in this passage?
A) God must forgive all sinners because forgiveness is good.
B) Forgiveness is rationally good for both humans and God, so it is possible for God to forgive unless revelation prevents it.
C) Human forgiveness is never a model for God's actions.
D) The Qur'an commands God to forgive.

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness is rationally praiseworthy for both humans and God, but revelation limits its scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have sinned in ways you deeply regret. Do you truly believe that God can forgive you, or do you let your shame convince you that your sin is beyond His mercy? How does this lesson on the rational permissibility of forgiveness confront that doubt?
?
If forgiveness is so praiseworthy that Allah praises those who pardon others, why do you still hold onto grudges? What would it cost you today to truly forgive someone who wronged you, and how does this lesson link your forgiveness to God's nature?
?
This lesson says God's forgiveness is possible but not guaranteed. Are you living as though His mercy is automatic, or are you taking the revealed warnings seriously? What one change in your daily life would reflect a balanced trust in both His mercy and His justice?

Are Muqallids of Disbelievers in the Fire?
Baqillani refutes a challenge to consensus about muqallids of disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's response to the objection regarding the status of muqallids of other religions and the role of rational inquiry in determining unbelief.

Core Idea
Muqallids of disbelievers are judged by reason, not revelation alone.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a challenge to the consensus that followers of previous religions who blindly imitate (taqlid) are in the Fire. Some theologians had claimed otherwise due to a doubt. Baqillani clarifies their position and affirms that the determination of unbelief is a matter of rational inquiry (nazar), not scriptural report. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall exchange.
2
Identify the objection and response
The passage begins with an objection about consensus. Note how Baqillani reframes the theologians' claim.
The objector is puzzled by a claimed consensus.
3
Note the key terms
Pay attention to مقلدة (muqallids), النظر (rational inquiry), التوقيف (revelation), and الخبر (report). These are central to the argument.
4
Understand the methodological point
The final sentence states that knowledge of a muqallid's unbelief is attained through nazar, not tawqif or khabar. Reflect on why this matters for theology.
This establishes reason as the arbiter in certain doctrinal questions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : وكيف يكون هذا إجماعاً من الأمة ، وقد زعم قوم من المتكلمين بأن مقلدة اليهود والنصارى وغيرهم من أهل الكفر ليسوا في النار؟ . قيل له : هؤلاء إنما أنكروا أن يكون المقلد كافراً لشبهة دخلت عليهم ، ولم يزعموا أن المقلد كافر وأنه مع ذلك ليس في النار. ذلك ليس في النار. والعلم بأن المقلد كافر أو غير كافر طريقه النظر دون التوقيف والخبر.
For if someone says: How can this be a consensus of the community, while a group of theologians claim that the followers of the Jews, Christians, and other disbelievers who practice taqlid are not in the Fire? They are told: These [theologians] only denied that the muqallid is a disbeliever due to a doubt that occurred to them, and they did not claim that the muqallid is a disbeliever and yet not in the Fire. That (i.e., not being in the Fire) is not the case. And knowledge of whether the muqallid is a disbeliever or not is attained through rational inquiry (nazar), not through revelation (tawqif) or report (khabar).
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector questions how a claimed consensus can stand when some theologians assert that taqlid-followers of Jews, Christians, and other disbelievers are not in the Fire. The answer clarifies that those theologians only denied the muqallid's status as a disbeliever due to a doubt that arose in their minds; they did not claim that a disbelieving muqallid would not be in the Fire. The key point: whether a muqallid is a disbeliever or not is known through rational inquiry (nazar), not through scriptural revelation (tawqif) or transmitted reports (khabar). This highlights the role of reason in determining takfir.

Key Terms
مقلدة
النظر
التوقيف
الخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some theologians claim that muqallids of disbelievers are not in the Fire, implying that disbelievers can be saved through taqlid.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that these theologians only denied the muqallid's status as a disbeliever due to a doubt; they did not claim that a disbeliever is not in the Fire. The passage also affirms that the muqallid's status is a matter of rational inquiry, not a license for salvation.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, how is the status of the muqallid (follower) of disbelievers known?
A) Through revelation alone
B) Through rational inquiry
C) Through consensus
D) Through scripture

Key Takeaway
Baqillani affirms that whether a muqallid is a disbeliever is determined by rational inquiry, not by revelation or reports alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you rely more on what you have been taught (taqlid) or on your own reasoning (nazar) when determining matters of faith and the status of others? Which should you rely on, and why?
?
Have you ever judged someone's ultimate fate (salvation or damnation) based solely on a text or a scholar's statement without thinking it through yourself? How might that be dangerous?
?
If rational inquiry (nazar) is essential for knowing the truth about disbelievers, what steps will you take to sharpen your intellect and not remain a blind follower?

The Second Proof: Warding Off Expected Harm
Baqillani argues from necessity of warding off harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that appointing an imam is obligatory because it prevents expected harm, grounded in the purposes of Islamic law and human nature.

Core Idea
Appointing an imam prevents harm that would destroy religion and worldly life.

Baqillani now presents a second rational proof for the obligation of imamate, shifting from direct textual arguments to a pragmatic consideration: the prevention of harm. He builds on the uncontested principle that warding off expected harm is obligatory by consensus, and then demonstrates why an imam is essential for preserving both religious and worldly interests. This passage lays out the logical chain from human nature to the necessity of leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the core claim
Begin by reading the opening line: 'Second: That in it is warding off expected harm, and that it is obligatory by consensus.' This sets up the entire argument.
Notice that Baqillani grounds the proof in a shared rational principle, not just revelation.
2
Understand the Lawgiver's purpose
Identify how Baqillani describes the objectives of Islamic legislation (transactions, jihad, hudud, etc.) as securing worldly and religious interests. This is the foundation.
These interests cannot be realized without an imam.
3
Examine the human condition
Look at Baqillani's description of human nature: divergent opinions, passions, enmity. Why does this make leadership necessary?
He argues that without an imam, conflict and destruction are inevitable.
4
Confirm through experience
Note that Baqillani appeals to empirical evidence: the tribulations that occur when a leader dies. This reinforces the rational necessity.
Experience shows that prolonged absence of authority leads to chaos and ruin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إن فيه دفع ضرر مظنون وأنه واجب إجماعاً. بيانه أنا نعلم علماً يقارب الضرورة أن مقصود الشارع فيما شرع من المعاملات والمناكحات والجهاد والحدود والمقاصات وإظهار شعار الشرع في الأعياد والجمعات إنما هو مصالح عائدة إلى الخلق معاشاً ومعاداً، وذلك لا يمكن إلا بإمام يكون من قبل الشارع يرجعون إليه فيما يعن لهم، فإنهم مع الأهواء وتشتت الآراء وما بينهم من الشحناء قلما ينقاد بعضهم لبعض فيفضي ذلك إلى التنازع والتواثب، وربما أدى إلى إهلاكهم جميعاً، ويشهد له التجربة والفتن القائمة عند موت الولاة إلى نصب آخر بحيث لو تمادى لعطلت المعايش وصار كل أحد مشغولاً بحفظ ماله ونفسه تحت قائم سيفه وذلك يؤدي إلى رفع الدين وهلاك جميع المسلمين. فإن قيل: وفيه إضرار، وأنه منفي بقوله عليه السلام: لا ضرر ولا ضرار في الإسلام، وبيانه عن ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: توليه الأيسان على من هو مثله ليحكم عليه فيما يهتدي إليه وفيما لا يهتدي إضرار به لا محالة. الثاني: قد يستنكف عنه بعضهم كما جرت به العادة فيفضي إلى الفتنة. الثالث: أنه لا يجب عصمته فيتصور منه الفسوق فإن لم يعزل أضر بالأمة بفسقه وإن عزل أدى إلى الفتنة. قلنا: الإضرار اللازم من تركه أكثر بكثير، ودفع الضرر الأعظم عند التعارض واجب.
Second: That in it is warding off expected harm, and that it is obligatory by consensus. Its explanation: We know with knowledge approaching necessity that the intent of the Lawgiver in what He legislated of transactions, marriages, jihad, hudud, qisas, and manifesting the symbols of the religion in feasts and Friday congregations is only interests returning to creation in livelihood and afterlife. And that cannot be except through an imam from the Lawgiver to whom they refer in what concerns them. For they, given their passions, divergence of opinions, and enmity among themselves, rarely yield to one another. That leads to conflict and aggression, and perhaps leads to the destruction of all of them. Experience bears witness to this, and the tribulations that arise at the death of governors until another is appointed, such that if it were prolonged, livelihoods would be ruined and everyone would be occupied with preserving his wealth and his life under the edge of his sword, and that leads to the lifting of the religion and the destruction of all Muslims. If it is said: But in it is harm, and it is negated by his statement, peace be upon him: 'No harm and no reciprocating harm in Islam.' Its explanation has three aspects: First: Appointing [the imam] over one like himself to judge over him in what he is guided and in what he is not guided is inevitably harm. Second: Some may disdain him, as is customary, and that leads to tribulation. Third: Infallibility is not required of him, so immorality can be imagined from him. If he is not removed, he harms the community by his immorality; if he is removed, it leads to tribulation. We say: The harm necessary from abandoning him is much greater, and warding off the greater harm when there is conflict is obligatory.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the obligation of appointing an imam. Baqillani argues that appointing an imam wards off expected harm (daf' dharar maznun) and this is obligatory by consensus. He explains that the Lawgiver's purposes in legislation are to secure worldly and religious interests for humanity. These interests cannot be achieved without an imam appointed by the Lawgiver to whom people refer their affairs. Given human nature—passions, divergent opinions, and enmity—people rarely submit to one another, leading to conflict and mutual destruction. Experience and historical tribulations demonstrate this. Then an objection is raised: appointing an imam itself involves harm, which is prohibited by the prophetic hadith 'La darar wa la dirar'. The objector offers three aspects: (1) placing a fellow human in authority over another is inherently harmful; (2) some may disdain the imam causing fitna; (3) the imam is not infallible, so his immorality either causes harm or his removal causes fitna. Baqillani answers by stating that the harm resulting from abandoning the imam is far greater, and warding off the greater harm when there is conflict is obligatory. Thus the objection is invalidated.

Key Terms
ضرر مظنون
دفع الضرر
مصالح
الشارع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think imamate is merely a political arrangement independent of religion.
Correction: Baqillani roots it in the purposes of Islamic law (sharia).

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the basis for the obligation of appointing an imam?
A) A direct command from the Qur'an
B) Consensus of the Companions
C) Warding off expected harm
D) The need for a military leader

Key Takeaway
Appointing an imam is rationally obligatory to prevent the expected harm that would undermine religion and worldly life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your own life do you see the passions (ahwa') and enmity (shahna') that prevent you from submitting to Allah's appointed authority? Today, will you surrender that area to the imam of the Shari'ah, even if it costs your ego?
?
The passage warns that without an imam, everyone becomes occupied with 'preserving his wealth and his life under the edge of his sword.' Are you more concerned with protecting your own interests than with preserving the religion and unity of the Muslims? What step can you take today to prioritize collective worship over individual security?
?
Baqillani says the Lawgiver's intent is to secure both livelihood and afterlife. If you honestly examine your daily routines, are they aligned with both goals, or are you neglecting one for the other? What would change if you truly acted as if the imam were present over your affairs?

Responding to the Harm Objection
Baqillani refutes objection based on 'la darar' by prioritizing greater harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the objection that appointing an imam causes harm, using the principle of warding off the greater harm.

Core Idea
The harm of abandoning the imam is greater than any harm from appointing him.

Having established the obligation of imamate, Baqillani anticipates a powerful objection: appointing an imam itself involves harm, which is prohibited by the prophetic hadith 'La darar wa la dirar'. He presents the objection in three aspects and then delivers a concise reply. This passage teaches a key principle of Islamic jurisprudence: when harms conflict, the greater harm must be avoided, even if it means accepting a lesser harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection: 'But in it is harm, and it is negated by the hadith...' Understand that the objector uses a textual proof against the rational proof.
This is a common pattern in kalam: objections based on revelation or reason.
2
Examine the three aspects
The objector gives three reasons why appointing an imam causes harm: (1) placing a human over another is inherently harmful, (2) some may disdain him causing fitna, (3) his possible immorality creates a dilemma.
These cover different types of potential harm: inherent, social, and moral.
3
Understand Baqillani's response
Baqillani answers simply: the harm from abandoning the imam is much greater, and warding off the greater harm is obligatory. This is a principle of Islamic legal theory.
The response does not deny that appointing an imam may involve some harm, but outweighs it.
4
Reflect on the principle
Notice that Baqillani does not refute the objection point by point. He uses a overarching rule: when harms conflict, choose the lesser evil.
This principle is widely accepted in fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second: That in it is warding off expected harm, and that it is obligatory by consensus. Its explanation: We know with knowledge approaching necessity that the intent of the Lawgiver in what He legislated of transactions, marriages, jihad, hudud, qisas, and manifesting the symbols of the religion in feasts and Friday congregations is only interests returning to creation in livelihood and afterlife. That leads to conflict and aggression, and perhaps leads to the destruction of all of them. Experience bears witness to this, and the tribulations that arise at the death of governors until another is appointed, such that if it were prolonged, livelihoods would be ruined and everyone would be occupied with preserving his wealth and his life under the edge of his sword, and that leads to the lifting of the religion and the destruction of all Muslims. If it is said: But in it is harm, and it is negated by his statement, peace be upon him: 'No harm and no reciprocating harm in Islam.' If he is not removed, he harms the community by his immorality; if he is removed, it leads to tribulation. This passage presents the second proof for the obligation of appointing an imam. Baqillani argues that appointing an imam wards off expected harm (daf' dharar maznun) and this is obligatory by consensus. Argument from Repelling Probable Harm and Its Refutation
The obligers argue that probable harm necessitates an imam; the answer denies rationality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligers' argument that probable harm necessitates an imam and the Ash'ari response denying reason's independent moral rulings.

Core Idea
Probable harm does not obligate action without revelation.

The obligers argue that just as certain harm must be avoided, probable harm must also be avoided, thus necessitating an imam. They give two vivid examples. However, the author refutes this by denying that reason alone can impose such obligations. This passage highlights a fundamental difference between rationalist and Ash'ari approaches to moral obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and examples
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two analogies: poison and falling wall.
2
Understand the obligers' reasoning
Identify the logical structure: from certain harm to probable harm, then apply to the imam. Consider why they think this analogy holds.
The obligers assume reason alone can mandate avoidance.
3
Examine the author's response
The author simply denies that reason has such a ruling. Reflect on what this implies about the source of moral obligation.
This is a Ash'ari position: moral rulings come from revelation, not reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الموجبون : إن أصل دفع المضرة واجب قطعاً. فكذلك المضرة المظنونة، وذلك مثل أن يعرف الإنسان أن كل مسموم يجب اجتنابه ثم يظن أن هذا الطعام مسموم فإن العقل الصريح يقضي بوجوب اجتنابه وكذا من علم أن الحائط الساقط لا يجوز الوقوف تحته، ثم ظن أن هذا الحائط يسقط، فالعقل الصريح يقضي بوجوب ألا يقف تحته. والجواب منع حكم العقل.
Then the obligers said: Indeed, the principle of repelling harm is absolutely obligatory. Likewise, probable harm is also obligatory. This is like one who knows that every poison must be avoided, then he thinks that this food is poison; then clear reason rules the obligation of avoiding it. Similarly, one who knows that a falling wall is not permissible to stand under, then he thinks that this wall is falling; then clear reason rules the obligation of not standing under it. And the answer: Denial of the ruling of reason.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from those who affirm the obligation of an imam. They argue that repelling harm is absolutely obligatory, and since probable harm (al-maḍarrat al-maẓnūna) is similar to certain harm, reason judges that it must be avoided. They give two examples: known poison and falling wall. If one thinks something might be poison, reason says avoid it. Therefore, since the absence of an imam involves probable harm, it must be avoided by appointing one. The author's answer is simply to deny that reason has such a ruling (man' ḥukm al-'aql). This is a brief refutation, likely assuming a theological position that reason does not independently determine moral obligation without revelation.

Key Terms
دفع المضرة
ضرر مظنون
عقل صريح
حكم العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume reason alone can impose obligations.
Correction: The author denies that reason has the authority to impose moral obligations; revelation is necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the argument that probable harm obligates the imam?
A) He accepts the argument
B) He denies the ruling of reason
C) He says probable harm is not real harm
D) He says the imam is not needed

Key Takeaway
Reason alone cannot impose moral obligations; revelation is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the examples given: poison and a falling wall. Your instinct says avoid them. But according to this lesson, reason does not obligate you to avoid them. How does this truth confront the way you make daily decisions? Will you now seek revelation before assuming something is obligatory?
?
You often hear people say 'it's just common sense' to do something. Based on this lesson, what is the danger of relying on 'common sense' for moral obligations? Where in your life have you used common sense to justify a choice without checking what Allah has commanded?
?
The author's answer is simply 'denial of the ruling of reason.' Do you find that uncomfortable? Why? What does your discomfort reveal about your trust in your own intellect versus your trust in revelation?

The Threefold Division of Reports and the First Type
Baqillani enumerates three types of reports and expounds the first

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's threefold classification of reports and why the first type (report about the necessary) is always true regardless of the reporter.

Core Idea
A report about the necessary can never be false because its content is firmly established.

Baqillani begins a key discussion on the epistemology of reports by asking how reports are divided. He answers with a threefold classification. The first type—report about the necessary—is so certain that its truth does not depend on the reporter at all. This passage lays the foundation for understanding why certain theological claims, like the existence of God, are considered indubitable.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer
Notice that the passage starts with a question: 'On how many divisions are reports divided?' followed by the answer. This dialectical style is common in kalām works.
The author uses a hypothetical objector to introduce the topic.
2
Identify the three types
The answer says 'into three types,' but only the first is described in detail here. Keep in mind that two more types will follow in later passages.
The passage only expounds the first type.
3
Understand the definition of the first type
The first type is a report about the necessary (wājib). Baqillani defines it as a report about a matter established by necessities, senses, and proofs.
This includes both immediate certainties and things proven by inference.
4
Look at the examples
Examples include sense perception, impossibility of opposites, impossibility of body in two places, originatedness of the world, and existence and attributes of God and messengers.
These range from obvious rational truths to theological doctrines.
5
Grasp the conclusion about truth
Baqillani asserts that such a report is never false, regardless of the reporter's status (eternal/temporal, believer/disbeliever, just/sinful, group/individual). The truth is guaranteed by the content, not the reporter.
This is a strong epistemological claim: some reports are self-validating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فعلى كم وجه تنقسم الأخبار؟ قيل له : على ثلاثة أضرب : فضرب منها خَبَرٌ عن واجب وهو كل خبر عن أمر ثابت قضت الضرورات ودرك الحواس على إثباته وقامت الأدلة على ذلك نحو الخبر عن حضور ما ندركه ونشاهده بحواسنا والخبر عن امتناع الضدين وكون الجسم في مكانين معاً وأمثال ذلك مما يُعلم فساده بضرورات العقول والخبر عن حدث العالم وإثبات محدثه وأنه على ما يجب كونه من صفاته وصحة أعلام رسله وما جرى مجرى ذلك من كل أمر ثبت العلم بصحته استدلالاً ونظراً وهذا ما لا يقع أبداً إلا صدقاً من قديم ومحدث ومؤمن وكافر وعدل وفاسق وجماعة وآحاد لثبوت مُخبره وصحته كيف تصرفت بالمخبرين عنه الحال.
If someone says: 'On how many divisions are reports divided?' It is said to him: 'Into three types.' One type is a report about the necessary (wājib), and it is every report about a matter that is established, which necessities (ḍarūrāt) and the perception of the senses have judged its affirmation, and evidences have been established for that, such as the report about the presence of what we perceive and witness with our senses, and the report about the impossibility of two opposites and the body being in two places simultaneously, and similar things whose falsity is known by necessities of the intellects, and the report about the originatedness of the world, the affirmation of its Originator, and that He is upon what must be of His attributes, and the soundness of the signs of His messengers, and whatever runs along that course of every matter whose correctness is established by inference and reflection. And this [report] never occurs except as truth, from the eternal or the originated, believer or disbeliever, just or sinful, group or individuals, due to the establishment of its reported content and its soundness, however the situation may change with the reporters.'
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents a question-and-answer format to introduce the classification of reports into three types. The first type is a report about the 'necessary' (wājib) — that is, a report whose content is necessarily true, either by immediate necessity (ḍarūra), sense perception, or by proofs. The author gives examples: reports about things we directly perceive, the impossibility of two opposites coexisting, the impossibility of a body being in two places at once, the originatedness of the world, and the existence and attributes of God and the truth of His messengers. Such reports can only be true, regardless of the status of the reporter — whether eternal (God) or temporal, believer or disbeliever, just or sinful, a group or an individual. This is because the content (mukhbar) is firmly established and true, regardless of the reporter's circumstances. The author emphasizes that this type is never false.

Key Terms
الخبر عن واجب (report about necessary)
الضرورات (necessities)
الحواس (senses)
الأدلة (evidences)
الصدق (truth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The truth of a report depends on the trustworthiness of the reporter.
Correction: Baqillani argues that for reports about the necessary, truth is independent of the reporter; it is guaranteed by the content itself.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, which of the following is NOT an example of a report about the necessary?
A) A report about the impossibility of two opposites
B) A report about the presence of something perceived by the senses
C) A report about a historical event known only from a single narrator
D) A report about the originatedness of the world

Key Takeaway
Baqillani establishes that reports about the necessary are always true, irrespective of the reporter, because their content is firmly grounded in necessity, sense, or proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a fact about God or the universe from someone you despise, do you instinctively doubt it? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to judge truth by the speaker's character?
?
Can you recall a time you rejected a true statement simply because it came from a person you considered unreliable? What does that reveal about your attachment to your own biases rather than to the truth?
?
If a disbeliever tells you 'the world had a beginning' using sound reasoning, will you accept it? Or will you reject it because of his disbelief? Your answer shows where your real trust lies.
